# 文始真经注

**Commentary on the *Wenshi Zhenjing* (True Scripture of the Primordial Beginning)**

**Title:** *Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing*
A commentary by the Daoist Niu Daochun of the Song-Yuan transition period. Nine fascicles.
Base text: *Zhengtong Daozang* (Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era), *Dongshen* section, *Yujue* category.
Collated with: (1) *Wenshi Zhenjing*; (2) *Guan Yinzi* as collected in *Congshu Jicheng*.

---

### **Guan Yinzi**
Guan Ling Yin Xi was a grandee of the Zhou dynasty. When Laozi journeyed west, Yin Xi perceived a purple qi hovering over the pass and knew a true person would pass through. He awaited and observed, and indeed encountered Laozi. Laozi, recognizing his extraordinary nature, composed a text for him. After receiving Laozi’s work, Yin Xi himself wrote nine chapters, titled *Guan Yinzi*. Today, the Taihu Guan in Lingbao County, Shanzhou, marks the ancient Hangu Pass where he awaited Laozi. The Zongsheng Gong on Mount Zhongnan was Yin Xi’s former residence; King Mu of Zhou later renovated its thatched pavilion, renaming it Louguan and establishing a shrine to Laozi. The rise of Daoist temples truly began here.

After transmitting the scripture, Laozi exited through the Dasan Pass and later met Yin Xi again at the Green Ram Market in Chengdu, where he bestowed upon him the title *Wenshi Xiansheng* (Master of the Primordial Beginning). The book he composed became the *Wenshi Zhenjing* (True Scripture of the Primordial Beginning).

---

### **Postscript to the *Wenshi Zhenjing Zhijie* (Direct Explication of the True Scripture of the Primordial Beginning)**
Bright as the full moon, its radiance is seen in a thousand rivers; wondrous existence arises from true emptiness, pervading the ten thousand scrolls of scripture. By water one perceives the moon’s round clarity; through scripture one awakens to wondrous existence. Round clarity has the bright moon as its root; wondrous existence has true emptiness as its source. Root and branch are one; source and flow are not distinct. Following the branch, one returns to the root; tracing the flow, one reverts to the source. The source is true emptiness—beyond all conception; the flow is wondrous existence—yet not confined to singular silence. The scripture is the wordless word; the wondrous is the speechless speech. The speechless speech is offered before words; the wordless word illumines beyond meaning. Before words lies profound mystery; beyond meaning dwells abyssal depth. Without direct explication, it is hard to fathom; without detailed annotation, it remains obscure. By the finger one sees the moon and forgets the finger—thus the true moon shines forth. Through explication one awakens to the scripture, and the true scripture becomes utterly clear. Seeing the moon’s reflection in a thousand rivers, one knows the single moon as the guiding principle. Investigating the true purport of the palm-held scripture, one awakens to the singular true essence that governs all. Seeing and knowing both dissolve; investigation and awakening are both forgotten. The celestial eye and dragon’s gaze—how could they glimpse even a shadow? The numinous wisdom of sages—how could it fathom even a trace? Beyond meaning, it defies thought; before words, it eludes discourse.

---

### **Commentary on the *Wenshi Zhenjing*, Fascicle One**
**Direct Explication by Shenfeng Xiaoyaozi Niu Daochun**

#### **The "One Character" Chapter**
The character *yu* (宇) is a metaphor for the Way of true emptiness. This chapter comprises twenty-eight sections.

**Guan Yinzi said:**
Without the Dao, it cannot be spoken.
That which is "without the Dao" refers to the fundamental absence of any forced attribution of "Dao," and thus it cannot be discussed in words. As the scripture says: "Only that which cannot be named"—this is why it "retires all words under heaven." Hence, it is said: without the Dao, it cannot be spoken.

That which cannot be spoken *is* the Dao.
This is awakening before words. Before any discourse arises, one clearly awakens to the wondrous Dao—thus words cannot reach it. Hence, it is said: that which cannot be spoken *is* the Dao.

Without the Dao, it cannot be contemplated.
Not only is there no forced name for the fundamental absence of Dao, but it also does not belong to thought. Thought pertains to the knowing and understanding of consciousness. The Dao cannot be known or recognized. Thus, it is said: "As soon as it falls into thought, it becomes the ghost’s livelihood." This is precisely the meaning. As the scripture says: "Only that which cannot be known"—this is why it "seizes all wisdom under heaven." Hence, it is said: without the Dao, it cannot be contemplated.

That which cannot be contemplated *is* the Dao.
This is awakening before thought. Before any thought arises, one clearly awakens to the wondrous Dao—only then does one know that thought cannot reach it. Hence, it is said: that which cannot be contemplated *is* the Dao.

Heavenly things surge in furious flow; human affairs are tangled and chaotic.
"Heavenly things" refers to the original endowment of celestial truth. "Anger" is majestic radiance—its brilliance vast and boundless. "Flow" is the heart-mind, its source streaming into the six functions, intermingling with human affairs in ceaseless confusion. As the scripture says: "Nature is water; the heart-mind is flow." Hence, it is said: heavenly things surge in furious flow; human affairs are tangled and chaotic. "Tangled" means mixed. It speaks of deluded people engaging with the Dao, their understandings scattered and divergent, as in the text below.

Seeming yet turning away,
"Seeming" means resemblance or likeness. One imagines the Dao as resembling the great void, the bright moon, a limpid pool, or a crystal vase. Such imaginings and comparisons are far removed from the Dao. Hence, it is said: seeming yet turning away. "Turning away" means divergence.

Clashing like a state at war,
"Clashing" is the sound of striking. "State" refers to contention. It warns against debating the Dao through verbal disputes. Hence, it is said: "Taking one’s endowment as doors and windows, each opens in its own way; using scriptural debate as shields and spears, they attack one another." This is the warning against contention. Hence, it is said: clashing like a gaping void.

Hurriedly, it seems yet is not.
Do not think in images; do not debate in contention. Even if one’s understanding seems to align, it is not so. Hence, it is said: hurriedly, it seems yet is not.

And they contend over it,
"Contend" means striving for supremacy. When two or three people gather to study the Dao, those who are courageous and diligent, who take pride in their determination—such views have not yet awakened. Hence, it is said: and they contend over it.

And they isolate themselves over it,
"Isolate" means solitary and aloof. Dwelling alone, one meditates deeply on the wondrous Dao. Such views have not yet awakened. Hence, it is said: and they isolate themselves over it.

And they praise it.
"Praise" means extolling the words of the ancients to glorify the wondrous Dao. Following words to contemplate and praise the wondrous Dao—this has not yet awakened. Hence, it is said: and they praise it.

And they sigh over it,
"Sigh" means exclaiming and murmuring over the renowned sayings and dharma-attributes of the ancients, silently and deeply contemplating the wondrous Dao. Such views have not yet awakened. Hence, it is said: and they sigh over it.

And they discard it, and they seek it.
"Discard" means relinquishing; "seek" means grasping. Relinquishing all delusions to grasp the true Dao—where there is grasping and relinquishing, this is still deliberate action, and thus has not yet awakened. Hence, it is said: and they discard it, and they seek it.

Words are like blowing at shadows,
"Shadows" serve as a metaphor for names. This refers to those who ceaselessly debate, extol, praise, and exclaim over names and forms. To rebuke names and attributes with words is like blowing at shadows—it cannot dispel them. What does this mean?

Thoughts are like carving dust,
"Dust" serves as a metaphor for consciousness; "carving" means to chisel and sculpt. This refers to the aforementioned imaginings, comparisons, and the striving, contending, isolating, solitary contemplation, relinquishing, and grasping. Such are all attributes of consciousness—like carving dust, it cannot be purified completely.

Sage wisdom creates delusion,
Sage wisdom can comprehend the myriad dharmas, yet using wisdom to construct the Dao cannot reach it—thus, wisdom itself is delusion. Only by transcending sageliness and abandoning wisdom, awakening to the wondrous Dao, does one realize that wisdom itself is delusion. Hence, it is said: sage wisdom creates delusion.

Even spirits and gods do not comprehend it.
Spirits and gods cling to perceptions and views, yet perceptions and views cannot know the Dao. Hence, it is said: even spirits and gods do not comprehend it.

Only that which cannot be acted upon—
The Dao is fundamentally natural and does not rely on cultivation. Hence, it is said: cannot be acted upon.

*"Only"* here is a phrase connecting the above to the below.

Cannot be attained,
"Attained" means inviting from without. The Dao is inherently complete and does not rely on external seeking. Hence, it is said: one who enters through the gate does not possess the family treasure. Thus, it is said: cannot be attained.

Cannot be fathomed,
The Dao is difficult for even spirits and gods to perceive; yin and yang cannot fathom it—how much less can ordinary beings? Hence, it is said: cannot be fathomed.

Cannot be divided,
The Dao is the undifferentiated principle of Heaven, profound and unfathomable. Thus, it cannot be divided or debated through the mind, words, or thoughts. Hence, it is said: cannot be divided.

Thus, it is called Heaven, called Mandate, called Spirit, called Mystery—together, they are called the Dao.
"Thus" is a phrase connecting the above to the below. "Heaven" is non-action that acts, naturally so. "Mandate" is that which is so without knowing why. "Spirit" refers to the unfathomable workings of yin and yang. "Mystery" is the profound and unfathomable, mystery upon mystery. "Together" means to harmonize and awaken. Only by awakening to Heaven, Mandate, Spirit, and Mystery does one harmonize with the wondrous Dao.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not reside in words or thoughts but in the awakening and profound harmony of the human heart.

**Chapter One**
Guan Yinzi said: There is no single thing that is not Heaven,
"Heaven" is naturally so, and "naturally so" is another name for the Dao. All things are born of the Dao, and thus each thing is the Dao—just as waves arise from water, and thus each wave is water. Hence, it is said: there is no single thing that is not Heaven.

There is no single thing that is not Mandate,
"Mandate" is that which is so without knowing why, and "without knowing why" is another name for the Dao. All things possess the Mandate of the Dao. Hence, it is said: there is no single thing that is not Mandate.

There is no single thing that is not Spirit,
"Spirit" is the spirit that is not spirit, the unfathomable workings of yin and yang—another name for the Dao. All things possess the Spirit of the Dao. Hence, it is said: there is no single thing that is not Spirit.

There is no single thing that is not Mystery.
"Mystery" is the profound and unfathomable—another name for the Dao. All things possess this Mysterious Dao. Hence, it is said: there is no single thing that is not Mystery.

If things are thus, how could humans be otherwise?
Heaven and Earth, the myriad things—great and small, coarse and fine, flying and crawling, moving and still—all possess the wondrous Dao. How could humans be otherwise? Hence, it is said: if things are thus, how could humans be otherwise?

All people may be called Heaven, all may be called Spirit, all may attain Mandate and penetrate Mystery.
Heaven, Mandate, Spirit, and Mystery are all alternative names for the Dao. Though the names are four, the Dao is not two. The Dao is complete in every person, inherently present in each. If one awakens, without relying on cultivation, one immediately realizes the unborn and stands equal to all sages. Hence, it is said: all people may be called Heaven, all may be called Spirit, all may attain Mandate and penetrate Mystery.

There is no "that Heaven" and "this non-Heaven," no "that Spirit" and "this non-Spirit," no "that Mandate" and "this non-Mandate," no "that Mystery" and "this non-Mystery."
Every person possesses their original, innate Heavenly truth; every person possesses the Spirit that is not spirit; every person possesses the Mandate that is so without knowing why; every person possesses the profound and unfathomable Mystery. Heaven, Mandate, Spirit, and Mystery—whether in the ordinary or the sage, they neither increase nor diminish; whether fine or vast, they neither lessen nor multiply. How could one say that only those things possess them while I alone do not? Hence, it is said: there is no "that Heaven" and "this non-Heaven," no "that Spirit" and "this non-Spirit," no "that Mandate" and "this non-Mandate," no "that Mystery" and "this non-Mystery."

Therefore, those who are adept in my Dao, within a single thing, know Heaven and exhaust Spirit, attain Mandate and reach Mystery.
"Therefore" is a phrase connecting the above to the below. "Those who are adept in my Dao" means that within a single thing, one knows Heaven and exhausts Spirit, attains Mandate and reaches Mystery—this is awakening to the Dao from the subtle. Hence, it is said: the entire ten-direction world is the Dao-person’s eye; the entire ten-direction world is the Dao-person’s body; the entire ten-direction world is one’s own light; the entire ten-direction world resides within one’s own light. However one moves, one merely acts within the realm of light and shadow. Only by awakening to the state before light and shadow arise does one truly realize that the Dao is everywhere. Within a single thing, one awakens to Heaven, Mandate, Spirit, and Mystery—nothing else.

Hence, it is said: therefore, those who are adept in my Dao, within a single thing, know Heaven and exhaust Spirit, attain Mandate and reach Mystery.
"Know" means to awaken; "attain" and "reach" both mean to arrive. This speaks of the ultimate realization of my Dao.

Those who study cling to distinctions, while names dissect what is essentially the same. Learners chase after the differences in Heaven, Mandate, Spirit, and Mystery, dividing names while failing to recognize the unchanging truth of the Dao. They are deluded by names and lost to reality. Hence, it is said: those who study cling to distinctions, while names dissect what is essentially the same.

When attainment aligns with sameness, reality forgets differing names. The Dao originally has no name; through names, one awakens to the Dao. The Dao is reality, while names are but guests to reality. Through names, one contracts and awakens to the truth that though names differ, reality does not. Awakening to reality, one forgets names—like catching fish and forgetting the trap, catching rabbits and forgetting the snare, grasping meaning and forgetting words. Names are but words. Hence, it is said: when attainment aligns with sameness, reality forgets differing names.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao is everywhere, and in awakening to reality, one forgets names.

---

Chapter Two

Guan Yinzi said: To observe the Dao is like observing water. To observe is to reflect; with wisdom, one reflects on principle, just as one reflects upon water. This is a borrowed analogy.

To consider observing a mere pond insufficient—ponds are metaphors for ordinary people. When one’s wisdom is shallow, one measures others by it, and though one may possess the Dao, one doubts that the Dao is fully present in ordinary people. Hence, it is said: to consider observing a mere pond insufficient.

Then one turns to rivers, to the Yangtze, to the sea, saying, “This is the ultimate of water.” To turn is to go forth. Rivers are metaphors for noble ones, the Yangtze for the worthy, and the sea for sages. Doubting that the Dao is fully present in ordinary people, one then observes noble ones. Still doubting that the Dao is fully present in noble ones, one then observes the worthy. Still doubting that the Dao is fully present in the worthy, one then observes sages, only then ceasing to doubt and declaring the Dao’s ultimate state. Hence, it is said: then one turns to rivers, to the Yangtze, to the sea, saying, “This is the ultimate of water.”

Yet one fails to realize that one’s own saliva, tears, and mucus are all water. Saliva, tears, and mucus are metaphors for movement and stillness, speech and silence. One fails to realize that movement and stillness, speech and silence are all functions of the Dao. Through function, one awakens to essence, only then trusting that the Dao is no less present in the ordinary, no more present in noble ones, the worthy, or sages. Hence, it is said: yet one fails to realize that one’s own saliva, tears, and mucus are all water.

This is the concluding analogy. This chapter clarifies that the Dao is neither lacking nor excessive, equally and fully present in sages and ordinary people alike.

**Chapter Three**

Master Guan Yin said: The Dao is without person—sages do not perceive it. *Jia* is the Dao; *yi* is not the Dao.

*Jia* represents the sage; *yi* represents the ordinary person. The Dao is fundamentally without the mark of self—how could it have the mark of person? One who has realized the Dao, the sage, forgets their own views and thus does not perceive it. The sage is the Dao; the ordinary person is not the Dao. Hence it is said: the Dao is without person—sages do not perceive it. *Jia* is the Dao; *yi* is not the Dao.

The Dao is without self—sages do not perceive it. One may advance toward the Dao or retreat from the Dao.

To advance is to grasp; to retreat is to discard. The Dao cannot be grasped when sought, nor lost when abandoned. Since the Dao is without the mark of self, the sage forgets their views and thus does not perceive it. One may advance toward the Dao in grasping it or retreat from the Dao in discarding it. Hence it is said: the Dao is without self—sages do not perceive it. One may advance toward the Dao or retreat from the Dao.

Because it does not possess the Dao, it is not without the Dao.

The Dao does not belong to being or non-being; the sage’s subtlety is impartial. This explains why one does not perceive *jia* as the Dao and *yi* as not the Dao. Hence it is said: because it does not possess the Dao, it is not without the Dao.

Because it does not attain the Dao, it does not lose the Dao.

The Dao does not belong to attainment or loss; it is originally and spontaneously complete. This explains why one does not perceive advancing toward the Dao or retreating from it. This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to being or non-being, attainment or loss.

**Chapter Four**

Master Guan Yin said: One who does not know the Dao yet recklessly conjectures and divines is like shooting at an overturned vessel.

One who does not know the Dao is a deluded person. One who recklessly conjectures and divines is one who vainly uses the intellect to speculate about the Dao. To shoot is to guess; the overturned vessel is a metaphor for the dust of delusion that obscures. The deluded person, failing to awaken to the wondrous Dao, vainly uses the intellect to speculate about it, like guessing at what lies beneath an overturned vessel. Hence it is said: one who does not know the Dao yet recklessly conjectures and divines is like shooting at an overturned vessel.

Those of lofty understanding say: “There is gold, there is jade.”

Gold and jade are metaphors for profound and wondrous words. Moreover, gold represents clarity; jade represents luminous purity. Those of superior wisdom, vainly using the intellect to speculate about the wondrous Dao, say it is as clear as gold, as pure as jade.

Some say: “gold-lead and jade-mercury,” or “mysterious within the mysterious, the gate of all wonders.” Is this the Dao or not? Though the dust of delusion obscures, and though they grasp the most refined words, they still do not awaken. Hence it is said: those of lofty understanding say, “There is gold, there is jade.”

Those of middling understanding say: “There is horn, there is feather.”

Horn represents the sharpness of wisdom; feather represents the lightness and clarity of those of middling wisdom. Vainly using the intellect to speculate about the wondrous Dao, they say wisdom is sharp and penetrating, or that one should cultivate non-action, tranquility, preserve spirit, and keep the body light and healthy.

Some say: “like the antelope hanging by its horns, like the simurgh and phoenix soaring into the clouds.” Is this the Dao? Such guesses still do not lead to awakening. Hence it is said: those of middling understanding say, “There is horn, there is feather.”

Those of inferior understanding say: “There is tile, there is stone.”

Tile represents having undergone refinement through firing; stone represents firmness. Those of lesser wisdom, vainly using the intellect to speculate about the wondrous Dao, say one should temper the stubborn mind and remain single-minded, firm, and unshaken.

Some say: “The Dao is in tiles and rubble.” Is this the Dao or not? Such guesses lead even further from awakening. Hence it is said: those of inferior understanding say, “There is tile, there is stone.”

Is it so or is it not so?

Only one who has set things aside knows.

To set aside is to abandon; to know is to awaken. The three types of people above—lofty, middling, and inferior—vainly use the intellect to speculate about gold, jade, horn, feather, tile, and stone, judging whether they are the Dao or not. Only by abandoning things, forgetting the intellect within, and clearly awakening—when the overturned vessel of delusion is entirely removed and nothing obscures—does one trust that the Dao is not obtained from without. Hence it is said: Is it so or is it not so? Only one who has set things aside knows. This chapter clarifies that the Dao is realized through the heart’s awakening, not through reckless conjecture and divination.

**Chapter Five**

Master Guan Yin said: A single potter can make ten thousand vessels, yet never has a single vessel been able to make or harm the potter.

To mold clay is called *tao*; the potter is the craftsman who fires ceramics and tiles. A single potter can make myriad ceramic and tile vessels. The potter can cause vessels to be formed or destroyed, but how could a vessel form or destroy the potter? Hence it is said: a single potter can make ten thousand vessels, yet never has a single vessel been able to make or harm the potter. This is a borrowed analogy.

A single Dao can make ten thousand things, yet never has a single thing been able to make or harm the Dao.

The Dao moves yin and yang, giving rise to heaven, earth, and the ten thousand things. Hence it is said: a single Dao can make ten thousand things. To make is to generate. The Dao is like empty space, profound and unfathomable, originally and spontaneously complete, not dependent on cultivation or fabrication. Hence it is said: never has a single thing been able to make the Dao. Since the Dao is like empty space, how could the ten thousand things harm empty space? Hence it is said: never has a single thing been able to harm it. This is the concluding analogy. This chapter clarifies that the Dao gives rise to the ten thousand things, yet no thing can harm the Dao.

**Chapter Six**

Master Guan Yin said: The Dao is vast and without knowledge; the heart is free and without constraint; things follow one another and are without error.

The Dao is the essence of the heart; the essence of the heart is true emptiness. True emptiness is profound and obscure, vast and boundless—like the state before the arising of knowledge. Since the heart takes true emptiness as its essence, it is free and unbound by the myriad conditions. Things are the function of the heart; essence and function are not two. Responding to change while remaining ever tranquil, things follow one another without departing from the Dao. *Diedie* means to circulate without ceasing. Hence it is said: the Dao is vast and without knowledge; the heart is free and without constraint; things follow one another and are without error.

The lightning’s swiftness—

Those of great attainment, having realized the wondrousness of self-benefit and benefiting others, are persons of superior capacity. Their spiritual sharpness is swift as striking flint or flashing lightning. In the space of a thought, they traverse a thousand mountains and ten thousand rivers. Persons of superior capacity directly and suddenly awaken, penetrating the wondrous Dao. Hence it is said: the lightning’s swiftness.

The sand’s flight—

Those of great attainment, in their equality and universal beneficence, do not forsake the middle and lower capacities. With swiftness of spirit, they guide those of superior capacity; through skillful expedients, they broadly expound the ten thousand dharmas, pervading the realm of sand-grains to reach those of middle and lower capacity. Hence it is said: the sand’s flight.

The sand’s flight speaks of the vast expounding of the wondrous Dharma, like wind scattering dust and sand. The sage knows that heart is one, things are one, and the Dao is one—these three are again united as one.

Heart is one, things are one, the Dao is one: these three names of oneness are like the sun’s radiance. The sun is the essence of brightness; light is the function of brightness. Though there are three names, the Dao is not two. Hence it is said: the sage knows that heart is one, things are one, and the Dao is one—these three are again united as one.

Not by oneness measuring non-oneness.
"One" is the essence of non-duality; "measure" is the act of gauging. "Non-one" is the function of responding to change. Essence and function are not two; the function of non-function is like an echo resounding in an empty valley—responding to change while ever tranquil. One does not, from the essence, generate the heart to measure responses to change. Hence it is said: not by oneness measuring non-oneness.

Not by non-oneness harming oneness.
The function of non-function is like an echo resounding in an empty valley—responding to change while ever tranquil, essence and function as they are. One does not, by function, obstruct the essence. Hence it is said: not by non-oneness harming oneness. "Harm" means to obstruct.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao, heart, and things are not two; essence and function are ever thus.

---

**Chapter Seven**

Guan Yinzi said: Using a basin as a pond, a stone as an island, fish circle and swim without end, traversing countless thousands of miles—why is this so?

Water has no source, no return.
Encircling it is water; within it, a mountain called an island. Using a basin as a pond, placing a stone within as an island, and releasing fish into the water, the fish circle the stone endlessly, traversing countless thousands of miles without exhaustion. Why is this so? It is because the water has no source, no flow, no return—hence it is inexhaustible. This is a borrowed analogy. Thus it is said: using a basin as a pond, a stone as an island, fish circle and swim without knowing thousands of miles, yet never exhaust—why is this so? Water has no source, no return.

The sage’s Dao has no beginning, no end, and thus responds to things without exhaustion.
"Beginning" is the essence of true emptiness; "end" is the function of wondrous existence. The sage, having penetrated the Dao of true emptiness and wondrous existence, knows the Dao has no beginning or end, no measure to fathom it. Responding to the pivot of change, it is inexhaustible. Hence it is said: the sage’s Dao has no beginning, no end, and thus responds to things without exhaustion.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao has no beginning or end and responds to things without exhaustion.

---

**Chapter Eight**

Guan Yinzi said: Do not love the Dao—love is water.
"Do not" is a prohibitive term. To say "do not love the Dao" means the Dao does not belong to love. When the slightest thought of love arises, one falls into the five phases, becoming water—thus one is far from the Dao. Hence it is said: do not love the Dao—love is water.

Do not observe the Dao—observation is fire. Do not pursue the Dao—pursuit is wood. Do not speak of the Dao—speech is metal. Do not think of the Dao—thought is earth.
Do not attain the Dao through observation, pursuit, speech, or thought. The Dao does not belong to form, action, name, or emotion—how could it be fathomed by observation, pursuit, speech, or thought? When the slightest thought of observation, pursuit, speech, or thought arises, one falls into the five phases. Observation belongs to fire, pursuit to wood, speech to metal, thought to earth. Enslaved by the five phases, one is far from the Dao. Hence it is said: do not observe the Dao—observation is fire; do not pursue the Dao—pursuit is wood; do not speak of the Dao—speech is metal; do not think of the Dao—thought is earth.

This is a profound admonition to later learners.

Only the sage does not depart from fundamental nature and ascends the great Dao; when the heart has not yet arisen, the Dao is but a provisional name.
"Only" connects the preceding and following. "Only the sage does not depart from nature" refers to the state before the arising of love, observation, pursuit, speech, or thought. Before the arising of love, observation, pursuit, speech, or thought, this is the essence of true emptiness—true emptiness is the Dao. When the heart of true emptiness has not yet arisen, the name of the Dao is also provisionally established. Thus, there is not even a name for the Dao. This points the way for learners to awaken to the Dao. Hence it is said: only the sage does not depart from fundamental nature and ascends the great Dao; when the heart has not yet arisen, the Dao is but a provisional name.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to the five phases and urges awakening before the arising of love, observation, pursuit, speech, or thought.

---

**Chapter Nine**

Guan Yinzi said: Dense clouds veil the sky, rivers and lakes grow dim; wandering fish are lost. Suddenly, they glimpse the shimmer of waves, the movement of food—believing it a gift from heaven, they rush toward it, and the fish are hooked and perish.

Dense clouds veil the night sky, thick and dark, obscuring the heavens. Without stars or moon to illuminate, the waters of rivers and lakes grow dim and shadowed. Wandering fish are lost and confused. Then, a fisherman casts a lantern’s light and bait into the water. The fish suddenly glimpse the shimmer of waves and the movement of food, unaware that it is a man’s bait. Believing it a gift from heaven, they rush to seize it—and the fisherman hooks and kills them. Thus it is said: dense clouds veil the sky, rivers and lakes grow dim; wandering fish are lost. Suddenly, they glimpse the shimmer of waves, the movement of food—believing it a gift from heaven, they rush toward it, and the fish are hooked and perish.

Not knowing the self is no-self, those who pursue the Dao are likewise.
Deluded people, obscured by ignorance, are like the sky veiled by dense clouds. Their minds are unclear, like rivers and lakes grown dim. Not awakening to the wondrous Dao, they are like wandering fish lost and confused. Drifting on the waves of intellect, they seek truth in dharmas—like suddenly glimpsing the shimmer of waves and the movement of food. The waves of intellect delude the Dao; the dust of dharmas obscures the heart—like the fish rushing toward the bait. Losing the Dao and deluding the true, they are like the fish hooked and perishing.

Those who do not know do not awaken. Not awakening to the self’s inherent no-self is the Dao of true emptiness. Yet clinging to a false self and seeking the Dao externally—this, too, is like the parable above. This is the synthesis of analogy and meaning, hence it is said: not knowing the self is no-self, those who pursue the Dao are likewise.

This chapter laments that the deluded, failing to awaken to the Dao, forfeit their lives.

**Chapter Ten**

Master Guan Yin said: The arts and techniques in the world are many. All beneath heaven—every school of thought—unfolds the Dao’s vast expanse. Thus it is said: the arts and techniques in the world are many. *Fang* is the Dao.

Some esteem concealment, some esteem clarity, some esteem strength, some esteem weakness. The hundred schools cannot be exhaustively listed; these four are but a rough outline. *Shang* means to value. Some value concealment, not wishing to be known; some value manifest display, teaching openly and brightly; some value the ability to act, ceaselessly strengthening themselves; some value yielding weakness, not contending with things. Thus it is said: some esteem concealment, some esteem clarity, some esteem strength, some esteem weakness.

Clinging to these is all *shi*; not clinging to them is all Dao. *Shi* means application. If one clings to esteeming concealment, clarity, strength, or weakness, these are all called *shi*. Clinging to application and losing sight of the essence—how can one penetrate the wondrous Dao? If one does not cling to application, then application becomes non-application, and there is nothing to attach to. With nothing to attach to, one departs from all extremes—this is called the wondrous Dao. Thus it is said: clinging to these is all *shi*; not clinging to them is all Dao.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao is present everywhere yet cannot be clung to.

**Chapter Eleven**

Master Guan Yin said: The Dao can never be attained. What can be attained is called *de* (virtue/power), not Dao. The Dao is fundamentally true emptiness, beyond gain and loss. Where there is gain, there is loss. The Dao resides in humanity—sage and commoner share the same essence, neither lacking nor excess. Like a newborn infant and an adult sharing the same body, yet not the same function.

Foolish people and sages share the same essence, yet not the same function. The essence is originally nameless; names arise according to function. When function is applied to evil, it is called a foolish person; when applied to good, a worthy person; when applied to nothing to be attained, a sage. Attaining nothing yet attaining it—this is called *de*, not Dao. Thus it is said: the Dao can never be attained. What can be attained is called *de*, not Dao.

The Dao can never be practiced. What can be practiced is called *xing* (conduct), not Dao. The Dao requires awakening of the heart, ascending the great Dao without departing from one’s original nature—thus it cannot be practiced. What can be practiced is called goodness, not Dao. Thus it is said: the Dao can never be practiced. What can be practiced is called *xing*, not Dao.

The sage employs what can be attained and practiced to perfect this life. The sage attains the Dao of self-benefit and benefiting others, universally and equally, seeking no reward—this is called *de* and *xing*. This *de* and *xing* perfect our interactions and benefit life. Thus it is said: the sage employs what can be attained and practiced to perfect this life—this is benefiting others.

By means of what cannot be attained or practiced, one perfects one’s death. Death is the heart’s rest. By means of what cannot be attained or practiced, one conceals all function. Concealing all function is non-action. Non-action perfects the heart’s rest. Thus it is said: by means of what cannot be attained or practiced, one perfects one’s death—this is self-benefit.

This chapter clarifies that *de* and *xing* are function, while the Dao is essence.

**Chapter Twelve**

Master Guan Yin said: After hearing the Dao, those who act and cling proceed toward the human. If a learner, after hearing the Dao, engages in deliberate action and clings to intellectual understanding, their path aligns with worldly affairs. *Zhi* means to proceed. Thus it is said: after hearing the Dao, those who act and cling proceed toward the human.

Those who act not and cling not proceed toward heaven. Heaven is natural truth. Hearing the Dao and awakening to it, ceasing study and abandoning inquiry, concealing all function—acting not, externally not clinging to forms, internally not attaching to emptiness—serene and pure, one accords with one’s original natural truth. Thus it is said: those who act not and cling not proceed toward heaven.

Acting inevitably leads to failure; clinging inevitably leads to loss. Those who act are bound to fail in the Dao; those who cling are bound to lose *de*. Thus it is said: acting inevitably leads to failure; clinging inevitably leads to loss.

Thus, hearing the Dao in the morning, one may die by evening. *Gu* is a connective phrase linking above and below. Hearing the Dao at dawn, one may die by dusk—completely letting go of the body, the heart dying and resting, preserving and maintaining this state. Thus it is said: hearing the Dao in the morning, one may die by evening.

This chapter clarifies that those who awaken to the Dao act not and cling not, resting and preserving.

**Chapter Thirteen**

Master Guan Yin said: When the one emotion is obscured, one becomes a sage. *Ming* means true emptiness, profound and unfathomable. *Yi qing* means emotion returning to true emptiness. Thus one is called a sage. Hence it is said: when the one emotion is obscured, one becomes a sage.

When the one emotion is good, one becomes a worthy person; when the one emotion is evil, one becomes a petty person. From the essence of true emptiness, a sudden emotion arises—if it does not incline toward goodness, it inclines toward evil. When emotion arises, how can one not be cautious? If it inclines toward goodness and accumulates goodness, the accumulation grows vast, forming the blessing of *de* and *xing*—thus one is called a worthy person. If it inclines toward evil and accumulates evil, the accumulation grows vast, forming the calamity of violence and cruelty—thus one is called a petty person. Hence it is said: when the one emotion is good, one becomes a worthy person; when the one emotion is evil, one becomes a petty person.

The obscured emotion is the self-existent nothingness—it cannot be shown.

The state of *one emotion obscured* is the self-existent nothingness—it is the return from traces to the origin. Returning from traces to the origin, one obscurely accords with the Dao of true emptiness, yet this cannot be shown to others. As the scripture says: "Even the sage cannot bring forth the Dao to show to others"—hence it is said: *the one emotion obscured* is the self-existent nothingness, which cannot be demonstrated.

The *one emotion* of goodness or evil arises from nothingness into being—it cannot be concealed. Nothingness is true emptiness; being is emotion. From the ground of true emptiness, a sudden emotion arises—inclining toward goodness and accumulating goodness, or inclining toward evil and accumulating evil—this is the descent from origin to trace. In this descent from origin to trace, the traces of goodness and evil cannot be concealed or hidden. Thus it is said: the *one emotion* of goodness or evil arises from nothingness into being and cannot be kept secret.

The *one emotion* of goodness or evil belongs to knowing; only sentient beings possess it. To say that the *one emotion* of goodness or evil pertains to knowledge is to equate it with the dull and moving creatures that possess perception. Hence it is said: the *one emotion* of goodness or evil belongs to knowing, and only sentient beings possess it.

The *one emotion obscured* belongs to unknowing; beneath the vast heavens, the Dao is present everywhere. When the *one emotion* merges with true emptiness, it is prior to the arising of knowledge. Before knowledge arises, the wondrous being of true emptiness fills the great void, present in all places without exception. Thus it is said: the *one emotion obscured* belongs to unknowing, and beneath the vast heavens, the Dao is present everywhere. This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to emotion or perception—there is no place where the Dao is not.

**Chapter Fourteen**
Master Guan Yin said: Do not say that because the sage exerts unceasing effort, the Dao is achieved through diligence.
*"Do not"* is a prohibitive term. One must not claim that because the sage applies effort with vigor and practices without laziness, the sage’s study of the Dao succeeds through toil and hardship. This is not so—the Dao is realized through the awakening of the heart, inherently complete from the beginning. How could it depend on unceasing effort and hardship before being achieved? Hence it is said: *Do not say that because the sage exerts unceasing effort, the Dao is achieved through diligence.*

Do not say that because the sage firmly maintains without change, the Dao is obtained through grasping.
One must not claim that because the sage firmly preserves and does not alter, the sage obtains the Dao through holding fast. This is also not so—the sage’s heart opens in awakened understanding, taking the Dao as its essence. It is truly constant and unchanging, non-doing and non-grasping, without loss or gain. Hence it is said: *Do not say that because the sage firmly maintains without change, the Dao is obtained through grasping.*

The sage’s exertion of effort is like the loosing of an arrow—acting in response to others, not acting of oneself.
An arrow is a shaft. The sage responds to beings with compassion, universally benefiting without cease. People may call this "exerting effort," yet they do not realize that the sage’s exertion of compassion and universal benefit without cease is like a person shooting a bow to loose an arrow—moved to respond, acting only when unavoidable. Hence it is said: *The sage’s exertion of effort is like the loosing of an arrow—acting in response to others, not acting of oneself.* This speaks of action without self.

The sage’s firm maintenance is like the grasping of an arrow—maintaining in response to others, not maintaining of oneself.
To *grasp* is to hold. The sage abides in tranquil solitude, and people may call this "firm maintenance," yet they do not realize that the sage’s firm maintenance is like a person holding an arrow before shooting—remaining still because there is no response. How could there be a heart that maintains stillness? Hence it is said: *The sage’s firm maintenance is like the grasping of an arrow—maintaining in response to others, not maintaining of oneself.* This chapter clarifies that the sage’s movement and stillness are without self.

**Chapter Fifteen**
Master Guan Yin said: If one seeks the Dao through words, actions, learning, and knowledge, revolving endlessly, there will be no time of attainment.
The Dao originally has no name and cannot be sought through words. The Dao originally has no trace and cannot be sought through actions. The Dao originally has no method and cannot be sought through learning. The Dao originally has no emotion and cannot be sought through knowledge. If one seeks it through names, traces, methods, and emotions, it is like stones grinding against one another in endless revolution—never departing from names, traces, methods, and emotions. When will one awaken to the Dao? Hence it is said: *If one seeks the Dao through words, actions, learning, and knowledge, revolving endlessly, there will be no time of attainment.*

Knowing words is like the murmuring of a spring; knowing actions is like the flight of birds; knowing learning is like grasping at shadows; knowing knowledge is like interpreting dreams. When not a single breath remains, the Dao will come to accord.
Knowing words is like the murmuring of a spring—its sounds cannot be distinguished. When sounds cannot be distinguished, one forgets names.
Knowing actions is like the flight of birds—its traces cannot be followed. When traces cannot be followed, one forgets traces.
Knowing learning is like grasping at shadows—its falsity cannot be seized. When it cannot be seized, one forgets methods.
Knowing knowledge is like interpreting dreams—its delusions cannot be trusted. When delusions cannot be trusted, one forgets emotions.
A *breath* is one inhalation and one exhalation. In the space of a single breath, names, traces, methods, and emotions are suddenly forgotten and no longer exist—then one accords with the wondrous Dao. Hence it is said: *Knowing words is like the murmuring of a spring; knowing actions is like the flight of birds; knowing learning is like grasping at shadows; knowing knowledge is like interpreting dreams. When not a single breath remains, the Dao will come to accord.* To *grasp* is to seize with the hand. This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to names, traces, methods, or emotions and cannot be sought through words, actions, learning, or knowledge.

**Chapter Sixteen**
Master Guan Yin said: To establish things through affairs is difficult; to abandon things through the Dao is easy.
*Through affairs* means all applications. *To establish* means to establish wondrous function. *Things* refers to beings. To establish wondrous function and universally benefit the world, enabling each person to realize the Dao, is truly not easy. Hence it is said: *To establish things through affairs is difficult.*
Sweeping away and returning to the origin, not a single thing remains—only the wondrous body of true emptiness is revealed. What difficulty could there be? Hence it is said: *To abandon things through the Dao is easy.*

**Chapter Seventeen**
In all under heaven, nothing is difficult to accomplish yet easy to destroy. The myriad things of the world, countless and boundless, all require perfect completion—truly no easy task. Hence it is said: *In all under heaven, nothing is difficult to accomplish.*
This explains *to establish things through affairs is difficult.* Consider Qin’s Epang Palace, which took more than a year to build, yet a single fire of Chu reduced it to ashes. Is this not *easy to destroy?* Hence it is said: *easy to destroy.* This explains *to abandon things through the Dao is easy.*
This chapter clarifies that establishing is not easy, while returning to the origin is not difficult.

**Chapter Eighteen**
Master Guan Yin said: A single spark of fire can burn ten thousand things—yet when the things are gone, where does the fire remain?
Fire is a thing without self. Whether struck from stone or kindled from wood, a single spark may be fanned into flame, and when great, it becomes a raging inferno capable of consuming all things. Yet when all things are burned away, the fire too ceases to exist, for fire has no self. Hence it is said: *A single spark of fire can burn ten thousand things—yet when the things are gone, where does the fire remain?*
This is the analogy.
A single breath of the Dao can obscure ten thousand things—yet when the things are gone, where does the Dao abide?
In the space of a single breath, the Dao of true emptiness pervades all, profound and obscure, with not a single thing remaining. Thus, one forgets all things externally, and in forgetting all things externally, one forgets internally as well. "True emptiness" is but a provisional name for the Dao. Hence it is said: *A single breath of the Dao can obscure ten thousand things—yet when the things are gone, where does the Dao abide?*
This is the application of the analogy.
This chapter clarifies that one who awakens to the Dao also transcends the provisional name of "Dao."

**Chapter Nineteen**
Master Guan Yin said: Among those born into the world, some die after a single day, some after ten years, some after a hundred years.
The lifespans of humans vary—some die in infancy, some in childhood, some live a full hundred years. Hence it is said: *Among those born into the world, some die after a single day, some after ten years, some after a hundred years.* This is the analogy.
Those who die after a single day are like those who attain the Dao in a single breath. Those who die after ten or a hundred years are like those who attain the Dao after a long time.
Those of sharp faculties, upon encountering a teacher’s guidance, awaken instantly. In the space of a single breath, their minds die, all doubt vanishes—like one who dies after a single day. Hence it is said: *Those who die after a single day are like those who attain the Dao in a single breath.*
Those of middling or lesser faculties, upon encountering a teacher’s guidance, struggle to awaken at once. Like water soaking into an object, they gradually penetrate, whether after ten or twenty years. With unwavering resolve, at some point they suddenly break through, their minds illuminated—like one who lives ten or a hundred years before dying. Hence it is said: *Those who die after ten or a hundred years are like those who attain the Dao after a long time.*
This is the application of the analogy.
Those who have not yet died, though they act with clarity and wisdom, are merely called "alive," not "dead."
"Those" refers to the three types mentioned above—those who die after a day, ten years, or a hundred years. Before their death, though they move and act with brightness and wisdom, they are merely called "alive," not "dead." Hence it is said: *Those who have not yet died, though they act with clarity and wisdom, are merely called "alive," not "dead."*
Those who have not yet realized the Dao, though they act with clarity and wisdom, are merely called "engaged in affairs," not "abiding in the Dao."
"Those" refers to the three types of sudden and gradual awakening mentioned above. Before realizing and embodying the Dao, though they move and act with brightness and wisdom, they are merely called "engaged in human affairs," not "abiding in the wondrous Dao." Hence it is said: *Those who have not yet realized the Dao, though they act with clarity and wisdom, are merely called "engaged in affairs," not "abiding in the Dao."*
This is the application of the analogy.
This chapter clarifies that awakening may be sudden or gradual, yet the Dao is not two.

**Chapter Twenty**
Master Guan Yin said: Not knowing that my Dao is without words or actions, yet seeking the Dao through words and actions, one may suddenly encounter strange phenomena and stubbornly cling to them as the Dao.
The deluded do not awaken to the fact that the Dao is originally without names or traces. Hence it is said: *Not knowing that my Dao is without words or actions.* The deluded, attached to the sacred words and dharmic forms of the ancients, seek the Dao through them. Or, with a mind that reveres the strange and wondrous, they may suddenly encounter demonic forces manifesting as extraordinary beings—expounding on heaven and earth, discussing affairs and principles, or displaying supernatural transformations. The deluded stubbornly cling to them as enlightened sages of the Dao, desiring to take them as teachers and seek the Dao. Hence it is said: *yet seeking the Dao through words and actions, one may suddenly encounter strange phenomena and stubbornly cling to them as the Dao.*
Little do they know that abandoning the source to seek the stream will never return them to the source; abandoning the root to seek the branches will never return them to the root.
The Dao is realized through the awakening of the mind. The numinous source is wondrously serene, its essence and function ever thus. The deluded, not awakening to this, abandon the source and seek the Dao externally through names and traces—this is to forsake the numinous source and chase after the flowing traces. The root is the source; the branches are the stream. Wandering in pursuit of branches, one can never return to the root. Hence it is said: *Little do they know that abandoning the source to seek the stream will never return them to the source; abandoning the root to seek the branches will never return them to the root.*
This chapter admonishes students of the Dao not to seek it through names and traces.

Guanyinzi said: In learning archery, charioteering, the zither, or chess, there is not one skill that can be mastered in a single breath. Learning archery means training with the bow and arrow; learning charioteering means training to drive a carriage; learning the zither means practicing its playing; learning chess means practicing the game of *qin*. These four pursuits all require years of practice, yet not one can be perfected in the span of a single breath. Thus it is said: *In learning archery, charioteering, the zither, or chess, there is not one skill that can be mastered in a single breath.*

Only the Dao, being formless and without direction, can be attained in a single breath. The Dao is true emptiness—without form or image, without orientation or position. Those of keen capacity may directly awaken to true emptiness in an instant, gaining penetrating clarity in the space of a single breath. Thus it is said: *Only the Dao, being formless and without direction, can be attained in a single breath.*

This chapter clarifies that the Dao is easier to learn than worldly skills.

Guan Yinzi said: When two archers meet, skill and clumsiness become apparent; when two chess players meet, victory and defeat become apparent. When archers meet but do not shoot, the one who hits the mark is seen as skillful, while the one who misses is seen as clumsy—thus it is said: *When two archers meet, skill and clumsiness become apparent.* When two players of *yi* meet to compete, the winner is seen as victorious, while the loser is seen as defeated—thus it is said: *When two chess players meet, victory and defeat become apparent.* When two practitioners of the Dao meet, there is nothing to display. Having nothing to display means there is no skill or clumsiness, no victory or defeat. When two people who have thoroughly penetrated the wondrous Dao meet, there is nothing to present or show—nothing to present or show means it is not like archery or chess, where skill and clumsiness, victory and defeat exist. Thus it is said: *When two practitioners of the Dao meet, there is nothing to display; having nothing to display means there is no skill or clumsiness, no victory or defeat.* This chapter clarifies that the Dao has no skill or clumsiness, no victory or defeat, and nothing to present or show to others.

---

Guan Yinzi said: My Dao is like the sea. Cast a hundred million pieces of gold into it, and it is not seen; cast a hundred million stones into it, and it is not seen; cast a hundred million impurities into it, and it is not seen. The sea is a metaphor for true emptiness. Gold is a metaphor for subtle words and wondrous conduct—true emptiness can obscure all wondrous conduct, so that when observed, it is not seen. Thus it is said: *My Dao is like the sea; cast a hundred million pieces of gold into it, and it is not seen.* Stones are a metaphor for all good deeds—true emptiness can obscure all goodness, so that when displayed, it is not seen. Thus it is said: *Cast a hundred million stones into it, and it is not seen.* Impurities are a metaphor for all evil—true emptiness can obscure all evil, so that when viewed, it is not seen. Thus it is said: *Cast a hundred million impurities into it, and it is not seen.* It can carry small minnows and small fish, and it can carry great leviathans and great whales. Small minnows and small fish are metaphors for petty people and ordinary beings; great leviathans and great whales are metaphors for noble persons and the worthy. True emptiness can carry petty people and ordinary beings, noble persons and the worthy—universally benefiting all equally, from birth to death, sustaining them without their awareness. Thus it is said: *It can carry small minnows and small fish, and it can carry great leviathans and great whales.* It gathers all waters and receives them without excess; it disperses all waters and divides them without insufficiency. True emptiness is not filled when augmented, so it obscures heaven and earth and the ten thousand things without being in excess. True emptiness is not diminished when reduced, so it gives rise to heaven and earth and the ten thousand things without being insufficient. Thus it is said: *It gathers all waters and receives them without excess; it disperses all waters and divides them without insufficiency.* This chapter clarifies that the Dao has no lack and no surplus, yet nothing is beyond its capacity.

---

Guan Yinzi said: My Dao is like dwelling in darkness. One who dwells in brightness cannot see a single thing in the dark, yet one who dwells in darkness can see all affairs in the light. The Dao is true emptiness—profound and obscure, beyond the reach of yin and yang, difficult for ghosts and spirits to fathom. Thus it is said: *My Dao is like dwelling in darkness; one who dwells in brightness cannot see a single thing in the dark.* Though true emptiness is profound and obscure, beyond the reach of yin and yang, and difficult for ghosts and spirits to fathom, its functioning is like pouring sweet wine into a vessel; its response to things is like a hanging mirror reflecting forms. It is also like an echo in an empty valley—still and unmoving, yet when stimulated, it instantly connects, and nothing can escape its measure. Thus it is said: *Yet one who dwells in darkness can see all affairs in the light.* This chapter clarifies that the Dao is profound and unfathomable, responding to change without end.

---

Guan Yinzi said: The power of petty people inclines toward evil. *Power* means application—the foolish and deluded among petty people apply themselves entirely to evil. What is evil? Killing, stealing, sexual misconduct, false speech, frivolous talk, divisive speech, greed, hatred, delusion, great rebellion, disobedience to parents, disloyalty, lack of benevolence, lack of righteousness—these are what is meant by *the power of petty people inclines toward evil.* The power of noble persons inclines toward goodness. Noble persons, enlightened to principle, apply themselves entirely to goodness. What is goodness? Not killing, not stealing, not engaging in sexual misconduct, not lying, not speaking with flattery or false charm, reverently practicing the Five Constants and the hundred virtues—this is what is meant by *the power of noble persons inclines toward goodness.* The power of sages inclines toward nothing to attain. Only with nothing to attain can one be in accord with the Dao. The application of sages is universally and equally beneficial, seeking no reward, embodying virtue without claiming virtue, responding to change without fixed method, transcending all extremes, surpassing all dharmic appearances, attaining nothing whatsoever—this is forcibly named the Dao of true emptiness. Thus it is said: *The power of sages inclines toward nothing to attain; only with nothing to attain can one be in accord with the Dao.* This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to goodness or evil, transcending all existence.

---

Guan Yinzi said: My Dao is like a sword. When its edge cuts things, it is sharp; when grasped by the hand, it injures. The sword is a metaphor for the Dao—the Dao obscures the ten thousand things, universally benefiting without end. Thus it is said: *My Dao is like a sword; when it cuts things, it is sharp.* Clinging and attachment obscure one’s nature and delude one about the Dao. Thus it is said: *When grasped by the hand, it injures.* This chapter clarifies that the Dao universally benefits without end, while those who are deluded and attached bring harm upon themselves.

---

Guan Yinzi said: A *lian* does not ask a *dou*, and a *dou* does not answer a *lian*; a tile does not ask a stone, and a stone does not answer a tile—yet the Dao is not lost. A *lian* is made of bamboo to hold dates and chestnuts; a *dou* is made of wood to hold offerings for sacrificial rites. The Dao is present in all things. Though the *lian*, *dou*, tile, and stone do not question or answer one another, they engage in questioning and answering all day long—yet though they question and answer, they have never truly questioned or answered.

Guan Yinzi said: Thus it is said—a *lian* does not ask a *dou*, and a *dou* does not answer a *lian*; a tile does not ask a stone, and a stone does not answer a tile—yet the Dao is not lost. This is the preaching of the insentient; the deluded do not understand. Thus it is said: though they do not respond, they conceal true response; where there is no knowing, knowing abides—this is true knowing. Question and answer arise from the single qi’s coming and going—where then is the Dao?

The Dao is fundamentally without question, without answer. Before question and answer arise, before I even conceive of questioning or answering, the heart does not stir. When the heart does not stir, the single qi neither comes nor goes. When the single qi neither comes nor goes, the heart knows no arising or ceasing. Without arising or ceasing, one merges with the wondrous Dao. Once the heart of questioning and answering stirs, wherever the heart goes, qi follows. Thus with each question and each answer, qi comes and goes. With qi’s coming and going, one drifts far from the Dao. Hence it is said: *At the subtlest point, the moment one conceives or deliberates, one falls.* Thus it is said: question and answer arise from the single qi’s coming and going—where then is the Dao?

This chapter clarifies that the Dao is universally present, yet question and answer are distinct and separate.

---

Guan Yinzi said: Those who look up to the Dao are lame; those who follow the Dao are errant. All know the affairs of the Dao, yet none know the Dao itself.

*Ba* means to aspire with longing; *bo* means to pursue with haste. *Ru* means to go forth. Those who look up in admiration of the sages’ Dao, aspiring yet never attaining—these are called *ba*, those who look up to the Dao. Those who go forth in hasty pursuit of the wondrous Dao yet never grasp it—these are called *bo*, those who follow the Dao. *Shi* means function. The above—longing aspiration and hasty pursuit—are but the functions of the Dao. Following function, one loses sight of its essence. Thus it is said: all know the affairs of the Dao, yet none know the Dao itself.

Thus it is said: the source of the Dao is not distant; the sea of nature is not remote. Seek only within yourself—do not search elsewhere. Even if you search, you will not find; even if you find, it will not be true. This is the truth of the matter.

Therefore, the sage neither longs for the Dao in want nor relies on the Dao in abundance. He does not borrow the Dao from the sage nor sell the Dao to the foolish.

*Qian* means deficiency; *feng* means surplus. *Jie* means to borrow; *gu* means to sell. *Shiyi* serves to connect the above and below. The wondrous Dao of true emptiness is inherently complete, neither deficient nor surplus. The sage, seeing clearly and penetratingly, knows the Dao is originally complete. Thus he does not long for the Dao in want, for true emptiness holds nothing. Nor does he rely on the Dao in abundance. Since the Dao is possessed by all, he does not borrow it from the sage—the sage cannot extract the Dao to show others. Nor does he sell the Dao to the foolish.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao is possessed by all, and one must not borrow or take it from another.

**#1** *"All people may attain life and penetrate the Mystery"* aligns with the *Collected Works of the Congshu Jichu Initial Compilation* version of the *Guan Yinzi*, while the *Wushang Miaodao Wenshi Zhenjing* in the *Daoist Canon* (Mu character section) reads *"All people may attain life and create the Mystery."*

**#2#3** *"Grasping the loss"* is an error; both the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wushang Miaodao Wenshi Zhenjing* read *"grasping the arrow."*

**#4** This phrase appears in the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wushang Miaodao Wenshi Zhenjing* as *"the things of the world."*

**#5** This phrase in the *Guan Yinzi* reads: *"yet those who seek the Dao through words and actions."*

---

**Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing, Scroll Two**
*Divine Peak’s Carefree Master Niu Daochun’s Direct Explication*
**The Two Pillars Chapter**
A pillar is that which establishes the wondrous workings of Heaven and Earth, yin and yang. This section comprises twelve chapters in all.

The *Guan Yinzi* states: Whether bowl or basin, whether cracked vessel or pot, whether jar or urn—all may establish Heaven and Earth.

The six items—bowl, basin, *ping* vessel, pot, jar, and urn—are all vessels in use. As objects, vessels are solid without and hollow within. The solid pertains to yin; the hollow pertains to yang. Yang belongs to Heaven; yin belongs to Earth. Thus, in establishing their wondrous workings, every thing may serve as a model.

Hence it is said: Whether bowl or basin, whether *ping* vessel or pot, whether jar or urn—all may establish Heaven and Earth.

Omen-tortoises, divining stalks, broken tiles, and veined stones—all may foretell good or ill fortune.

*Omens* are the precursors of good or ill fortune. By scorching a tortoise shell with fire and drilling its cracks, one examines the fissures to discern the omen of good or ill. *Divining stalks* are stalks of milfoil, a plant of the mugwort family from Caizhou. One bundle contains fifty stalks. Following the *Great Expansion* number, forty-nine are used, with one representing the Great Unity. These are evenly divided into two to symbolize the Two Modes. One is set aside to symbolize the intercalary month. They are arranged in fours to symbolize the four seasons. Three arrangements form one line, and eighteen changes form one hexagram. Once the hexagram is determined, the omen of good or ill fortune is revealed.

*Broken tiles* refer to tile divination: when a tile is struck and shatters unevenly, the odd fragments pertain to yang and foretell good fortune; even fragments pertain to yin and foretell ill fortune.

*Veined stones* are stones whose fissures are examined to discern good or ill fortune. If the veins are straight and the count odd, they pertain to yang and foretell **#1** fortune. If the veins are slanted and the count even, they pertain to yin and foretell ill fortune. Thus it is said: Omen-tortoises, divining stalks, broken tiles, and veined stones—all may foretell good or ill fortune.

From this, we know that the principle of Heaven and Earth **#2** is contained within a single thing, and every thing contains it, each without reliance on another.

*From this, we know*: Based on the above—that bowls, basins, *ping* vessels, pots, jars, and urns may all establish Heaven and Earth, and that omen-tortoises, divining stalks, broken tiles, and veined stones may all foretell good or **#3** fortune—we thus know that the principle of Heaven and Earth is contained within a single thing. *Principle* is the Dao.

The Dao is inherently complete and requires no cultivation. The Dao is omnipresent—Heaven and Earth, the myriad things, persist when aligned with the Dao and perish when they lose it. Thus it is said: To perceive the inherent Dao is to recognize that every thing contains the wondrous Dao. It is not that things contain it, but that Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things never depart from the wondrous Dao. Hence it is said: We know that the principle of Heaven and Earth is contained within a single thing, and every thing contains it, each without reliance on another. "Without reliance" means each thing is self-sufficient and needs not borrow from another.

When my essence converges with its essence, the two essences interact and the spirit responds. "It" refers to tortoises, stalks, tiles, and stones; "I" refers to the diviner. Though the tortoise be withered, the stalks dried, the tiles scorched, or the stones hardened, how could they possess inherent spiritual omens of fortune or misfortune? It is because my sincerity merges with the tortoise, stalks, tiles, and stones—the essence of the principle of Heaven and Earth—so that the two essences stir and interact, eliciting this spiritual response. Thus it is said: When my essence converges with its essence, the two essences interact and the spirit responds.

One female and one male hatch from eggs; one yin and one yang gestate in the womb. Among flying creatures, pairs are called female and male, and those with eight orifices hatch from eggs. Among walking beasts, pairs are called yin and yang, and those with nine orifices gestate in the womb. Thus it is said: One female and one male hatch from eggs; one yin and one yang gestate in the womb.

Form is its essence; principle is its spirit. "It" refers to female and male, yin and yang. When female and male, yin and yang unite, their essences and spirits merge as one to form eggs or embryos. The form of eggs and embryos is the essence of female and male, yin and yang. "Principle" is consciousness—this is their spirit. Thus it is said: Form is its essence; principle is its spirit.

Love is my essence; observation is my spirit. Love is water; observation is fire. When love clings and observation follows, they transform into wood. When observation abides and love gathers, they coalesce into metal. "I" refers to every person’s self. When a person conceives a thought of love, it pertains to the essence of their nature; when their gaze rests upon form, it pertains to their spirit. Essence belongs to water, hence love is water; spirit belongs to fire, hence observation is fire. Love as water and observation as fire cling and unite, thereby transforming into wood. When wood is pressed, water emerges to reveal the mother’s form; when drilled, fire appears to reveal the child’s form. Observation as spirit and love as essence abide and unite, thereby coalescing into metal. When metal is struck, fire appears to reveal the husband’s form; when smelted, water emerges to reveal the child’s form. Thus it is said: Love is my essence; observation is my spirit. Love is water; observation is fire. When love clings and observation follows, they transform into wood; when observation abides and love gathers, they coalesce into metal.

First conceive the primordial qi within a single thing, then cling to love to unite with its form and obscurely observe to unite with its principle—thus the image takes shape. "It" refers to the mother. The father’s nature is true emptiness, originally pure and still. When desire arises in the pure and still true emptiness, it is like the Dao generating the One qi. Thus it is said: First conceive the primordial qi. Once desire arises, the mother’s form is already present in consciousness. Thus it is said: It is contained within a single thing. The mother’s form exists in consciousness, and through clinging love, one unites with her essence and blood. Thus it is said: Cling to love to unite with its form. As the essences of father and mother merge in the indistinct and obscure, observation of form gives rise to love, completing their union. The father’s consciousness unites with the mother’s consciousness, forming the image of the embryo. The human form already exists within the womb. Thus it is said: Obscurely observe to unite with its principle—thus the image takes shape. "Principle" is consciousness. The above describes how the union of beasts and humans, their essences and spirits, elicits a response akin to the spiritual resonance between a diviner’s sincerity and tortoises, stalks, tiles, and stones.

The image of the One’s movement pervades the Great Void. From the center it ascends to become Heaven; from the center it descends to become Earth. The Dao generates the One—the Dao is the Limitless, and the One is the Supreme Ultimate. From the Limitless emerges the Supreme Ultimate, and within the Supreme Ultimate, the images of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are already contained. Though the Supreme Ultimate encompasses all images, it has not yet departed from the Limitless. Thus it is said: The image of the One’s movement pervades the Great Void. The Great Void is the Limitless. When the Supreme Ultimate moves, movement generates yang. The yang qi, light and pure, rises from the center of the Supreme Ultimate and coalesces to form Heaven. Thus it is said: From the center it ascends to become Heaven. When movement reaches its limit, it becomes still, and stillness generates yin. The yin qi, heavy and turbid, descends from the center of the Supreme Ultimate and congeals to form Earth. Thus it is said: From the center it descends to become Earth.

Nothing ascends without descending; nothing descends without ascending. At the winter solstice, yang is born, and over thirty-six pentads, the qi ascends for one hundred eighty days. When the yang qi reaches Heaven, yang at its extreme generates yin. At the summer solstice, yin is born, and over thirty-six pentads, the qi descends for one hundred eighty days. When the yin qi reaches Earth, yin at its extreme again generates yang. That which belongs to yang naturally ascends; that which belongs to yin naturally descends. Ascending and descending, coming and going, they circulate endlessly, thus generating and transforming without cease. Where there is ascent, there is descent; without ascent, there would be no descent. Without ascent or descent, there would be no life or death. Thus it is said: Nothing ascends without descending; nothing descends without ascending.

That which ascends is fire; that which descends is water. Fire belongs to yang and naturally ascends—fire flares upward, embodying the image of ascent. Water belongs to yin and naturally descends—water flows downward, embodying the image of descent.

Thus it is said: that which ascends is fire; that which descends is water. That which desires to ascend yet cannot ascend is wood; that which desires to descend yet cannot descend is metal.

Water generates wood, and wood generates fire. Fire is the child of wood; water is the mother of wood. The affection between child and mother resides entirely within wood. Fire desires to ascend, water desires to descend, yet wood’s qi desires to ascend but cannot—thus it is said: that which desires to ascend yet cannot ascend is wood. Water is the child of metal; fire is the husband of metal. The affection between husband and child resides entirely within metal. Water desires to descend, fire desires to ascend, yet metal’s qi desires to descend but cannot—thus it is said: that which desires to descend yet cannot descend is metal.

As for wood: when drilled, it yields fire; when wrung, it yields water. As for metal: when struck, it yields fire; when smelted, it yields water. Metal and wood are the intercourse of water and fire. Drilling wood to obtain fire reveals the child’s affection; wringing wood to obtain water reveals the mother’s affection—this explains why that which desires to ascend yet cannot ascend is wood, hence it is said: as for wood, when drilled, it yields fire; when wrung, it yields water. Striking metal to obtain fire reveals the husband’s affection; smelting metal to obtain water reveals the child’s affection—this explains why that which desires to descend yet cannot descend is metal, hence it is said: as for metal, when struck, it yields water.

As stated above, we know that metal and wood each contain the affections of water and fire, their intercourse giving rise to the transformation of metal and wood—thus it is said: metal and wood are the intercourse of water and fire.

Water is essence and Heaven; fire is spirit and Earth. Water is yang within yin—like the *kan* trigram, which belongs to yin, yet its central line belongs to yang. Yang naturally ascends, embodying the image of Heaven. In the Five Phases, water is water; in qi, it is Heaven; in humans, it is essence; in direction, it is north; in season, it is winter; in numbers, it is one and six; in trigrams, it is *kan*; in the Five Constants, it is wisdom; in stars, it is the Pole Star; in mountains, it is Mount Heng; in the viscera, it is the kidneys; in earthly branches, it is *hai* and *zi*; in heavenly stems, it is *ren* and *gui*—thus it is said: water is essence and Heaven.

Fire is yin within yang—like the *li* trigram, which belongs to yang, yet its central line belongs to yin. Yin naturally descends, embodying the image of Earth. In the Five Phases, fire is fire; in qi, it is Earth; in humans, it is spirit; in direction, it is south; in season, it is summer; in numbers, it is two and seven; in trigrams, it is *li*; in the Five Constants, it is propriety; in stars, it is Mars; in mountains, it is Mount Heng; in the viscera, it is the heart; in earthly branches, it is *si* and *wu*; in heavenly stems, it is *bing* and *ding*—thus it is said: fire is spirit and Earth.

Wood is the soul and humanity; metal is the corporeal soul and things. In the Five Phases, wood is wood; in qi, it is humanity; in spirit, it is the soul; in direction, it is east; in season, it is spring; in numbers, it is three and eight; in trigrams, it is *zhen*; in the Five Constants, it is benevolence; in stars, it is Jupiter; in mountains, it is Mount Tai; in the viscera, it is the liver; in earthly branches, it is *yin* and *mao*; in heavenly stems, it is *jia* and *yi*—thus it is said: wood is the soul and humanity.

In the Five Phases, metal is metal; in qi, it is things; in spirit, it is the corporeal soul; in direction, it is west; in season, it is autumn; in numbers, it is four and nine; in trigrams, it is *dui*; in the Five Constants, it is righteousness; in stars, it is Venus; in mountains, it is Mount Hua; in the viscera, it is the lungs; in earthly branches, it is *shen* and *you*; in heavenly stems, it is *geng* and *xin*—thus it is said: metal is the corporeal soul and things.

That which moves without cease is time; that which encompasses and contains is direction. The Five Phases revolve endlessly, forming the four seasons of spring, summer, autumn, and winter—thus it is said: that which moves without cease is time. Heaven and Earth encompass the myriad things, forming the ten directions of the eight extremes—thus it is said: that which encompasses and contains is direction.

Only Earth begins and ends all things. There are those who resolve it, and those who reveal it. Earth has no fixed phase but flourishes in the four seasons. Throughout the year, from beginning to end, all generation and completion are imbued with Earth’s qi. Spring and summer are for generation, the beginning of growth; autumn and winter are for completion, the fulfillment of things—thus it is said: only Earth begins and ends all things.

To resolve is to disperse and conceal: in the first and second months, wood reigns and Earth is hidden; in the third month, wood flourishes for twelve days, while Earth flourishes for eighteen days—flourishing is manifesting. In the fourth and fifth months, fire reigns and Earth is hidden; in the sixth month, fire flourishes for twelve days, while Earth flourishes for eighteen days. In the seventh and eighth months, metal reigns and Earth is hidden; in the ninth month, metal flourishes for twelve days, while Earth flourishes for eighteen days. In the tenth and eleventh months, water reigns and Earth is hidden; in the twelfth month, water flourishes for twelve days, while Earth flourishes for eighteen days.
