In the Five Phases, Earth is Earth; in direction, it is center; in season, it is the four seasons; in numbers, it is five and ten; in trigrams, it is *kun* and *gen*; in the Five Constants, it is trustworthiness; in stars, it is Saturn; in mountains, it is Mount Song; in earthly branches, it is *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*; in heavenly stems, it is *wu* and *ji*; in the viscera, it is the spleen; in spirit, it is intention.

When subtle words are heard, the wondrous intention is resolved and awakened, yielding the marvel of self-benefit—thus it is said: there are those who resolve it. Having awakened to the wondrous intention and attained self-benefit, one may also benefit others. For those of superior capacity, a gesture or secret indication suffices for sudden enlightenment, where even the wondrous intention transcends the measure of wondrous intention—thus it is said: there are those who reveal it.

This chapter elucidates the wondrous functioning of the establishment of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things. End of Chapter One.

Master Guan Yinzi said: The people of the world cannot be counted by the hundreds of millions. All who dwell in the world belong to boundless kinds—beyond reckoning by the measure of ten myriad as one *yi* or ten *yi* as one *zhao*. Thus it is said: the people of the world cannot be counted by the hundreds of millions.

Each person’s dreams differ; each night’s dreams differ. There is heaven, there is earth, there are people, there are things—all formed by thought. They cannot be counted by grains of dust. When all the world dreams in the same night, each dream realm is different—thus it is said: each person’s dreams differ. Each night’s dream realms differ—thus it is said: each night’s dreams differ. When the people of the world dream each night, they generally see heaven and earth, people and things—thus it is said: there is heaven, there is earth, there are people, there are things. What is seen in dreams—heaven and earth, people and things—is but the deluded perceptions and attachments of ordinary days. When dreaming, the spirit is confused and the *shi*-soul seizes control; what one has been attached to in waking life appears as visions in dreams—thus it is said: all are formed by thought. The dreams of all the world, though differing in the same night, cannot be numbered, let alone the endless variations night after night—thus they cannot be counted by grains of dust. (Dust is like sunlight glinting on motes.) How can we be certain that the present heaven and earth are not likewise the product of thought? *An* means “how can we know?” How can we know that in this waking state, where we see heaven and earth, people and things, there is no thinker? This is to say: the unenlightened, bound by their perceptions and attachments, see heaven and earth, people and things no differently than in dreams—deluded alike by day and night. Thus it is said: how can we know that the present heaven and earth are not the product of thought?

This chapter clarifies that the perceptions of the deluded—whether in waking or dreaming—take heaven and earth, people and things to be real, when in truth they are but the creations of deluded thought.

End of Chapter Two.

---

Master Guan Yinzi said: The heart corresponds to the jujube, the liver to the elm; I commune with heaven and earth. Heaven and earth generate the myriad things, each belonging to the Five Phases. The jujube is red and belongs to fire; fire corresponds to the heart—thus it is said: the heart corresponds to the jujube. The elm is green and belongs to wood; wood corresponds to the liver—thus it is said: the liver corresponds to the elm. Heaven and earth’s yin and yang *qi* intermingle to produce jujubes and elms; the heart corresponds to the jujube, the liver to the elm—this is how I commune with heaven and earth. Thus it is said: I commune with heaven and earth.

When rain is coming, one dreams of water; when clear skies are coming, one dreams of fire—heaven and earth commune with me. When the *qi* of heaven descends and the *qi* of earth ascends, clouds gather to bring rain. The yin *qi* affects a person, and at night one dreams of water—thus it is said: when rain is coming, one dreams of water. When the descending *qi* of heaven is restrained and the ascending *qi* of earth is withdrawn, the yang *qi* affects a person, and at night one dreams of fire—thus it is said: when clear skies are coming, one dreams of fire. As stated above, the yin and yang *qi* of heaven and earth affect a person, first manifesting in dreams of water or fire—this is how heaven and earth commune with me. Thus it is said: heaven and earth commune with me.

I and heaven and earth seem joined, seem separate, each returning to purity. As stated above, I commune with heaven and earth, and heaven and earth commune with me—thus I and heaven and earth seem joined, yet not joined. Heaven and earth contain humanity, and humanity contains heaven and earth; heaven and earth are the great person, and the person is the small heaven and earth—seeming distant, yet not distant. Thus it is said: I and heaven and earth seem joined, seem separate. Heaven and earth and I arise together. Heaven and earth have the Dao, and humanity has the Dao. The Dao is pure and without admixture, originally complete and true. The Dao is the归宿 of heaven, earth, and humanity—thus it is said: each returning to purity.

This chapter clarifies the Dao by which heaven, earth, and the myriad things are born.

End of Chapter Three.

---

Master Guan Yinzi said: Though heaven and earth are vast, they have color and form, number and direction. Heaven is dark and earth is yellow—thus they have color. Heaven is round and earth is square—thus they have form. Heaven and earth, having color and form, will eventually return to dust—thus they have number. Heaven and earth have the eight directions and above and below—thus they have direction. Though heaven and earth are vast, they have observable colors and forms, and their number and direction can be exhausted. Thus it is said: though heaven and earth are vast, they have color and form, number and direction.

Yet I possess that which is neither color nor form, neither number nor direction, yet gives rise to heaven and earth. I possess the Dao of true emptiness: it cannot be seen—thus it is not color; it cannot be grasped—thus it is not form. True emptiness is inexhaustible—thus it is not number; true emptiness is without location—thus it is not direction. The Dao of true emptiness generates heaven and earth. Heaven and earth have their number and will return to dust, yet dust gives rise to them again, and they to dust once more—endlessly arising and returning, arising and returning. Yet the Dao neither arises nor returns; it abides through endless kalpas. Thus it is said: I possess that which is neither color nor form, neither number nor direction, yet gives rise to heaven and earth.

This chapter clarifies that heaven and earth arise and return, while the Dao neither arises nor returns.

End of Chapter Four.

---

Master Guan Yinzi said: Those who die in the womb, those who die in the egg—whether human or thing—though heaven and earth are vast, they know nothing of them. *Bi* refers to those who die in the womb and those things that die in the egg. The natures of humans and things enter the womb and egg, but perish before birth. Though heaven and earth are vast, the natures of these humans and things know nothing of them. Thus it is said: those who die in the womb, those who die in the egg—whether human or thing—though heaven and earth are vast, they know nothing of them.

Those who conceive of heaven and earth do so through their limited perceptions. Though the human nature resides in the womb, it knows nothing of heaven and earth. Only after birth, in childhood, does one begin to know that what is above is called heaven and what is below is called earth. From childhood to old age, this knowledge is never forgotten. Thus, those who measure and conceive of heaven and earth do so through their limited perceptions. Thus it is said: those who conceive of heaven and earth do so through their limited perceptions.

End of Chapter Five.

Just as the hand does not touch the blade, the blade does not wound the person. The hand is likened to perception; the blade, to heaven and earth. Just as the hand does not grasp the sword’s edge, the sword’s edge does not wound the hand—so too does perception remain unstained by heaven and earth, and heaven and earth do not obscure the nature of perception. When perception is forgotten, one knows nothing of heaven and earth. Thus it is said: just as the hand does not touch the blade, the blade does not wound the person. This chapter clarifies how deluded perception obstructs the Dao.

End of Chapter Five.

Master Guan Yin said: In dreams, in mirrors, in water—heaven and earth exist therein. When one sleeps and dreams, one dimly perceives heaven, earth, and people. When a mirror reflects above and below, it captures heaven, earth, and people. Gazing into a clear pool, one sees heaven above and earth below; even the mountains and plains beside it become heaven and earth within the water. Thus it is said: in dreams, in mirrors, in water—heaven and earth exist therein.

To dispel the heaven and earth of dreams, one must not sleep. To dispel the heaven and earth of mirrors, one must not look. To dispel the heaven and earth of water, one must not draw from the vessel. The deluded do not awaken: true emptiness is unimpeded by heaven and earth, and heaven and earth are likewise unimpeded by true emptiness. Yet deluded perception, clinging to false habits, seeks to remove the heaven and earth of dreams, unaware that dreams themselves are illusory—how can they be dispelled? If one attempts to dispel them, one cannot rest peacefully. Thus it is said: to dispel the heaven and earth of dreams, one must not sleep.

Ancients cast mirrors to reflect forms, to see heaven when gazing at heaven, to see earth when gazing at earth. The mirror itself is without mind, yet deluded perception conceives of it as heaven and earth—does the mirror know this? How can it be dispelled? If one seeks to remove the heaven and earth within the mirror, one must shatter it. Yet if it is shattered, there will be no means to reflect the beauty or ugliness of forms. Thus it is said: to dispel the heaven and earth of mirrors, one must not look.

When water is clear, it reflects the sky above and the mountains and plains beside it; it nourishes things and, when drawn, sustains people. To remove the heaven and earth within water, one may dry it up. Yet if it is dried, the vessel will have no use for drawing. Thus it is said: to dispel the heaven and earth of water, one must not draw from the vessel.

Their existence or nonexistence lies not in them but in this. Therefore, the sage does not remove heaven and earth but removes perception. "Them" refers to dreams, mirrors, and water; "this" refers to perception. In dreams, mirrors, and water, sometimes heaven and earth appear, sometimes they do not. Whether they are reflected or not, whether heaven and earth exist or not, lies in the false conceptions of perception—not in the dreams, mirrors, or water themselves. Thus it is said: their existence or nonexistence lies not in them but in this.

"Therefore" is a phrase connecting the above to what follows. The sage, having awakened to true emptiness, perceives the stillness of the mind’s waves. Heaven and earth and true emptiness each abide in their own suchness, without mutual obstruction. Thus it is said: therefore, the sage does not remove heaven and earth but removes perception. This chapter clarifies how awakening shatters deluded perception, and true emptiness is unimpeded.

End of Chapter Six.

Master Guan Yin said: Heaven is not heaven of itself; there is that which makes it heaven. Earth is not earth of itself; there is that which makes it earth. Heaven and earth cannot generate themselves; that which generates heaven and earth is the Dao. The Dao generates the One; the One is the Supreme Ultimate. The One generates the Two; the Two are heaven and earth. Thus it is said: heaven is not heaven of itself; there is that which makes it heaven. Earth is not earth of itself; there is that which makes it earth.

For example, houses, boats, and carts depend on people to be completed; they do not complete themselves. Heaven and earth depend on the Dao for their generation and completion, just as houses, boats, and carts depend on human craftsmanship to be realized. How could houses, boats, and carts complete themselves? From this, we know that heaven and earth are generated and completed by the Dao’s operation of the Supreme Ultimate’s primordial qi. Thus it is said: for example, houses, boats, and carts depend on people to be completed; they do not complete themselves. "Them" refers to heaven and earth.

Knowing that they depend on something, knowing that this does not depend on anything—above, one does not see heaven; below, one does not see earth; within, one does not see the self; without, one does not see others. Knowing that heaven and earth depend on the Dao for their generation and completion, knowing that the great Dao depends on nothing for its generation and completion—as the classic says: "I know not whose child it is; it seems to have been before the Lord of Heaven." "Knowing" means awakening. Having awakened to the wondrous Dao, utterly free from duality, one transcends all opposition. Having transcended opposition, above and below, heaven and earth, within and without, self and others—all dualities vanish. Thus it is said: knowing that they depend on something, knowing that this does not depend on anything—above, one does not see heaven; below, one does not see earth; within, one does not see the self; without, one does not see others. This chapter clarifies that heaven and earth depend on the Dao for their generation, and the Dao is without duality in life and death.

End of Chapter Seven.

Master Guan Yin said: That which has time is qi; that which is not qi has never known day or night. What has temporal sequence is merely the movement of qi. The wondrous Dao and true emptiness are not qi. Before the Dao generated the One qi, there was no heaven, no earth, no sun, no moon—how could there have been day and night or the twelve hours? Thus it is said: that which has time is qi; that which is not qi has never known day or night.

That which has direction is form; that which is not form has never known north or south. What has spatial orientation is defined by form. The wondrous Dao and true emptiness are not form. Before the Dao generated the One qi, there was no heaven, no earth, no spatial orientation—how could there have been east, west, south, or north? Thus it is said: that which has direction is form; that which is not form has never known north or south.

What is meant by "not qi"? That from which qi itself arises. Like the wind obtained by waving a fan—before it is waved, there is no wind qi; once it is waved, it is called qi. Here, the text poses a question: What is meant by the wondrous Dao and true emptiness not being qi? It answers: that from which qi itself arises. For example, when one waves a bamboo fan to obtain a cool breeze, it is like the Dao’s operation generating the One qi. Thus it is said: What is meant by "not qi"? That from which qi itself arises—like the wind obtained by waving a fan.

The *dun* refers to a bamboo fan. Before the bamboo fan is waved, there is no wind qi—this illustrates the state before the Dao generated the One qi. Thus it is said: before it is waved, there is no wind qi. Yet when the bamboo fan is waved and a cool breeze arises, it is called qi—this illustrates the Dao in motion generating the One, which is then named qi. Thus it is said: once it is waved, it is called qi.

What is meant by "not form"? That from which form itself arises. For example, when one drills wood to obtain fire, before drilling, there is no form of fire; once drilled, it is called form. Here, the text poses another question: What is meant by the true emptiness of the Dao? It answers: that from which the forms of heaven and earth arise. For instance, when one drills wood and the form of fire appears, this illustrates the Supreme Ultimate (*Taiji*) dividing into the forms of height and depth. Thus it is said: What is meant by "not form"? That from which form itself arises—like drilling wood to obtain fire. Before one drills the wood, there is no form of fire; this illustrates the state before the Supreme Ultimate existed. Thus it is said: before drilling, there is no form of fire. Once the wood is drilled and fire appears, it is called the form of fire—this illustrates the state of "Non-Ultimate (*Wuji*) and yet Supreme Ultimate," where the Supreme Ultimate divides into the forms and positions of heaven and earth. Thus it is said: once drilled, it is called form. This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to time, number, or spatial orientation.

**Chapter Eight**
Master Guan Yin said: The transformations of cold, heat, warmth, and chill are like those of tiles and stones. Placed in fire, they become hot; placed in water, they become cold; breathed upon, they become warm; blown upon, they become chilled. The Dao moves primordial qi, transforming yin and yang and the Five Phases to produce winter’s cold, summer’s heat, spring’s warmth, and autumn’s chill—the seasonal shifts of primordial qi. Like tiles and stones, when placed in fire, they grow hot, just as in summer, when fire prevails, primordial qi follows the fire’s nature to become scorching heat. When tiles or stones are immersed in water, they grow cold, just as in winter, when water prevails, primordial qi follows the water’s nature to become severe cold. When one breathes upon tiles or stones for a long time, they grow warm, just as in spring, when wood prevails, primordial qi follows the wood’s nature to become mild warmth. When one blows upon tiles or stones for a long time, they grow cool, just as in autumn, when metal prevails, primordial qi follows the metal’s nature to become crisp chill. Thus it is said: The transformations of cold, heat, warmth, and chill are like those of tiles and stones—placed in fire, they become hot; placed in water, they become cold; breathed upon, they become warm; blown upon, they become chilled.

Yet these changes arise only because external things come and go, while the tiles and stones themselves neither come nor go. Tiles and stones are inherently without cold, heat, warmth, or chill; it is only because of external factors—water soaking, fire burning, breathing, or blowing—that cold, heat, warmth, or chill come and go, while the tiles and stones themselves remain without such comings or goings. Thus it is said: these changes arise only because external things come and go, while the tiles and stones themselves neither come nor go. This illustrates that primordial qi is inherently without the comings and goings of cold, heat, warmth, or chill. It is only because the qi of water, fire, metal, and wood wax and wane that cold, heat, warmth, and chill come and go. Like reflections in water, which come and go, the water itself does not come or go. First, the example of tiles and stones with water, fire, breath, and blowing illustrates the comings and goings of primordial qi’s cold, heat, warmth, and chill. Fearing this may not be clear, the text offers another analogy to enlighten: primordial qi, like the qi of water, fire, metal, and wood, is like reflections in water—while the reflections come and go, the water itself does not. From this, we know that while the qi of water, fire, metal, and wood wax and wane, primordial qi itself has no comings or goings. If primordial qi is without the comings and goings of cold, heat, warmth, or chill, how much more so the Dao? Thus it is said: like reflections in water, which come and go, the water itself does not come or go. This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to primordial qi, and primordial qi does not belong to time.

**Chapter Nine**
Master Guan Yin said: Shake a garment in empty space to summon wind; exhale upon an object to produce water; pour water into water to create sound; strike stone against stone to generate light. One who understands this principle can command wind, rain, thunder, and lightning. Humans are miniature heavens and earths, and heaven and earth are great humans. Wind, rain, thunder, and lightning are the works of heaven and earth, yet humans can do the same. When a person shakes a garment, wind arises from empty space; when one exhales upon an object, water forms—this is the image of wind and rain. When water is poured into water, sound resounds; when two stones are struck together, sparks fly—this is the image of thunder and lightning. One who understands this principle can summon wind and rain, instantly arouse thunder and lightning—it is no difficult feat. Thus it is said: shake a garment in empty space to summon wind; exhale upon an object to produce water; pour water into water to create sound; strike stone against stone to generate light. One who understands this principle can command wind, rain, thunder, and lightning.

Wind, rain, thunder, and lightning all arise from qi, and qi arises from the mind. *Yin* means "cause"—wind, rain, thunder, and lightning arise from yin and yang. When yang qi descends but finds no yin qi to receive it, it transforms into wind. When yin qi ascends but finds no yang qi to meet it, it transforms into clouds. When yin and yang clash, they generate thunder and lightning. From this, we know that wind, rain, thunder, and lightning arise from qi. Thus it is said: wind, rain, thunder, and lightning all arise from qi. One who awakens to the wondrous Dao of true emptiness, grasps the source of yin-yang and the One qi, and penetrates the marvels of creation, can wield the true mind to transform qi, summoning wind and rain, instantly arousing thunder and lightning—these are but trifling feats for the sage. Thus it is said: and qi arises from the mind. Just as inwardly contemplating great fire for a long time produces warmth, and inwardly contemplating great water for a long time produces cold, one who understands this principle can emulate the powers of heaven and earth.

The preceding discussion of wind, rain, thunder, and lightning as attainable arts may still leave some in doubt, so further analogies are offered to clarify. Consider those who practice forced and contrived methods, inwardly visualizing the lower elixir field as a great wheel of fire—over time, their entire body grows warm, and they mistake this warmth for harmony, though it is not. Or those who inwardly contemplate their body and mind as a vast, still, icy pool—over time, they feel their whole body grow cold and assume this to be a realm of pure clarity, though it is not. Such deluded imaginings of heat and cold still shift with the mind’s intent; how much more so when the true mind is awakened? One who grasps this analogy can summon wind and rain, instantly arouse thunder and lightning—these are the virtues of heaven and earth, which the enlightened may all emulate. This chapter reveals the Dao as the origin of yin and yang, its power harmonizing with the workings of heaven and earth.

**Chapter Ten**
Master Guan Yin said: The transformations of the five clouds can foretell the year’s abundance or scarcity. The five clouds are the clouds of five colors—blue, yellow, red, white, and black. Their transformations refer to the mutual changes among these five. "The year" means the current year; "abundance" means a bountiful harvest, while "scarcity" means famine and crop failure. According to *The Book of Cloud Omens*: On the first morning of the first lunar month, observe the five directions. The appearance of five-colored clouds in each direction indicates abundance or scarcity for that region. Black clouds foretell little snow and much rain in that direction; red clouds foretell drought and crop failure; blue clouds foretell pests and poor wheat harvests; white clouds foretell human calamities and poor yields of japonica and glutinous rice; yellow clouds foretell peace among the people and moderate harvests of crops. If the yellow clouds take on a verdant hue, the region will enjoy a great harvest of the five grains. The five grains are those with edges, husks, awns, and ears. If black, red, blue, white, or yellow clouds appear alone, scarcity follows; if the five colors intermingle or take on a verdant yellow, abundance ensues. Thus it is said: The transformations of the five clouds can foretell the year’s abundance or scarcity.

The arrival of the eight winds can foretell the fortune or misfortune of the moment. The eight winds are the winds arising from the directions of the eight trigrams. "Arrival" refers to winds blowing from those directions. According to *The Book of Wind Omens*: Each day at the hour of *yin*, observe the following: Winds from the *qian* direction are called the *buzhou* wind—neither wholly auspicious nor inauspicious. Winds from the *kan* direction are called the *guangmo* wind—neither fortunate nor misfortunate. Winds from the *gen* direction are called the *rong* wind, also known as the inauspicious wind—half fortunate, half misfortunate. Winds from the *zhen* direction are called the *mingshu* wind—greatly auspicious. Winds from the *xun* direction are called the *qingming* wind—greatly auspicious. Winds from the *li* direction are called the *jing* wind—greatly auspicious. Winds from the *kun* direction are called the *dong* wind—misfortunate. Winds from the *dui* direction are called the *yanhe* wind—greatly misfortunate. Thus it is said: The arrival of the eight winds can foretell the fortune or misfortune of the moment.

From this, we know that good and ill fortune, calamity and blessing, all arise from the movement of qi. "From this, we know" means that by divining abundance or scarcity, fortune or misfortune through wind and clouds, we understand that their transformations—whether auspicious or inauspicious, calamitous or blessed, scarce or abundant—all proceed from the movement of qi. Thus it is said: From this, we know that good and ill fortune, calamity and blessing, all arise from the movement of qi.

Blending self and others, harmonizing with heaven and earth—yet those of private cleverness claim it as their own. Self, others, heaven, and earth all arise from the movement of the One qi, and when that qi is exhausted, self, others, heaven, and earth perish. That they endure now shows their unity in the One qi. Thus it is said: Blending self and others, harmonizing with heaven and earth. The foregoing divinations of wind and clouds, the blending of self and others, and the harmony with heaven and earth are all products of deluded perception and private cleverness. Such deluded perception and private cleverness are not the original nature. Those who mistake deluded perception and private cleverness for their true nature are in error. Thus it is said: yet those of private cleverness claim it as their own. This chapter reveals that private cleverness is not the nature, for all arises from the movement of the One qi.

**Chapter Eleven**
Master Guan Yin said: Heaven and earth are lodged, the myriad things are lodged, the self is lodged, the Dao is lodged. If separated from lodging, the Dao cannot stand. "Lodged" means to be entrusted. Nothing surpasses yin and yang in qi, nor heaven and earth in form. Heaven and earth are the greatest of the formed, yet they are but minute things within the Great Void—they are entrusted within the primordial qi of the Great Void. Thus it is said: Heaven and earth are lodged. The myriad things are entrusted within the primordial qi between heaven and earth. Thus it is said: The myriad things are lodged. The self, originally without self, is the self without self, entrusted within the primordial qi of the Dao to gain its life. Thus it is said: The self is lodged. The Dao, originally without name, is entrusted by the sage with a forced name to proclaim it. Thus it is said: The Dao is lodged. "If" means supposing; if separated from the forced name of the Dao, then no name can be named, and the forced name of the Dao cannot stand. Thus it is said: If separated from lodging, the Dao cannot stand. This chapter reveals that from the original source, the trace descends, yet does not depart from lodging; the name, following the trace, returns to the source, and the forced name of the Dao cannot stand.

**End of *Wenzhi Zhenjing Zhu*, Scroll Two**

**Annotations to the Text**

1. "告" should be emended to "吉."
2. This sentence appears in *Guanyinzi* and *Wushang Miaodao Wenzhi Zhenjing* as: "Thus it is known that heaven, earth, and the myriad things attain their principle."
3. "皆能告吉大" should be emended to "皆能告吉凶."
4. "卯生" should be emended to "卵生."
5. "故云安知令之大地" should be emended to "故云安知今之天地."
6. According to the engraved text above, this sentence should read: "故云欲去梦天地者."
7. In *Guanyinzi*, following "万物寓," there is also the phrase "我寓."

**End of *Wenzhi Zhenjing Zhu*, Scroll Three**
**Direct Explication by Shenfeng Xiaoyaozi Niu Daochun**
**The Three Ultimates**

The "Ultimate" signifies the supreme attainment of transcending the ordinary and surpassing the sagely to embody the Dao, comprising twenty-seven chapters.

Master Guanyin said: In governing the world, the sage does not impose his own standards of worth or folly, thus he responds to others' worth by affirming their worth, and to others' folly by affirming their folly.

The sage is the sagely Son of Heaven who has fully realized the Dao. To govern the world means the sagely Son of Heaven pacifies and comforts the people through virtue and the Dao. Thus it is said: "the sage governs the world." "Does not impose his own standards of worth or folly" means the sage is without self; the worthy manifest their worth, and the foolish manifest their folly, yet the names of worth and folly do not originate from the sage. Thus it is said: "does not impose his own standards of worth or folly." "Thus" is a phrase connecting the preceding and following statements.

When the multitude all declare someone worthy, the sage, responding to their acclaim, also affirms their worth and advances them. When the multitude all declare someone foolish, the sage, responding to their censure, also affirms their folly and demotes them. Thus it is said: "he responds to others' worth by affirming their worth, and to others' folly by affirming their folly." He does not impose his own standards of right or wrong, thus he responds to affairs that are right by affirming their rightness, and to affairs that are wrong by affirming their wrongness.

Since the sage is without self, right and wrong arise from the affairs themselves, and the names of right and wrong do not originate from the sage. Thus it is said: "does not impose his own standards of right or wrong." "Affairs" refer to application. Though the essence is originally nameless, names are established according to function. When applied to good, if the multitude all declare it right, the sage, responding to their acclaim, also affirms its rightness and rewards it. When applied to evil, if the multitude all declare it wrong, the sage, responding to their censure, also affirms its wrongness and punishes it. Thus it is said: "he responds to affairs that are right by affirming their rightness, and to affairs that are wrong by affirming their wrongness."

He knows the great unity of past and present, thus sometimes he precedes the past, sometimes he precedes the present.

"Knows" means awakening. The past refers to the principles before the present; the present refers to the affairs before us. The sage, having realized the Dao and awakened to affairs, perceives principle and affair as non-dual, past and present as one continuum. Thus it is said: "knows the great unity of past and present." The sage attains the marvel of self-benefit and benefiting others, thus sometimes he first reveals principle to others, sometimes he first reveals affairs to others. Those of superior capacity may enter through principle or through affairs; though principle and affair differ, entry is without distinction. Thus it is said: "sometimes he precedes the past, sometimes he precedes the present."

He knows the great unity of inner and outer, thus sometimes he precedes the inner, sometimes he precedes the outer.

"Knows" means awakening. The sage, having fully realized the marvelous Dao, attains the wondrous union of form and spirit, body and mind as one continuum. Thus it is said: "knows the great unity of inner and outer." The sage, with boundless compassion and universal benefit, for those of superior capacity, first reveals the clarity of mind and awakening of nature. Those of superior capacity directly and suddenly awaken to the originally pure source of the mind. Thus it is said: "sometimes he precedes the inner." For those of middling and lesser capacity, fearing they may not easily awaken, the sage first reveals the marvels of cultivating the body and preserving life, enabling them to gradually comprehend. Thus it is said: "sometimes he precedes the outer."

The things of the world cannot burden him, thus he fundamentally embodies humility.

The sage, having penetrated true emptiness and embodied the great void, though the things of the world are myriad and boundless, how could they ever burden or obstruct the void? Thus it is said: "the things of the world cannot burden him." The sage, in nurturing the world, models himself after the rivers and seas, dwelling below, embracing dust and enduring defilement. The myriad states flow toward and submit to him; the world delights in exalting him without weariness. All are effects of the fundamental virtue of humility. Thus it is said: "fundamentally embodies humility."

The things of the world cannot estrange him, thus he embraces them with emptiness.

"Things" refer to all beings. The sage, governing the world with virtue and the Dao, causes the myriad states to return to virtue and submit to transformation, willingly becoming his subjects without weariness. Thus the people of the world cannot be estranged from the sage. Thus it is said: "the things of the world cannot estrange him." Having gained the submission and transformation of the myriad states, willingly becoming his subjects without weariness, the sage also does not become self-satisfied; his mind is one with the great void. Thus it is said: "he embraces them with emptiness."

The things of the world cannot make governance difficult for him, thus he conducts affairs with ease.

The people of the world delight in returning to sagely virtue; there are no arduous or difficult tasks to be imposed. The common people delight in peace and prosperity, and there are no people difficult to transform. Thus the affairs he administers are without difficulty. Thus it is said: "the things of the world cannot make governance difficult for him, thus he conducts affairs with ease."

The things of the world cannot obstruct him, thus he transforms them with adaptability.

In a world of great peace, the common people prosper, harmonious qi flows freely, heaven and earth, yin and yang are in harmonious accord, and the myriad things fulfill their growth and completion without a single thing being obstructed or blocked. The sage, nurturing himself with the Dao and acting without contrivance, responds to change with virtue and applies it. "Adaptability" means application. Thus it is said: "the things of the world cannot obstruct him, thus he transforms them with adaptability."

With this centered governance of the world, rites may be established. "With this" refers to the application of adaptability. "Centered" means without the faults of excess or deficiency. When the application of adaptability is fitting and does not lose the hearts of the world's people, rites may be established to rectify the relationships between ruler and subject, superior and inferior, and to distinguish the roles of father and son. Thus it is said: "With this centered governance of the world, rites may be established."

With this harmonization of the world, music may be created. Music embodies harmony, valuing it without indulgence. When this virtue is applied to nurture the age, the world attains peace, rites and music flourish, and moral transformation becomes beautiful. Thus it is said: "With this harmonization of the world, music may be created." "Creation" signifies flourishing.

With this impartial governance of the world, finances may be managed. When this virtue and power nurture the age, those who are just and selfless may be entrusted with the administration of the world's resources. Thus it is said: "With this impartial governance of the world, finances may be managed."

With this all-encompassing governance of the world, insults may be repelled. When this virtue and power nurture the age, transforming the entire world, those who insult their superiors or act with arrogance will cease of their own accord without the need for restraint. "Repel" means to prohibit. Thus it is said: "With this all-encompassing governance of the world, insults may be repelled."

With this responsiveness to the world, laws may be established. When this virtue and power nurture the age, if there are those who harm the world's people, laws may be established to eliminate them; if there are those who benefit the world's people, laws may be established to promote them. Thus it is said: "With this responsiveness to the world, laws may be established."

With this observation of the world, tools may be crafted. When this virtue and power nurture the age, observing what is convenient for the world's people, tools may be crafted and provided for their use. Thus it is said: "With this observation of the world, tools may be crafted."

The sage does not govern the world by himself but governs it through the world. The sage, free from self, nurtures the age with virtue and power, so that the world's people each settle into their occupations, delight in great peace, and forget the ruler's influence. Thus it is said: "The sage does not govern the world by himself but governs it through the world."

The world attributes merit to the sage, yet the sage ascribes merit to the world. When the world's people attribute the merit of great peace to the sage, the sage does not claim it as his own, nor does he say, "I am capable of governing the world." The sage nurtures himself with humility and ascribes merit to the world's people. This explains why the sage does not govern the world by himself but governs it through the world. Thus it is said: "The world attributes merit to the sage, yet the sage ascribes merit to the world."

Therefore, in the governance of Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang, the world all declared it to be natural. The four emperors—Tang Yao, Yu Shun, Xia Yu, and Shang Tang—governed the world to great peace, causing the people to forget the ruler's influence. The world's people all declared the peace to be natural, asking, "What has the ruler's power to do with it?" Thus it is said: "In the governance of Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang, the world all declared it to be natural." This chapter clarifies that the sage nurtures the age with virtue and power, free from self.

**Chapter One**

Master Guan Yin said: Heaven covers all without exception, giving life and bringing death, yet heaven harbors no love or hatred. The greatest is heaven, which shelters not a single thing from its covering. It gives life in spring and brings death in autumn, yet it acts without intent. How could heaven love and give life, or hate and bring death? Thus it is said: "Heaven covers all without exception, giving life and bringing death, yet heaven harbors no love or hatred."

The sun shines upon all without exception, revealing beauty and ugliness, yet the sun shows no partiality. The brightest is the sun, which shines upon not a single thing without its illumination. Because of the sun's light, beauty is revealed in its loveliness and ugliness in its coarseness, yet the sun treats beauty and ugliness equally, shining upon all without intent. How could it favor beauty with its light or slight ugliness by withholding it? Thus it is said: "The sun shines upon all without exception, revealing beauty and ugliness, yet the sun shows no partiality." This chapter uses heaven and the sun as metaphors for the sage's universal benefit and impartiality.

**Chapter Two**

Master Guan Yin said: The sage's Way follows the mandate of heaven, not something the sage can claim as his own. The Way is beyond thought or discussion. "Heaven" refers to that which is so of itself. "Mandate" refers to that which is so without knowing why. The sage thoroughly comprehends the unfathomable Way, which is so of itself, so without knowing why. Even the forced name of the Way is not established, nor is there anything the sage can claim as his ability. Thus it is said: "The sage's Way follows the mandate of heaven, not something the sage can claim as his own."

The sage's virtue accords with the times, not something the sage can claim as his own virtue. "Accords" means to align. The sage universally benefits the age without seeking repayment or gratitude, forgetting his abilities and not clinging to the name of virtue. Thus it is said: "The sage's virtue accords with the times, not something the sage can claim as his own virtue."

The sage's affairs are for the sake of others, not something the sage can claim as his own affairs. "Affairs" means application. The sage applies the use of non-use to universally benefit without end, acting for others without the intent to act. Thus it is said: "The sage's affairs are for the sake of others, not something the sage can claim as his own affairs."

Therefore, the sage does not possess the Way, does not possess virtue, and does not possess affairs. The sage acts without intent, so without knowing why. Thus he does not possess the name of the Way, the traces of virtue, or the application of affairs. Thus it is said: "Therefore, the sage does not possess the Way, does not possess virtue, and does not possess affairs." This chapter clarifies that the sage sweeps away all to return to the root, free from self.

**Chapter Three**

Master Guan Yin said: The sage knows that the self is no-self, thus he unites all with benevolence. Benevolence is compassion, kindness, and benefiting others. The sage profoundly embodies true emptiness, where the self is fundamentally no-self, and the name "self" is merely provisional. The sage awakens to this, so in benevolence, there is no difference between self and other. Thus it is said: "The sage knows that the self is no-self, thus he unites all with benevolence."

**Chapter Four**

Master Guan Yin said: Knowing that affairs are no-self, thus he adapts them with rightness. Affairs are application; adaptation is also application. The sage awakens to the application of no-self to suit all things. Thus it is said: "Knowing that affairs are no-self, thus he adapts them with rightness." Rightness is appropriateness. Knowing that the mind is no-self, thus he disciplines it with ritual. The sage awakens to the originally wondrous mind, fundamentally without the mark of self, thus he disciplines others with the practice of proper conduct. Thus it is said: "Knowing that the mind is no-self, thus he disciplines it with ritual." Ritual is practice, also proper conduct. Knowing that consciousness is no-self, thus he illuminates it with wisdom. The sage awakens to the source of numinous consciousness, fundamentally without self, thus he employs illumination to discern right and wrong, truth and falsehood. Thus it is said: "Knowing that consciousness is no-self, thus he illuminates it with wisdom." Wisdom is the discernment of right and wrong, also the examination of truth and falsehood. Knowing that speech is no-self, thus he upholds it with trustworthiness. The sage awakens to speech without speech, also without the mark of self in names, thus he preserves true sincerity, where word and deed accord. Thus it is said: "Knowing that speech is no-self, thus he upholds it with trustworthiness." This chapter clarifies that the sage, being without self, wondrously applies the five constant virtues.

**Chapter Five**

Master Guan Yin said: The Way of the sage may employ benevolence as benevolence, or rightness as benevolence, or ritual, wisdom, or trustworthiness as benevolence. "Employ" means application. The sage takes the Dao as essence and the five constants as application. Applying them to compassion, kindness, and benefiting others, thus it is said: "The Way of the sage may employ benevolence as benevolence." Or adjudicating right and wrong, making things fitting and appropriate while rooted in compassion, thus it is said: "or rightness as benevolence." Or practicing proper conduct while rooted in compassion and kindness, or discerning truth and falsehood while rooted in compassion and kindness, or aligning word and deed while rooted in compassion and kindness, thus it is said: "or ritual, wisdom, or trustworthiness as benevolence." Benevolence, rightness, ritual, wisdom, and trustworthiness each encompass the five. Sometimes practicing transformation encompasses rightness, ritual, wisdom, and trustworthiness; sometimes practicing rightness encompasses ritual, wisdom, trustworthiness, and benevolence; sometimes practicing ritual encompasses wisdom, trustworthiness, transformation, and rightness; sometimes practicing wisdom encompasses trustworthiness, rightness, ritual, and benevolence; sometimes practicing trustworthiness encompasses benevolence, rightness, ritual, and wisdom. Thus it is said: "Benevolence, rightness, ritual, wisdom, and trustworthiness each encompass the five." The sage unifies without attachment; the world cannot name him. The sage penetrates true emptiness, transcends all extremes, surpasses all dharmic marks, and abides in non-attainment, unstained by names. No name can capture him, and the people of the world cannot describe the sage’s appearance. Thus it is said: "The sage unifies without attachment; the world cannot name him." "Without attachment" means without clinging. This chapter clarifies the sage’s application without application, where no traces or names remain.

**Chapter Six**

Master Guan Yin said: Do not observe the sage through conduct, for the Dao leaves no traces. The sage moves contrary or in accord, unfathomable even to spirits and gods. The Dao of true emptiness leaves no trace to follow. Thus it is said: "Do not observe the sage through conduct, for the Dao leaves no traces." "Traces" are footprints; "do not" is a prohibitive term. Do not observe the sage through speech, for the Dao is without speech. "Do not observe the sage through speech" means the sage’s speech is speechless, allowing others to awaken to the meaning beyond words. The meaning beyond words is the Dao itself, which is fundamentally nameless and cannot be captured in speech. Do not observe the sage through ability, for the Dao is without action. "Do not observe the sage through ability" means the sage acts without acting, acting through non-action. Thus it is said: "Do not observe the sage through ability, for the Dao is without action." Do not observe the sage through appearance, for the Dao is without form. "Do not observe the sage through appearance" means the sage’s appearance does not differ from others. His essence merges with true emptiness, leaving no form to behold. Thus it is said: "Do not observe the sage through appearance, for the Dao is without form." This chapter clarifies that the sage’s Dao does not belong to traces, speech, action, or form, and cannot be observed through conduct, speech, ability, or appearance.

**Chapter Seven**

Master Guan Yin said: Though conduct may be most eminent, it does not depart from high and low. When one cultivates goodness, standing out from the multitude, the multitude is low while virtuous conduct is high. High and low are relative and do not depart from traces. Thus it is said: "Though conduct may be most eminent, it does not depart from high and low." This chapter explains the previous chapter’s "Do not observe the sage through conduct." Though speech may be most refined, it does not depart from right and wrong. When one speaks with wondrous principle, differing from the multitude, the multitude’s speech is unrefined and thus wrong, while refined speech is right. Right and wrong are relative and do not depart from names and marks. Thus it is said: "Though speech may be most refined, it does not depart from right and wrong." "Refined" means skillful and wondrous. This chapter explains the previous chapter’s "Do not observe the sage through speech." Though ability may be most divine, it does not depart from skill and clumsiness. When one’s creations and actions are divinely wondrous, differing from the multitude, those who cannot are clumsy, while those who can are skillful. Skill and clumsiness are relative and do not depart from action. Thus it is said: "Though ability may be most divine, it does not depart from skill and clumsiness." This chapter explains the previous chapter’s "Do not observe the sage through ability." Though appearance may be most extraordinary, it does not depart from beauty and ugliness. When one’s appearance is surpassingly beautiful, differing from the multitude, the multitude’s appearance is not beautiful and thus ugly, while beautiful appearance is lovely. Beauty and ugliness are relative and do not depart from provisional form. Thus it is said: "Though appearance may be most extraordinary, it does not depart from beauty and ugliness." This chapter explains the previous chapter’s "Do not observe the sage through appearance." The sage employs these to manifest to the world; when the world darkly comprehends them, only then does it perceive the sage. The sage descends into the world, employing conduct, speech, ability, and appearance to manifest to all. Thus it is said: "The sage employs these to manifest to the world." When students of the world awaken before the arising of conduct, speech, ability, or appearance, penetrating and comprehending the profound emptiness of the sage’s wondrous Dao, thus it is said: "When the world darkly comprehends them, only then does it perceive the sage."

This chapter clearly instructs students in attaining the wondrous by following the flow.

**Chapter Seven**
Master Guan Yin said: The sage took the bee as teacher to establish ruler and subject, took the spider as teacher to establish nets and snares, took the bowing rat as teacher to create ritual, and took the fighting mantis as teacher to devise weaponry.

The sages of antiquity, observing the bees’ hierarchical order, took them as their model and thus established ruler and subject to govern the world—hence it is said: *The sage took the bee as teacher to establish ruler and subject.* The sages of the middle age, observing the spider weaving its web to catch food, took it as their model for combat and thus established nets and snares to capture birds and beasts for sacrifice—hence it is said: *took the spider as teacher to establish nets and snares.* The sage, observing the mountain rat that bows and chirps before humans, took it as their model and thus created ritual and music to rectify high and low, distinguish noble and base, and order the great human relationships. Further observing the mole crickets in their two burrows, fighting and biting over food, they took them as their model and thus devised weaponry to punish the rebellious—hence it is said: *took the bowing rat as teacher to create ritual, took the fighting mantis as teacher to devise weaponry.*

The common people take the worthy as their teachers; the worthy take the sages as their teachers; the sages take the myriad things as their teachers. Only the sage is one with things, and thus has no self.

The common people take the worthy as their teachers to learn the Five Constants and the hundred practices—hence it is said: *The common people take the worthy as their teachers.* The worthy take the sages as their teachers to learn the great Dao of non-action—hence it is said: *The worthy take the sages as their teachers.* The sage, with boundless compassion and universal benefit, takes the natural principles of the myriad things as their teachers and employs all manner of expedient means to benefit the world—hence it is said: *The sages take the myriad things as their teachers.* The sage, penetrating the true emptiness, dwells in the world with harmonious light yet unobtrusive, moving with the dust yet unstained, no different from others, embodying the void and thus without self—hence it is said: *Only the sage is one with things, and thus has no self.*

This chapter reveals the sage’s boundless universal benefit.

**Chapter Eight**
Master Guan Yin said: The sage says: *Observe the Dao—heaven, earth, and the myriad things are all my Dao.* They harmonize with its call and response, its beginnings and endings, its green and yellow, its hatching and nurturing. They do not cling to the Dao, do not discard things, do not exalt the noble, do not despise the lowly.

The sage’s mind merges with the supreme Dao, and in reflection, heaven, earth, and the myriad things are all their wondrous Dao—hence it is said: *The sage says: Observe the Dao—heaven, earth, and the myriad things are all my Dao.* They further observe the call and response of husband and wife, the beginnings and endings of the myriad things, the green and yellow of grasses and trees, the hatching and nurturing of male and female—all are the wondrous Dao. Hence it is said: *They harmonize with its call and response, its beginnings and endings, its green and yellow, its hatching and nurturing.* The sage, awakening to the truth that heaven, earth, and the myriad things—great and small, soaring and hidden, moving and still—are all the wondrous Dao, does not generate thoughts of cherishing the Dao or discarding things, of exalting the noble or despising the lowly. Hence it is said: *They do not cling to the Dao, do not discard things, do not exalt the noble, do not despise the lowly.*

The worthy say: *Each thing differs; daily they discard, daily they bestow, lengthen and shorten, straighten and square—these are those enslaved by things.*

*Things* here mean *use.* The worthy, deluded about their essence, focus solely on use, and their uses are not uniform—hence it is said: *Each thing differs.* *Dawn* means *day.* The worthy daily discard evil and daily bestow kind words, urging others to practice—hence it is said: *daily they discard, daily they bestow.* The worthy instruct others, clarifying that what is right is long in principle, what is wrong is short in principle, and strive to make people practice upright and square conduct—hence it is said: *lengthen and shorten, straighten and square.* Those who, deluded about their essence, focus solely on use, whose uses are not uniform, who ceaselessly debate discarding and bestowing, lengthening and shortening, straightening and squaring—these are those who make use their servants and masters. Hence it is said: *these are those enslaved by things.*

Little do they know that the sage scorns the impure and distinguishes proper dwellings, doing so for others, not for themselves.

*Scorns the impure* means despising those who are base, mixed, and defiled by impurity. *Distinguishes proper dwellings* means using ritual propriety to rectify ruler and subject, father and son, noble and base, high and low, and to distinguish the proper places of men and women, husbands and wives, and brothers. The worthy little know that the sage despises those who are base, mixed, and defiled by impurity, establishing ritual propriety to distinguish the dwellings of ruler and subject, father and son, husband and wife, brothers, men and women, preventing them from mixing in filthy conduct. They do so for the sake of the world’s people, not for themselves. Hence it is said: *Little do they know that the sage scorns the impure and distinguishes proper dwellings, doing so for others, not for themselves.*

This chapter reveals that the Dao is omnipresent, and the sage benefits things without self.

**Chapter Nine**
Master Guan Yin said: In food and drink, clothing, houses, boats, and carriages, the sage is the same as the common people; in nobility and baseness, wealth and poverty, they are the same.

The sage, penetrating the wondrous Dao, deeply hidden in the world, harmonizes their light yet does not shine, moves with the dust yet is unstained. What is meant by *harmonizing their light and moving with the dust, no different from others?* It is their food and drink, clothing, houses, boats and carriages, nobility and baseness, wealth and poverty. The sage dwells in the world: when the common eat, they eat; when the common wear clothes, they wear clothes; when the common dwell in houses, they dwell in houses; when the common ride boats and carriages, they ride boats and carriages. When the common serve in office and are noble, they serve in office and are noble; when the common are commoners and base, they are commoners and base. When the common are poor and without wealth, they are poor and without wealth; when the common are rich and have wealth, they are rich and have wealth. Their traces in the world are never different from others. Hence it is said: *In food and drink, clothing, houses, boats, and carriages, the sage is the same as the common people; in nobility and baseness, wealth and poverty, they are the same.*

The common people often emulate the sage; the sage often emulates the common people. Those who look up to their loftiness and exaggerate their greatness—is it so, or is it not?

The common people’s traces are no different from the sage’s traces in the world—hence it is said: *The common people often emulate the sage.* The sage often emulates the common people. Those common people who admire the sage’s high Dao and great virtue—hence it is said: *Those who look up to their loftiness and exaggerate their greatness.*

At times, the sage establishes wondrous function—impartial and universally beneficial—without seeking gratitude in return. The common people, admiring the loftiness of the Dao and extolling the vastness of its virtue, when they witness the sage sweeping away all traces to return to the root, leaving not a speck of dust, beyond the reckoning of yin and yang, beyond the scrutiny of gods and spirits—how much more so for ordinary beings—hence it is said: *Is it so, or is it not?* This chapter clarifies the sage’s unfathomable concealment and revelation.

**Chapter Ten Concludes**

Master Guan Yin said: If a fish wishes to differ from the school of fish, abandoning the water to leap onto the shore, it dies at once. If a tiger wishes to differ from the pack of tigers, abandoning the mountain to enter the marketplace, it is captured. The fish is a metaphor for the common people; the water, a metaphor for the vast and wondrous Dao. When people delight in treading paths of unrighteousness, devising strange schemes, they violate the supreme Dao. Condemned by men and reproached by spirits, they fail to complete their allotted years and die—like a fish that, wishing to differ from the school, is deprived of water and leaps onto the shore, only to wither and perish. Hence it is said: *If a fish wishes to differ from the school of fish, abandoning the water to leap onto the shore, it dies at once.*

The tiger is a metaphor for the noble person; the mountain, a metaphor for towering supreme virtue. When the noble person pursues fame at the expense of true virtue, coveting rank and emolument, ensnared by wealth and honor without knowing retreat, they may be slandered and brought low, or tread perilous paths without meeting a good end—like a tiger that, wishing to differ from the pack, abandons the great mountain to enter the city, only to be seized and slain by men. Hence it is said: *If a tiger wishes to differ from the pack of tigers, abandoning the mountain to enter the marketplace, it is captured.* "At once" means immediately.

The sage does not differ from the common people; thus, external things cannot constrain them. The sage’s conduct is not eccentric; their traces are the same as the common people’s. They tread no path but the Dao, enact no power but virtue. Their body merges with true emptiness, unbound by the myriad conditions. Free and at ease, uniquely liberated and unshackled, they wander beyond the dust and grime, transcendent and carefree before the forms of emperors and gods. Hence it is said: *The sage does not differ from the common people; thus, external things cannot constrain them.* "Thus" means uniquely. This chapter clarifies that the sage’s conduct is not eccentric, their dwelling in the world free and unfettered.

**Chapter Eleven Concludes**

Master Guan Yin said: The Dao does not act. Those who respond to the world with the Dao engage in affairs, not the Dao. "Act" means to do; "affairs" means function. The sage embodies the Dao, acting without contrivance, naturally. Hence it is said: *The Dao does not act.* The sage employs virtue as function, arising from the essence to manifest function. Hence it is said: *Those who respond to the world with the Dao.* Arising from the essence to manifest function, their responses to change are inexhaustible. The common people, seeing the sage’s functional affairs, mistake them for the wondrous essence of the Dao. Little do they know that these are functions, not the essence. Hence it is said: *They engage in affairs, not the Dao.*

The Dao has no direction. Those who lodge the Dao in things engage with things, not the Dao. The Dao is originally formless—how could it have the eight directions, above and below, the ten quarters? "With" means to employ; "things" means people; "lodge" means entrusting to wondrous words to instruct people. The common people, seeing the sage’s profound and wondrous principles, mistake them for the Dao. Little do they know that these are traces of instruction, not the Dao itself. Hence it is said: *The Dao has no direction. Those who lodge the Dao in things engage with things, not the Dao.*

The sage ultimately cannot bring forth the Dao to show others. "Ultimately" means in the end; "show" means to make clear. The Dao is true emptiness—formless, without appearance, without likeness, without comparison. The mind cannot fathom it through thought, the mouth cannot discuss it through speech. It is realized only when the heart awakens to its secret accord. Though the sage may do so, they ultimately cannot grasp the wondrous Dao to enlighten disciples. Hence it is said: *The sage ultimately cannot bring forth the Dao to show others.* This chapter clarifies that the Dao is realized through the heart’s awakening; even the sage cannot convey it to others.

**Chapter Twelve Concludes**

Master Guan Yin said: Like a bell sounding when struck, like a drum resounding when beaten—such are the sage’s words. Bells and drums do not sound themselves; they respond when touched, their resonance without intent. This illustrates how the sage responds only when moved, their words without premeditation. The bell’s sound is clear, illustrating how the sage, moved to respond, instructs people with principle, guiding them to awaken through reason. Hence it is said: *Like a bell sounding when struck.* The drum’s sound is deep, illustrating how the sage, moved to respond, instructs people with affairs, guiding them to awaken through practice. Hence it is said: *Like a drum resounding when beaten—such are the sage’s words.*

Like a cart moving when drawn, like a boat gliding when poled—such are the sage’s actions. Carts and boats do not move themselves; they proceed only when impelled, their motion without intent. This illustrates how the sage acts only when compelled, proceeding only when unavoidable, their actions without premeditation. The cart’s movement leaves traces, illustrating how the sage at times comforts the world, at times assists it, universally benefiting all under heaven. Their traces are luminous, enduring through the ages without fading. Hence it is said: *Like a cart moving when drawn.* The boat’s movement leaves no trace, illustrating how the sage, compelled to respond to the world, enacts virtue. The highest virtue does not claim virtue, leaving traces that are no traces, confounding the wise and foolish alike, beyond the ken of gods and spirits. Hence it is said: *Like a boat gliding when poled—such are the sage’s actions.*

Only when none can name it does it silence the world’s words. Only when none can know it does it strip the world’s wisdom. The Dao is originally nameless; it is forcibly called the Dao. When the forced name is dissolved, the Dao too ceases to stand. Hence it is said: *Only when none can name it.* When none can name it, words have no ground for discussion. Even the world’s most capable, confronted with this, fall silent in retreat. Hence it is said: *It silences the world’s words.* The Dao is originally without inclination; contemplation cannot grasp it to know it. When contemplation cannot grasp it to know it, even the world’s greatest sages, confronted with this, are rendered powerless. Hence it is said: *Only when none can know it does it strip the world’s wisdom.*

This chapter elucidates that the sage’s words and actions are without deliberate intent, and the Dao is beyond contemplation and discussion.

**Chapter Thirteen**
Master Guan Yin said: The centipede eats the snake, the snake eats the toad, and the toad eats the centipede—each devours the other. The "centipede" here refers to the *wugong*; the "toad," to the *hama*. The centipede enters the snake’s nostrils to consume its brain and marrow; the snake swallows the toad, and the toad swallows the centipede. These three creatures mutually consume one another, hence the saying: *The centipede eats the snake, the snake eats the toad, the toad eats the centipede*—serving as an analogy.

The sage’s words are likewise: they speak of the flaws of "being and non-being," then of the flaws of "neither being nor non-being," and then of the flaws of abandoning "neither being nor non-being." The named teachings and dharmic appearances left by the sage resemble the centipede, snake, and toad devouring one another. Thus it is said: *The sage’s words are likewise.* The sage’s words encompass both "being" and "non-being." Those who are deluded cling to these two extremes, which becomes the flaw in cultivating the Dao. This aligns with the analogy of *the centipede eating the snake*, hence the saying: *they speak of the flaws of "being and non-being."*

Once one recognizes that the two extremes of "being and non-being" are erroneous, one neither clings to being nor to non-being, and only practices the Middle Way. Yet they then speak of the flaws of "neither being nor non-being." Once one recognizes that the Middle Way of "neither being nor non-being" is also a flaw, one discards "neither being nor non-being" and does not establish either the two extremes or the Middle Way. The deluded, failing to awaken, cling to these words as if they were the Dao itself—this, too, is a flaw in cultivating the Dao. This corresponds to the analogy of *the snake eating the toad, the toad eating the centipede*, hence the saying: *they speak of the flaws of abandoning "neither being nor non-being."*

Words are like drawing a saw: the previous statements on "being and non-being," "neither being nor non-being," and abandoning "neither being nor non-being" all reveal flaws. These words mutually erode one another, like pulling a saw back and forth, deepening the traces. Thus it is said: *Words are like drawing a saw.*

Only those who are truly adept do not cling to a single word. "Clinging" means attachment. The sage’s words are like the fish trap and rabbit snare: the trap is the means to catch fish, but once the fish is caught, the trap is forgotten; the snare is the means to catch the rabbit, but once the rabbit is caught, the snare is forgotten. Words are the means to convey meaning, but once the meaning is grasped, the words are forgotten. To "forget words" is to not cling to them. Only those who profoundly awaken to the sage’s Dao do not cling to words, rendering them ineffable and beyond mutual erosion. Thus it is said: *Only those who are truly adept do not cling to a single word.*

This chapter clarifies that the sage’s Dao does not belong to names and words, and any attempt to conceive or discuss it becomes a flaw.

**Chapter Fourteen**

Master Guan Yin said: Be it dragon or flood dragon, serpent or tortoise, fish or clam—all these the dragon can become. The dragon as a creature rides the clouds and qi, nurtures itself in yin and yang, gathers to form shape, disperses to form pattern, its transformations unfathomable. It can be small or great: when great, it fills heaven and earth; when small, it becomes flood dragons, serpents, tortoises, fish, or clams of every kind. Thus it is said: be it dragon or flood dragon, serpent or tortoise, fish or clam—all these the dragon can become. This is a borrowed analogy.

The flood dragon is but a flood dragon—it cannot become a dragon, nor serpent, tortoise, fish, or clam. The flood dragon resembles the dragon but is not the dragon; yet it may ultimately transform into one. Before its transformation, it is merely a water beast, hence it is called *flood dragon*—nothing more. As a water beast, it cannot change its form; how then could it become dragon, serpent, tortoise, fish, or clam? Thus it is said: it cannot become a dragon, nor serpent, tortoise, fish, or clam. This too is a borrowed analogy.

The sage is like the dragon; the worthy, like the flood dragon. Water is an analogy for the Dao; the dragon, for the sage; the flood dragon, for the worthy; the serpent and tortoise, for the noble person; the fish and clam, for the common people. Dragon, flood dragon, serpent, tortoise, fish, and clam—all dwell in water, from birth to old age, inhaling and exhaling, partaking of its bounty, each lacking nothing. Yet while flood dragon, serpent, tortoise, fish, and clam all live in water, they cannot transform within it—only the dragon can. Sage, worthy, noble person, and common people all live within the Dao, from birth to old age, their daily needs all met, each lacking nothing. But while the worthy, noble person, and common people live within the Dao, they do not comprehend it—only the sage comprehends it. Thus it is said: the sage is like the dragon; the worthy, like the flood dragon. This is a combined analogy.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao resides in the sage’s subtlety, complete and without lack—others do not comprehend it, but the sage does.

---

Master Guan Yin said: Abiding nowhere within oneself, the forms of things manifest of themselves. In movement, it is like water; in stillness, like a mirror; in response, like an echo.

Only the true and constant abides within, hence it is said *abiding within oneself*. Not clinging to the appearance of emptiness, hence *abiding nowhere*. True emptiness is perfectly luminous, and every thing becomes manifest—thus it is said *the forms of things manifest of themselves*. *Manifest* means clearly revealed. True emptiness stands alone, unclinging to the appearance of emptiness; every particular is distinctly seen, every thing clearly revealed. In movement, it is like water, flowing yet leaving no trace; in stillness, like a bright mirror, reflecting forms without intent; in responding to things, like an echo in an empty valley, ever silent. Thus it is said: *Abiding nowhere within oneself, the forms of things manifest of themselves. In movement, it is like water; in stillness, like a mirror; in response, like an echo.*

Vague, as if vanished; silent, as if pure. The sage, having thoroughly comprehended true emptiness, has a mind like cold ashes or withered wood, seemingly devoid of vitality—thus it is said *vague, as if vanished*. *Vague* means dim and obscure, like cold ashes without light; *vanished*, like withered wood without vitality. The source of the mind is perfectly clear and still, pure as water in its tranquility—thus it is said *silent, as if pure*. *Silent* means still.

Those who harmonize are in accord; those who grasp lose. Those of keen capacity, drawn by shared qi, study under the sage. The sage subtly demonstrates to them, and they immediately comprehend, transforming from ordinary to sage—thus it is said *those who harmonize*. The former sage initiates, and the later sage responds—thus it is said *those who harmonize are in accord*. Those of dull capacity, not yet awakened to the Dao, grasp at the sage’s words and dharmic forms, believing they have gained something. With such views, they do not forget—and thus they lose by a great margin. Thus it is said: *those who grasp lose.*

Never preceding others, yet always following them. The sage, dwelling in the world, responds only when moved, compelled to establish words and dharmic forms for those of middling and lesser capacity—all expedient means. Yet the sage never first sets forth these words, for those of middling and lesser capacity become attached and deluded. Only then does the sage, according to their portion, loosen their bonds, prescribing remedies according to their ailments. Thus it is said: *Never preceding others, yet always following them.* This chapter clarifies that the sage’s movement and stillness are without intent, responding only when moved.

---

Master Guan Yin said: Vast and boundless, roaming the Great Beginning! The great Dao is undifferentiated and boundless as the ocean—this is the sage’s essence. Thus it is said: *Vast and boundless.* *Great Ease* is the state before qi is seen; *Great Beginning* is the inception of qi. When the singular qi first stirs, it is the functioning of the Dao—the sage embodies both essence and function, roaming without trace. Thus it is said: *Roaming the Great Beginning!*

Now gold, now jade; now dung, now earth. The sage, dwelling in the world, does not oppose the times. When the time is right to assist the world and govern the realm, people regard it as precious as gold—thus it is said *now gold.* When the time is right to comfort the world and pacify the realm, people regard it as noble as jade—thus it is said *now jade.* When the time is right to withdraw and live in obscurity, humble and poor, benefiting others and aiding the people, they are scorned as lowly as dung or earth—thus it is said *now dung, now earth.*

Now soaring above things, now following them. When the time is right for the sage to cultivate alone, they roam freely, like the phoenix soaring through the clouds, leaving no trace—thus it is said *now soaring above things.* When the time is right to follow the multitude, they conceal their spiritual powers—thus it is said *now following them.* *Following* means accompanying.

Now mountain-dwelling, now abiding in the depths. When the time is right, the sage may dwell in the mountains, drinking from streams, sleeping beneath the moon and clouds, concealing their brilliance and finding joy. Or they may take a solitary boat with a short oar, clad in straw raincoat and bamboo hat, wandering rivers and lakes—unfit to be the emperor’s subject, nor the feudal lord’s friend—freely roaming without constraint. Thus it is said: *Now mountain-dwelling, now abiding in the depths.*

Upright or adaptive, mad or foolish?
*Upright or adaptive* explains the earlier *now metal-aligned, now jade-aligned*—speaking of aiding and governing the world with upright principles and adaptive governance to bring peace and comfort to all under heaven. Thus it is said: *upright or adaptive.* *Mad or foolish* explains the earlier *now dung-aligned, now earth-aligned; now soaring above things, now following them; now mountain-dwelling, now abiding in the depths*—meaning that the sage sometimes acts with unrestrained self-mastery, unbound by rules, elusive in concealment or revelation, appearing mad or foolish, beyond all conjecture. Thus it is said: *mad or foolish.* This chapter clarifies how the sage adapts to the times, eluding all prediction in concealment or revelation.

**Chapter Seventeen**

Master Guan Yin says: One skilled in the zither, when moved by sorrow, produces sounds that are mournful; when lost in thought, sounds that are slow and measured; when filled with resentment, sounds that are winding and bitter; when gripped by longing, sounds that are lingering and tender. *Mournful* describes the voice of grief; *slow and measured*, the voice of contemplation; *winding and bitter*, the voice of resentment; *lingering and tender*, the voice of yearning. The heart’s intent is lodged in the zither, manifesting through its tones. When the heart harbors sorrow, the zither’s voice becomes mournful and stirring—thus it is said: *one skilled in the zither, when moved by sorrow, produces sounds that are mournful.* When the heart is lost in thought, the zither’s voice becomes slow and measured, as if pondering—thus it is said: *when lost in thought, sounds that are slow and measured.* When the heart resents another, the voice becomes winding and bitter with grievance—thus it is said: *when filled with resentment, sounds that are winding and bitter.* When the heart longs for something, the voice becomes lingering and tender with attachment—thus it is said: *when gripped by longing, sounds that are lingering and tender.*

What is called *sorrow, thought, resentment, longing* arises neither from the hands nor the bamboo, neither from the strings nor the paulownia wood. It is grasped by the heart, matched by the hands; grasped by the hands, matched by the instrument. Those who embody the Dao never miss the mark. The aforementioned *sorrow, thought, resentment, longing*—these tones of mourning, slowness, bitterness, and tenderness do not arise from the hands, bamboo, strings, or zither itself. Thus it is said: *what is called sorrow, thought, resentment, longing arises neither from the hands nor the bamboo, neither from the strings nor the paulownia wood.* When one skilled in the zither internalizes it, the heart’s intent aligns with the hands, and the hands’ movements align with the strings, bamboo, and paulownia wood. Naturally, the heart’s intent manifests through the sound. If even one skilled in the zither can thus reveal the heart’s intent through sound, how much more so the adept who awakens to the Dao! The adept’s essence and function are ever-present; whether grasping or releasing, speaking or silent, rising or resting, all is in perfect accord with the Dao. Thus it is said: *grasped by the heart, matched by the hands; grasped by the hands, matched by the instrument. Those who embody the Dao never miss the mark.* This chapter shows that in movement and stillness, speech and silence, all is the Dao.

**Chapter Eighteen**

Master Guan Yin says: The sage employs speech, action, and thought to align with others. *Speech, action, and thought* are all means of engagement.
