The sage’s traces of response may at times differ little from those of ordinary people—thus it is said: *the sage employs speech, action, and thought to align with others.* Yet the sage also embodies what has never been spoken, never been done, never been thought, and this sets them apart from others. *Never* means *not once*; *what has never been spoken, done, or thought* refers to the unconditioned essence prior to the arising of speech, action, or thought. The sage’s unconditioned essence is fundamentally no different from that of ordinary people, but because the many are deluded while the sage is awakened, they differ from the many. Thus it is said: *the sage embodies what has never been spoken, never been done, never been thought, and this sets them apart from others.* This chapter clarifies how the deluded share the sage’s traces but not the sage’s Dao.

**Chapter Nineteen**

Master Guan Yin says: The sharper the mind’s discernment of profit and harm, the less harmony among kin; the sharper the mind’s distinction between worthy and foolish, the fewer bonds of friendship; the sharper the mind’s judgment of right and wrong, the less success in affairs; the sharper the mind’s preference for beauty and ugliness, the less resonance with things. When the mind is fixated on discerning profit, it pursues it single-mindedly; when fixated on discerning harm, it avoids it single-mindedly. In this way, even parents and children cannot live in harmony—how much less others? Thus it is said: *the sharper the mind’s discernment of profit and harm, the less harmony among kin.* When the mind is fixated on distinguishing the worthy from the foolish, it clings to the worthy and distances the foolish. Yet if another is wiser than oneself, they will surely regard one as foolish and distance themselves in turn. Thus, even in old age, one cannot form bonds with like-minded friends. Thus it is said: *the sharper the mind’s distinction between worthy and foolish, the fewer bonds of friendship.* When the mind is fixated on judging right and wrong, it clings to right and rejects wrong, becoming ensnared by the labels of right and wrong. No matter how trivial or grand the matter, one dares not act. Thus, no affair, great or small, can be accomplished. Thus it is said: *the sharper the mind’s judgment of right and wrong, the less success in affairs.* When the mind is fixated on preferring beauty and rejecting ugliness, love and aversion cloud the heart, and one becomes obsessed with appearances. Even the bond between husband and wife cannot find resonance. Thus it is said: *the sharper the mind’s preference for beauty and ugliness, the less resonance with things.* *Sharper* means *fixated*; *things* refers to all beings.

Therefore, the sage blends them all.

Thus, *shi yi* serves as a phrase linking the preceding and following. *Hun* means the undifferentiated true heart, free from discriminations. Therefore, the sage’s true heart remains undifferentiated, free from discriminations, and thus transcends the labels of profit and harm, worthiness and folly, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness. Hence it is said: *Therefore, the sage blends them all.*

This chapter clarifies that the sage’s true heart is undifferentiated and free from discriminations.

**Chapter Twenty-One**
Master Guan Yin said: The foolish and clumsy of the world falsely invoke the sage’s foolishness and clumsiness to justify themselves. *Yuan* means *to cite*. Among the people of the world, there are those who are dull, clumsy, and slow-witted, fearing ridicule, and so they falsely invoke the sage’s traces of concealment, feigned madness, and clumsiness to explain themselves, claiming that the sage, too, possessed such foolishness and clumsiness. Hence it is said: *The foolish and clumsy of the world falsely invoke the sage’s foolishness and clumsiness to justify themselves.*

Yet they fail to realize that the sage is at times foolish, at times enlightened, at times clumsy, at times skillful. The foolish and clumsy of the world do not understand that the sage adapts to circumstances and responds to change, never bound by traces. When concealment is fitting, the sage feigns madness like a fool—this is the height of concealment. When revelation is fitting, the sage’s name shines brighter than the sun and moon. When non-action is fitting, the sage forgets ability and appears clumsy. When action is fitting, the sage employs skillful means to guide the deluded. Hence it is said: *Yet they fail to realize that the sage is at times foolish, at times enlightened, at times clumsy, at times skillful.*

This chapter clarifies that the sage’s concealment and revelation are unfathomable.

**Chapter Twenty-Two**
Master Guan Yin said: Those who model themselves on the sage with sagely wisdom are worthy persons; those who model themselves on the sage with worthy wisdom become sages. Those who, with sagely wisdom, emulate the sage’s virtue reach its utmost and become worthy persons. Hence it is said: *Those who model themselves on the sage with sagely wisdom are worthy persons.* Those who, with the heart of a worthy person, emulate the sage’s Way reach its utmost and become sages. Hence it is said: *Those who model themselves on the sage with worthy wisdom become sages.*

For those who model themselves on the sage with sagely wisdom cling to traces and forget the Way, while those who model themselves on the sage with worthy wisdom reverse traces and unite with the Way. Virtue and its application leave traces. Those who, with sagely wisdom, emulate the sage’s virtue and its application cling to the traces of its application and forget the substance of the Way. Hence it is said: *For those who model themselves on the sage with sagely wisdom cling to traces and forget the Way.* The substance of the Way is true emptiness, leaving no traces to cling to. Those who, with the heart of a worthy person, emulate the sage’s Way awaken understanding, their hearts merging with true emptiness, reversing traces to unite with the Way. Hence it is said: *Those who model themselves on the sage with worthy wisdom reverse traces and unite with the Way.*

This chapter clarifies that descending from the root to traces makes one a worthy person, while returning from traces to the root makes one a sage.

**Chapter Twenty-Three**
Master Guan Yin said: The worthy incline upward and do not see below; the common people incline downward and do not see above. The sage encompasses both above and below, acting as is fitting. The worthy understand principle—what is above form is called the Dao, and what is below form is called the instrument—and fixate solely on principle, unaware that attachment to principle is but one side. Hence it is said: *The worthy incline upward and do not see below.* The common people are benighted to principle and infatuated with affairs, unaware that attachment to affairs is also but one side. Hence it is said: *The common people incline downward and do not see above.*

*Above* refers to principle; *below* refers to affairs. The sage, penetrating true emptiness, is unbound by affairs or principle. At times, the sage teaches through principle; at other times, through affairs. Observing the moment, the sage instructs, adapting to circumstances with perfect appropriateness. Hence it is said: *The sage encompasses both above and below, acting as is fitting.*

Is it said that apart from the worthy and the common people, there exists another kind of sage? The sage is like water; the worthy and common people are like ice. Water congeals into ice when cold, and ice melts back into water when warm. Though the ice has not yet melted, one cannot say the ice is not water, for the ice is wholly water. When the sage is deluded, they become an ordinary person; when they awaken, they become a sage again. Though they have not yet awakened, one cannot say the ordinary person is not a sage, for the ordinary person is wholly the sage. How tragic for those who remain deluded!

Thus, to know water, do not depart from ice—seek it, and ice is water. To awaken to the sage’s Way, do not depart from the ordinary—investigate it, and the ordinary is the sage. Hence it is said: *Is it said that apart from the worthy and the common people, there exists another kind of sage?* *Zai* is an exclamation of deep lament for the deluded.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao resides in the sage and the ordinary person alike, equally complete, differing only in delusion and awakening.

**Chapter Twenty-Four**
Master Guan Yin said: In the principles of the world, the husband leads, and the wife follows; the male runs, and the female pursues; the rooster crows, and the hen responds. In the world, there are natural principles: yang is honored, yin is humble; yang is firm, yin is yielding; yang precedes, yin follows. Thus, the husband leads, and the wife follows in harmony. Among beasts, the male runs first, and the female pursues. Among birds, the rooster crows from the windward, and the hen responds from the leeward. Hence it is said: *In the principles of the world, the husband leads, and the wife follows; the male runs, and the female pursues; the rooster crows, and the hen responds.*

Therefore, the sage establishes words and deeds, and the worthy adhere to them. Observing the world’s natural principles of yin and yang, honor and humility, precedence and sequence, the sage establishes the words and deeds of ruler and minister, father and child, husband and wife, elder and younger, their honor and humility, precedence and sequence. The worthy of past and present dare not transgress or deviate from them, bound by these words and deeds, unable to act with freedom and ease. Hence it is said: *Therefore, the sage establishes words and deeds, and the worthy adhere to them.*

This chapter clarifies that the sage’s Way transcends norms yet sets an example for later generations.

**Chapter Twenty-Five**
Master Guan Yin said: Though the sage’s Way transforms like a tiger, in affairs, it moves like a turtle. Though the Way is as fine as silk, in affairs, it is as coarse as sackcloth.

**Chapter Twenty-Five**
The "tiger’s transformation" illustrates the awe-inspiring radiance of the sage’s Way, responding to change without fixed form. The "turtle’s movement" illustrates the sage’s handling of affairs—reluctantly and with measured delay—hence it is said: *Though the sage’s Way transforms like a tiger, in affairs, it moves like a turtle.*

The "silken threads" illustrate the sage’s Way, intricate and all-encompassing. The "sackcloth" illustrates the sage’s handling of affairs, each step grounded in principle and reason—hence it is said: *Though the Way is as fine as silk, in affairs, it is as coarse as sackcloth.*

This chapter clarifies that the sage’s Way is unfathomable, yet in action, it follows discernible patterns.

**Chapter Twenty-Six**
Master Guan Yin said: The so-called Way of the sage—how solitary it is! How perfect it is! How profound it is! How wondrous it is!

"Solitary" means without counterpart. "Perfect" means utterly good and beautiful. "Profound" means deep and unfathomable. "Wondrous" means marvel upon marvel. "How" asks: *In what way?*

The Way of the sage—how solitary it stands, transcending all dualities! Hence it is said: *The so-called Way of the sage—how solitary it is!*

The Way of the sage—how perfect in its utter goodness and beauty! How profound in its unfathomable depth! How wondrous in its boundless marvels, beyond all praise! Hence it is said: *How perfect it is! How profound it is! How wondrous it is!*

Only because it pervades and pairs with the myriad things, yet no single thing can pair with it, does it hold all things in honor.

"Pair" means to depend upon. Only the Way, being without self, gives birth to the myriad things yet remains undifferentiated from them. All things depend upon the Way to exist, yet the Way depends upon nothing. Thus it transcends and honors all things. Hence it is said: *Only because it pervades and pairs with the myriad things, yet no single thing can pair with it, does it hold all things in honor.*

"No single thing can pair with it" means no single thing can compare to the Way.

This chapter clarifies that the Way transcends all existence, utterly beyond all dualities.

**Chapter Twenty-Seven**
Master Guan Yin said: The curling and unfurling of clouds, the flight of birds—all occur within the empty void, and thus their transformations are inexhaustible.

Clouds arise when the qi of earth and water ascends and coalesces. Within the empty void, they curl or unfurl, gathering and dispersing, coming and going without trace.

Birds, born of yang qi, soar through the empty void, north or south, east or west, wherever their will inclines. Their comings and goings, their free movement—no trace can be found.

Clouds and birds alike dwell within the empty void, curling and unfurling, flying and soaring, their transformations endless. Hence it is said: *The curling and unfurling of clouds, the flight of birds—all occur within the empty void, and thus their transformations are inexhaustible.*

This is a metaphor.

So too is the sage’s Way.

The sage’s Way, penetrating the true emptiness and wondrous existence, embodies both substance and function with perfect equanimity. Responding to change without fixed form, it is like the curling and unfurling of clouds, the flight of birds—free and boundless in its transformations, without end, yet leaving no trace to follow. Hence it is said: *So too is the sage’s Way.*

This chapter clarifies that the sage’s Way is inexhaustible in its transformations, now hidden, now manifest, beyond all reckoning.

**End of *Wenshi Zhenjing Zhu*, Scroll Three**

#1 This passage appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing* as: *"No thing under heaven can burden it, and so it is rooted in humility."*

#2 The character *wu* (無) was originally omitted in printing and has been restored here based on the context before and after.

#3 This passage appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing* as: *"Observing the Dao, heaven and earth, and all beings are but my Dao."*

#4 *"Does not receive the Dao"* (不受道) appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing* as *"does not cherish the Dao"* (不愛道).

#5 This passage appears in the *Master Guan Yin* as *"This is called the mutability of things"* (是為物易也), while in the *Wenshi Zhenjing* it reads: *"This is called one who alters things"* (是為物易者也).

#6 This passage appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing* as: *"Nor can it become a snake or a tortoise."*

#7 *"Nine"* (九) should be an error for *"self"* (自).

#8 This passage appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing* as: *"Only because it can pair with the myriad things."*

**End of *Wenshi Zhenjing Zhu*, Scroll Four**
*Divine Peak’s Free and Easy Master Niu Daochun’s Direct Explication*

**Chapter on the Four Symbols**

The *Symbols* refer to essence, spirit, soul, and spirit—comprising seventeen sections in all.

Master Guan Yin said: *"Water may be divided or united. Essence has no person."*
Among the Five Phases, water divides into the myriad waters yet unites as one water, manifesting without reliance on other things—thus we know water has no person. In the human body, water pertains to the kidney’s stored essence; hence, essence has no person. Therefore it is said: *"Water may be divided or united; essence has no person."*

*"Fire depends on oil or fuel. Spirit has no self."*
Among the Five Phases, fire arises from lamp oil, wicks, or burning wood, revealing its form. Without oil or fuel, fire cannot manifest alone—thus we know fire has no self. In the human body, fire pertains to the heart’s stored spirit; hence, spirit has no self. Therefore it is said: *"Fire depends on oil or fuel; spirit has no self."*

Thus:
*"The ear, though obstructed, hears before and behind—no person."*
*"Wisdom, lofty—no person."*
*"The singular, odd—no person."*
*"Winter withers autumn’s things—no person."*
*"Black cannot be altered—no person."*
*"The North endures—no person."*
All these pertain to essence.

Since water has no person, wherever it resides, there is no person. In the viscera, water is the kidney; in form, it is the ear—the ear being the kidney’s external manifestation. Though obstructed, it hears sounds before and behind; as the ear belongs to water, it too has no person. Hence: *"The ear, though obstructed, hears before and behind—no person."*

Among the Five Constants, water corresponds to wisdom. Wisdom’s solitary eminence is called *loftiness*. As wisdom belongs to water, it has no person. Hence: *"Wisdom, lofty—no person."*

In numbers, water is one—the singular, odd number, water’s generative number. As one belongs to water, it too has no person. Hence: *"The singular, odd—no person."*

In seasons, water is winter. Winter’s severity withers autumn’s grasses and trees. As winter belongs to water, it too has no person. Hence: *"Winter withers autumn’s things—no person."*

In colors, water is black. Black cannot be changed into another color. As black belongs to water, it too has no person. Hence: *"Black cannot be altered—no person."*

In directions, water is the North. Water’s longevity never dries up; thus we know those who preserve essence live long. As the North belongs to water, it too has no person. Hence: *"The North endures—no person."*

The above six—ear, wisdom, singular, winter, black, and North—all pertain to water. Among the Five Spirits, water governs essence; hence it is said: *"All these are essence."*

*"The tongue, with teeth, forms speech—no self."*
*"Ritual, humble—no self."*
*"The dual, even—no self."*
*"Summer relies on spring’s things—no self."*
*"Red can be altered—no self."*
*"The South is short-lived—no self."*
All these pertain to spirit.

Since fire has no self, wherever it resides, there is no self. In the viscera, fire is the heart; in form, it is the tongue—the tongue being the heart’s external manifestation. The tongue alone cannot speak; speech arises through the teeth. As the tongue belongs to fire, it too has no self. Hence: *"The tongue, with teeth, forms speech—no self."*

Among the Five Constants, fire corresponds to ritual. Ritual’s foundation is humility. As ritual belongs to fire, it too has no self. Hence: *"Ritual, humble—no self."*

In numbers, fire is two—the dual, even number, fire’s generative number. As two belongs to fire, it too has no self. Hence: *"The dual, even—no self."*

In seasons, fire is summer. Summer relies on spring’s grasses and trees to grow them. As summer belongs to fire, it too has no self. Hence: *"Summer relies on spring’s things—no self."*

In colors, fire is red. Red can be altered into another color. As red belongs to fire, it too has no self. Hence: *"Red can be altered—no self."*

In directions, fire is the South. Fire is easily extinguished, hence short-lived; thus we know those who indulge in true fire die young. As the South belongs to fire, it too has no self. Hence: *"The South is short-lived—no self."*

The above six—tongue, ritual, dual, summer, red, and South—all pertain to fire. Among the Five Spirits, fire governs spirit; hence it is said: *"All these are spirit."*

*"As essence has no person, when rice is husked from grain, essence remains."*
Among the Five Spirits, water governs essence. Since water has no person, essence too has no person. As essence has no person, it is like grain husked to reveal pure rice—this is the image of no-person. Hence: *"As essence has no person, when rice is husked from grain, essence remains."*

*"As spirit has no self, when ghosts rely on things, spirit manifests."*
Among the Five Spirits, fire governs spirit. Since fire has no self, spirit too has no self. As spirit has no self, it is like ghosts relying on people or objects to reveal their numinous power—this is the image of no-self. Hence: *"As spirit has no self, when ghosts rely on things, spirit manifests."*

*"Those who preserve essence forget right and wrong, forget gain and loss. What is here is not there."*
One who preserves perfect essence stands alone, unpaired with the myriad dharmas, forgetting good and evil, honor and disgrace, gain and loss. Standing alone, apart from the myriad dharmas, essence exists here, independent of other things. Hence: *"Those who preserve essence forget right and wrong, forget gain and loss. What is here is not there."*

*"Those who embrace spirit alternate between obscurity and clarity, strength and weakness. What is there is not here."*
One who embraces perfect spirit, selfless and obscure, adapts to the times. When obscurity is fitting, they withdraw and cultivate alone; when clarity is fitting, they emerge to benefit the world. When strength and unceasing effort are fitting, they practice virtue and persevere; when humility and softness are fitting, they restrain themselves. Obscurity and clarity, strength and weakness depend on the times, not on this spirit. As spirit has no self, hence: *"Those who embrace spirit alternate between obscurity and clarity, strength and weakness. What is here is not there."*

This chapter clarifies that those who preserve essence and spirit have no self or person.

**End of Chapter One**

Master Guan Yin said: Spirit and essence are water and fire, mutually generated and extinguished by the Five Phases. Their coming has no beginning, their going has no end.

Essence pertains to water, spirit pertains to fire—thus spirit and essence are water and fire. Water generates wood, wood generates fire, fire generates earth, earth generates metal, metal generates water; water extinguishes fire, fire extinguishes metal, metal extinguishes wood, wood extinguishes earth, earth extinguishes water—thus it is said the Five Phases mutually generate and extinguish one another. In the first and second months, wood flourishes; in the third month, wood flourishes for twelve days, earth for eighteen days. In the fourth and fifth months, fire flourishes; in the sixth month, fire flourishes for twelve days, earth for eighteen days. In the seventh and eighth months, metal flourishes; in the ninth month, metal flourishes for twelve days, earth for eighteen days. In the tenth and eleventh months, water flourishes; in the twelfth month, water flourishes for twelve days, earth for eighteen days. The four seasons of spring, summer, autumn, and winter cycle endlessly, their beginning and end traceable—thus it is said their coming has no beginning, their going has no end.

Then my essence, a single drop, neither exists nor perishes. Essence pertains to water—hence it is called a single drop. The Way of my perfect essence does not pertain to existence or nonexistence, presence or absence—thus it is said: *Then my essence, a single drop, neither exists nor perishes.*

My spirit, a single spark, neither arises nor ceases. Spirit pertains to fire—hence it is called a single spark. My perfect spirit, in that single spark, is neither born nor destroyed. Arising is birth—thus it is said: *My spirit, a single spark, neither arises nor ceases.*

Only with no self, no person, no beginning, no end does one merge with heaven and earth. As stated above: spirit has no self, essence has no person; the Five Phases mutually generate and extinguish them, their coming has no beginning, their going has no end. Thus we know there is no self or person. Beginning and end are what unite with heaven and earth in the profound and obscure Way—thus it is said: *Only with no self, no person, no beginning, no end does one merge with heaven and earth.*

This chapter clarifies that spirit and essence have no self or person, and together with heaven and earth, arise from the Dao.

**End of Chapter Two**

Master Guan Yin said: Essence is water, po is metal, spirit is fire, hun is wood. Among the five spirits, water is essence—thus it is said *essence is water.* Among the five spirits, metal is po—thus it is said *po is metal.* Among the five spirits, fire is spirit—thus it is said *spirit is fire.* Among the five spirits, wood is hun—thus it is said *hun is wood.*

Essence governs water, po governs metal; metal generates water—thus essence is stored within po. Essence pertains to water, po pertains to metal; metal is the mother of water, metal generates water. From this we know po conceals essence—thus it is said: *Essence governs water, po governs metal; metal generates water—thus essence is stored within po.*

Spirit governs fire, hun governs wood; wood generates fire—thus spirit is stored within hun. Spirit pertains to fire, hun pertains to wood; wood is the mother of fire, wood generates fire. From this we know hun conceals spirit—thus it is said: *Spirit governs fire, hun governs wood; wood generates fire—thus spirit is stored within hun.*

Only water as an element can conceal metal and still it, can nourish wood and make it flourish—thus it separates hun and po. The Xixuan edition includes these four lines, which seem to follow the flow of the text without disrupting its order—thus they are also explained here. When metal is placed in water, it hides and becomes still; when wood receives water, it is nourished and flourishes. Since essence pertains to water, po to metal, and hun to wood, and water conceals metal, we know essence can separate hun and po—thus it is said: *Only water as an element can conceal metal and still it, can nourish wood and make it flourish—thus it separates hun and po.*

Only fire as an element can melt metal and dissolve it, can burn wood and consume it—thus it unites hun and po. Fire can melt and dissolve metal, and can also burn and consume wood. Since spirit pertains to fire, po to metal, and hun to wood, and fire can dissolve metal and burn wood, we know spirit can unite hun and po—thus it is said: *Only fire as an element can melt metal and dissolve it, can burn wood and consume it—thus it unites hun and po.*

Only essence, in heaven, is cold; on earth, is water; in humans, is essence. Essence pertains to water; when water is in heaven, it becomes winter’s cold; when water is in earthly form, it is the water of the Five Phases; when water is among the five spirits of humans, it is essence—thus it is said: *Only essence, in heaven, is cold; on earth, is water; in humans, is essence.*

Spirit, in heaven, is heat; on earth, is fire; in humans, is spirit. Spirit pertains to fire; when in heaven, it becomes summer’s heat; when in earthly form, it is the fire of the Five Phases; among the five spirits of humans, it is spirit—thus it is said: *Spirit, in heaven, is heat; on earth, is fire; in humans, is spirit.*

Po, in heaven, is dryness; on earth, is metal; in humans, is po. Po pertains to metal; in heavenly qi, it becomes scorching dryness; in earthly form, it is the metal of the Five Phases; among the five spirits of humans, it is po—thus it is said: *Po, in heaven, is dryness; on earth, is metal; in humans, is po.*

Hun, in heaven, is wind; on earth, is wood; in humans, is hun. Hun pertains to wood; in heavenly qi, it becomes wind; in earthly form, it is the wood of the Five Phases; among the five spirits of humans, it is hun—thus it is said: *Hun, in heaven, is wind; on earth, is wood; in humans, is hun.*

Only by uniting my essence with the essence of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as the myriad waters can merge into one water. Using the analogy of myriad waters merging into one water, we know my essence can unite with the essence of heaven, earth, and the myriad things into one essence—thus it is said: *Only by uniting my essence with the essence of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as the myriad waters can merge into one water.*

Just as my spirit can unite with the spirit of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as the myriad fires can merge into one fire. Using the analogy of myriad fires merging into one fire, we know my spirit can unite with the spirit of heaven, earth, and the myriad things into one spirit—thus it is said: *Just as my spirit can unite with the spirit of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as the myriad fires can merge into one fire.*

Just as my po can unite with the po of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as metal, as a substance, can fuse different metals into one. Using the analogy of different metals fusing into one, we know my po can unite with the po of heaven, earth, and the myriad things into one po—thus it is said: *Just as my po can unite with the po of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as metal, as a substance, can fuse different metals into one.*

Just as my hun can unite with the hun of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as wood, as a substance, can graft different woods to grow into one. Using the analogy of different woods grafting into one, we know my hun can unite with the hun of heaven, earth, and the myriad things into one hun—thus it is said: *Just as my hun can unite with the hun of heaven, earth, and the myriad things—just as wood, as a substance, can graft different woods to grow into one.*

Then heaven, earth, and the myriad things are all my essence, my spirit, my po, my hun—what dies, and what lives? As stated above, the essence, spirit, po, and hun of heaven, earth, and the myriad things all arise together from the Dao. Since they arise together from the Dao, how could the Dao have life and death? Thus it is said: *Then heaven, earth, and the myriad things are all my essence, my spirit, my po, my hun—what dies, and what lives?* This chapter clarifies that heaven and earth are born together with me, and the myriad things and I are one—one born from the Dao, and the Dao has no life or death.

---

**Chapter Three**

Guan Yinzi said: The movement of the Five Phases arises from essence giving rise to hun, hun giving rise to spirit, spirit giving rise to intent, intent giving rise to po, and po giving rise to essence. The Five Phases—metal, wood, water, fire, and earth—revolve and mutually generate one another.

Essence pertains to water; hun pertains to wood. Water generates wood—thus it is said: *The movement of the Five Phases arises from essence giving rise to hun.* Spirit pertains to fire; wood generates fire—thus it is said: *from hun giving rise to spirit.* Intent pertains to earth; fire generates earth—thus it is said: *from spirit giving rise to intent.* Po pertains to metal; earth generates metal—thus it is said: *from intent giving rise to po.* Essence pertains to water; metal generates water—thus it is said: *from po giving rise to essence.* These five revolve endlessly. Essence, spirit, hun, po, and intent generate one another in an unceasing cycle. *Yi* means to cease—thus it is said: *these five revolve endlessly.* The sequence of generation is: essence generates hun, hun generates spirit, spirit generates intent, intent generates po, and po generates essence, which in turn generates hun.

If the human heart’s spirit does not arise, then intent does not arise—without fire, there is no earth. If intent does not arise, then po does not arise—without earth, there is no metal. If po does not arise, then essence does not arise—without metal, there is no water. If essence does not arise, then hun does not arise—without water, there is no wood. If hun does not arise, then spirit does not arise—without wood, there is no fire. From this, we know that if the heart does not arise, all five cease—this is the swift path out of the cycle of rebirth.

Thus, my false heart has flowed through the transformations of creation for countless eons without end. The false heart is karmic consciousness; all sentient beings’ karmic consciousness rushes about without foundation, drifting through life and death, entering and exiting the hub, through the four births and six destinies, their bones piled like mountains, revolving for countless eons without end or escape. The great sage, in compassion, expounded this scripture for this reason—thus it is said: *Thus, my false heart has flowed through the transformations of creation for countless eons without end.* *Zaohua* means life and death; *I* refers to all sentient beings.

Yet, from a single kernel, countless trees arise. Though heaven and earth are vast, they cannot sprout a kernel suspended in empty space. Fruit-bearing trees, when their kernels are buried in soil, sprout and grow into trees, which then bear fruit and produce kernels. These kernels, when buried again, sprout and grow into trees, bearing fruit and kernels once more. Thus, they generate one another endlessly, producing countless trees—thus it is said: *Yet, from a single kernel, countless trees arise.* This analogy illustrates that if the heart contains consciousness, the false heart flows through the transformations of creation for countless eons without end. But if a fruit kernel is tied with a string and suspended in empty space, though heaven and earth are vast, how could it sprout? Thus it is said: *Though heaven and earth are vast, they cannot sprout a kernel suspended in empty space.* This analogy illustrates that if the heart unites with true emptiness, the transformations of creation cannot cause it to revolve.

From a single hen’s egg, countless birds arise. Though yin and yang are wondrous, they cannot hatch an egg from a hen without a rooster. A hen, after mating with a rooster, lays an egg; the hen broods over the egg and hatches a chick. When the chick grows, it too has hens and roosters that mate and lay eggs, generating one another endlessly to produce countless birds—thus it is said: *From a single hen’s egg, countless birds arise.* This analogy illustrates that if the heart turns away from awakening and unites with dust, life and death continue without end. But if a hen does not mate with a rooster, though yin and yang are wondrous, how could it lay an egg? Thus it is said: *Though yin and yang are wondrous, they cannot hatch an egg from a hen without a rooster.* This analogy illustrates that if the heart does not unite with dust, there is no life or death.

Only when all that comes to me is gathered in a single breath—then things are transformed into me, and there is nothing that is not me. As for the so-called Five Phases, who can transform them?

The self is the heart. The heart is originally heartless—only when external objects and realms come to disturb it does the heart suddenly appear in a single breath. From this we know that objects are the heart itself. Thus it is said: *Only when all that comes to me is gathered in a single breath are things transformed into me.* If no external objects come to disturb, the heart does not appear. When the heart does not appear, this is true emptiness. How could the Five Phases transform true emptiness? Since they cannot transform true emptiness, there is no cycle of life and death. Thus it is said: *There is nothing that is not me; as for the so-called Five Phases, who can transform them?* Therefore it is said: *Seeing objects, one sees the heart; without objects, the heart is not seen.* In the ten directions, whether open or obstructed, the true heart pervades all—this is precisely what is meant.

This chapter clarifies that the moment a single thought arises, one falls into the Five Phases and the cycle of rebirth continues without end. When not a single thought arises, there is true emptiness, and yin and yang cannot mold or shape it.

---

**Chapter Four**
Guan Yinzi said: The multitude are those who use the *po* to restrain the *hun*. When metal is in excess, wood is insufficient. The *po* is yin—deluded thoughts, attachment to worldly desires, and the myriad evils. The *hun* is yang—purity, stillness, and the myriad virtues. The multitude, foolish and inverted, cling to deluded thoughts, outwardly commit evils, inwardly covet sensual pleasures, deplete their hidden virtue externally, and exhaust their primordial yang internally. When the yang *hun* is spent, the yin *po* alone grows strong. Seized by evil habits, they sink into the yin realms at death, like water flowing downward—this is the nature of yin descending. Since the yin *po* alone is strong, and the *po* belongs to metal, metal is in excess. Since the yang *hun* is spent, and the *hun* belongs to wood, wood is insufficient. *She* means to restrain—to use the strong *po* to subdue the weak *hun*. Thus it is said: *The multitude use the po to restrain the hun; when metal is in excess, wood is insufficient.*

The sage uses the *hun* to move the *po*—when wood is in excess, metal is insufficient. *Yun* means to circulate. When the sage first cultivates the Dao, the heart awakens to clarity, pure and unstained. Outwardly, they practice the myriad virtues without clinging to the traces of good deeds; inwardly, they preserve primordial yang without dwelling in emptiness, transcending all extremes. Pure yang flows and abides, the yang *hun* transforms, and the yin *po* dissolves into stillness. When merit is complete, the form is shed, and they ascend to the yang realms—thus they become sages. Like fire rising upward, this is the nature of yang ascending. Since the yang *hun* transforms, and the *hun* belongs to wood, wood is in excess. Since the yin *po* dissolves into stillness, and the *po* belongs to metal, metal is insufficient.

When the *po* conceals itself, the *hun* follows; when the *hun* wanders, the *po* accompanies it. The *po* belongs to yin, and yin governs stillness—stillness means concealment. When the *po* hides at night, the *hun* also follows it into concealment. Thus it is said: *When the po conceals itself, the hun follows.* *Gai* means "in general, it is so." The *hun* belongs to yang, and yang governs movement—movement means wandering. When the *hun* wanders by day, the *po* follows it in wandering. Thus it is said: *When the hun wanders, the po accompanies it.*

By day, the *hun* lodges in the eyes; by night, the *po* rests in the liver. Lodging in the eyes enables sight; resting in the liver enables dreams. The *hun* is the spirit of the liver, and the eyes are the liver’s outward manifestation—thus the *hun* lodging in the eyes by day is beyond doubt. The *po* is the spirit of the lungs, and the nose is the lungs’ outward manifestation. It should follow that the *po* rests in the lungs by night, but the text states that the *po* rests in the liver by night, which contradicts reason and is likely a scribal error. Though we may force an interpretation by following "rests in the liver," it remains strained. It would be more fitting to say: By day, the *hun* wanders and lodges in the eyes, and the *po* follows it in lodging there as well. By night, the *po* conceals itself in the lungs, and the *hun* follows it in concealment. This reasoning is sound. To say that by day the *hun* wanders and lodges in the eyes, and the *po* follows it in lodging there, while by night the *po* conceals itself in the liver, and the *hun* follows it in concealment, is even more forced—since the liver is the *hun*’s original abode, it should not be said to "follow." When the *hun* wanders and lodges in the eyes by day, it can perceive all forms and appearances, which are discerned by the *hun*’s consciousness. Thus it is said: *By day, the hun lodges in the eyes; lodging in the eyes enables sight.* When the *po* conceals itself in the lungs by night, it produces all dream realms, which are the creations of the *shi-po*’s deluded thoughts and inverted perceptions. Thus it is said: *By night, the po rests in the lungs; resting in the lungs enables dreams.*

What sees is the *hun*, undiscerning; what analyzes is discernment. To analyze and call it "heaven and earth" is the *hun*’s habitual clinging. *Ju* means familiarity. By day, the *hun* wanders and lodges in the eyes, perceiving all forms and appearances, which are discerned by the *hun*. Thus it is said: *What sees is the hun.* When the *hun* first lodges in the eyes, it is originally undiscerning—thus it is said to be *undiscerning*. Suddenly, the faculty of discernment arises—thus it is said: *What analyzes is discernment.* Once discernment arises, it knows what is above as "heaven" and what is below as "earth." Thus it is said: *To analyze and call it "heaven and earth."* Having discerned heaven and earth, the *hun*’s consciousness becomes habitually stained by familiarity, like the moon’s reflection entering the rhinoceros’s horn—though the moon’s form fills the horn, the rhinoceros cannot rid its horn of the moon’s image. The staining of consciousness by heaven, earth, and the myriad things is likewise—this is the cause of the cycle of rebirth, from which one cannot escape for kalpas. Thus it is said: *The hun’s habitual clinging.*

What dreams is the *po*, undiscerning; what analyzes is discernment. To analyze and call it "self and other" is the *po*’s habitual clinging. By night, the *po* conceals itself in the lungs and produces all dreams—thus it is said: *What dreams is the po.* When the *po* first conceals itself in the lungs, it is originally undiscerning, but when the faculty of discernment arises, it analyzes and calls it "that person" and "this self"—all of which is the *po*’s consciousness habitually clinging, deluded dreaming, and mistaking the false for the real. Thus it is said: *Undiscerning; what analyzes is discernment. To analyze and call it "self and other" is the po’s habitual clinging.*

Fire generates earth, thus spirit generates intent. (The *Guozi Qian* edition includes these seven characters, which harmonize smoothly with the scriptural phrasing, and so they are adopted here for explication.) Spirit pertains to fire; intent pertains to earth. Fire generates earth, thus spirit generates intent—hence it is said: *Fire generates earth, spirit generates intent.*

Earth generates metal, thus intent generates the *po*. Intent pertains to earth; the *po* pertains to metal. Thus intent generates the *po*—hence it is said: *Earth generates metal, thus intent generates the po.*

When spirit moves, it is no longer called spirit but intent. When intent moves, it is no longer called intent but the *po*. "Movement" means generation. What spirit generates can no longer be named spirit but is called intent. Spirit pertains to fire; intent pertains to earth. Fire generates earth, and thus when spirit generates, it is named intent—hence it is said: *When spirit moves, it is no longer called spirit but intent.* What intent generates can no longer be named intent but is called the *po*. The *po* pertains to metal; earth generates metal, and thus when intent generates, it is named the *po*—hence it is said: *When intent moves, it is no longer called intent but the po.*

Only the sage knows that the self is no-self and that phenomena are no-phenomena, all of which arise from discriminative thought and calculation. The sage awakens to true emptiness, originally devoid of self. "True emptiness" means thought has not yet arisen. When thought has not yet arisen, inwardly one does not know the self, and outwardly one does not know phenomena. "Knows" means awakens. The sage awakens to true emptiness, originally devoid of self and phenomena. Now, the calculation of self and phenomena arises entirely from deluded thought—hence it is said: *Only the sage knows that the self is no-self and that phenomena are no-phenomena, all of which arise from discriminative thought and calculation.*

Therefore, when the myriad things arise, I respond to them with nature, not with the heart-mind. "Nature" means the heart-mind has not yet arisen. "Therefore" is a phrase connecting the preceding and following. Because ordinary people, through discriminative thought, falsely calculate the myriad things as self and other, when the myriad things arise, I merely respond with nature. "Nature" is true emptiness; true emptiness responds to things with constant stillness—hence it is said: *Therefore, when the myriad things arise, I respond to them with nature.* Why respond with nature and not with the heart-mind? The answer: "Nature" is the heart-mind before it arises. The heart-mind before it arises is the nature of true emptiness—hence it is said: *Not with the heart-mind; nature is the heart-mind before it arises.*

Without the heart-mind, there is no intent—for without fire, there is no earth. Without intent, there is no *po*—for without earth, there is no metal. The heart-mind pertains to fire; intent pertains to earth. Without fire, earth is not generated—hence it is said: *Without the heart-mind, there is no intent—for without fire, there is no earth.* Intent pertains to earth; the *po* pertains to metal. Without earth, metal is not generated—hence it is said: *Without intent, there is no po—for without earth, there is no metal.*

If the one is not preserved, the five are all abolished. The "one" is the heart-mind. If the one heart-mind does not arise, then the five—spirit, intent, *po*, essence, and *hun*—are all abolished and no longer generate one another—hence it is said: *If the one is not preserved, the five are all abolished.*

Having already merged the myriad things of heaven and earth into the *hun*, one can then merge the myriad things of heaven and earth into the *po*. "Having already" refers to the preceding chapter’s discussion of uniting one’s *hun* with the *hun* of heaven and earth. Having already merged one’s *hun* with the *hun* of heaven and earth, one can then merge one’s *po* with the *po* of heaven and earth—hence it is said: *Having already merged the myriad things of heaven and earth into the hun, one can then merge the myriad things of heaven and earth into the po.*

All that the creative transformation subtly manifests is my *hun*; all that the creative transformation possesses is my *po*—thus there is not a single thing that can enslave me. As stated above, my *hun* and *po* merge with the *hun* and *po* of heaven and earth. From this, one knows that whatever the creative transformation subtly manifests or possesses is my *hun* and *po*—hence it is said: *All that the creative transformation subtly manifests is my hun; all that the creative transformation possesses is my po.* "Creative transformation" means the creator. Having roamed with the creator, one can command the myriad things, and not a single thing can command me. The scripture says: *The Way alone can create the myriad things, yet there is not a single thing that can create the Way or harm it*—this is analogous. Hence it is said: *Thus there is not a single thing that can enslave me.*

This chapter clarifies that when the one heart-mind arises, the five spirits falsely generate without cease. When the one heart-mind does not arise, the five spirits return to their origin, and one can roam with the creator.

**Chapter Five**
Guan Yinzi says: "Ghost with *yun* is *hun*; ghost with *bai* is *po*—this is so in terms of script." The character *hun* is composed of *gui* (ghost) and *yun* (cloud); the character *po* is composed of *gui* (ghost) and *bai* (white)—hence it is said: *Ghost with yun is hun; ghost with bai is po—this is so in terms of script.*

A ghost is what a person transforms into after death. If a fraction of yin is not exhausted, one cannot become an immortal; if a fraction of yang is not exhausted, one cannot become a ghost. From this, one knows that when a person’s original yang is entirely depleted, even if they have not died, they are as good as dead. A person is a ghost wearing a husk; a ghost is a person who has shed their husk. It is deluded, pernicious habits that, after death, sink one into the yin realm and transform one into a ghost—hence it is said: *A ghost is what a person transforms into after death.*

*Yun* (cloud) is wind; wind is wood. *Bai* (white) is *qi*; *qi* is metal. Yang *qi* becomes clouds. When clouds rise without yin to meet them, they transform into wind. Wind pertains to *xun* (the trigram); *xun* pertains to wood—hence it is said: *Yun is wind; wind is wood.* The color white pertains to metal; metal *qi* transforms into the color white—hence it is said: *Bai is qi; qi is metal.*

Wind disperses, thus it is light and pure; what is light and pure ascends to heaven. Metal is hard, thus it is heavy and turbid; what is heavy and turbid descends into the earth. Wind pertains to yang and can disperse the myriad things, thus it is light and pure. Since wind pertains to yang and is light and pure, it naturally ascends to heaven—hence it is said: *Wind disperses, thus it is light and pure; what is light and pure ascends to heaven.*

Metal’s form pertains to yin, thus it is hard and heavy-turbid. Since metal’s form pertains to yin, being hard and heavy-turbid, it naturally sinks into the earth—hence it is said: *Metal is hard, thus heavy-turbid; what is heavy-turbid descends into the earth.*

What is light and pure: the *po* ascends following the *hun*. What is heavy and turbid: the *hun* descends following the *po*. When one cultivates all virtues, one pertains to yang; at death, the blessings of goodness sustain them, so their *qi* is light and pure, and the *po* naturally ascends with the *hun* into the yang realm—this, too, is a matter of natural correspondence. Hence it is said: *What is light and pure: the po ascends following the hun.*

When one commits all evils, one pertains to yin; at death, the karmic weight of wickedness seizes them, so their *qi* is heavy and turbid, and the *hun* naturally descends with the *po*, sinking into the yin realm—this, too, is a matter of natural correspondence. Hence it is said: *What is heavy and turbid: the hun descends following the po.*

Those who ascend through benevolence become assistants to Jupiter; those who ascend through righteousness become assistants to Venus; those who ascend through propriety become assistants to Mars; those who ascend through wisdom become assistants to Mercury; those who ascend through sincerity become assistants to Saturn.

One who devotes themselves entirely to benevolence and perfects its practice, at death ascends to become an assistant to Jupiter, the Year Star—for benevolence pertains to wood. This, too, follows naturally from its kind. Hence it is said: *Those who ascend through benevolence become assistants to Jupiter.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to righteousness and rightly orders all things, perfecting its practice, at death ascends to become an assistant to Venus, the Great White Star—for righteousness pertains to metal. Hence it is said: *Those who ascend through righteousness become assistants to Venus.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to propriety and honors ruler and father, perfecting its practice, at death ascends to become an assistant to Mars, the Sparkling Deluder—for propriety pertains to fire. Hence it is said: *Those who ascend through propriety become assistants to Mars.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to wisdom, discerning truth from falsehood and right from wrong, following truth and rejecting falsehood, perfecting its practice, at death ascends to become an assistant to Mercury, the Culminating Star—for wisdom pertains to water. Hence it is said: *Those who ascend through wisdom become assistants to Mercury.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to sincerity, aligning word with deed, perfecting its practice, at death ascends to become an assistant to Saturn, the Quelling Star—for sincerity pertains to earth. Hence it is said: *Those who ascend through sincerity become assistants to Saturn.*

Those who sink through lack of benevolence are punished by wood; those who sink through lack of righteousness are punished by metal; those who sink through lack of propriety are punished by fire; those who sink through lack of wisdom are punished by water; those who sink through lack of sincerity are punished by earth.

One who practices no benevolence, monopolizing all evils and perfecting their practice, at death sinks into the yin realm, where the Eastern Marchmount seizes and punishes them—for the Eastern Marchmount pertains to wood. If they are seized and punished, how is this not the predation of wood? Hence it is said: *Those who sink through lack of benevolence are punished by wood.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to unrighteousness, inverting all things and defying principle, perfecting their practice, at death sinks into the yin realm, where the Western Marchmount seizes and punishes them—for the Western Marchmount pertains to metal. Hence it is said: *Those who sink through lack of righteousness are punished by metal.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to impropriety, insulting ruler and father, disloyal and unfilial, perfecting their practice, at death sinks into the yin realm, where the Southern Marchmount seizes and punishes them—for the Southern Marchmount pertains to fire. Hence it is said: *Those who sink through lack of propriety are punished by fire.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to folly, deluded and obstructed, unable to discern truth from falsehood or right from wrong, inverting all things, at death sinks into the yin realm, where the Northern Marchmount seizes and punishes them—for the Northern Marchmount pertains to water. Hence it is said: *Those who sink through lack of wisdom are punished by water.*

One who devotes themselves entirely to insincerity, believing all deceitful words and lies, aligning neither word nor deed, their deceit and falsehood excessive, at death sinks into the yin realm, where the Central Marchmount seizes and punishes them—for the Central Marchmount pertains to earth. Hence it is said: *Those who sink through lack of sincerity are punished by earth.*

Those who perfect the virtues of the Five Constants become assistants to the Five Stars; those who betray the virtues of the Five Constants are, in turn, preyed upon by the Five Phases. How can one not be cautious!

When *hun* and *po* are evenly matched, one remains among humans.
One who practices both good and evil in equal measure has blessings and sins balanced: goodness pertains to the yang *hun*, evil to the yin *po*. Thus, they neither ascend to the yang realm nor sink into the yin realm, but remain among humans, revolving through birth and death. Hence it is said: *When hun and po are evenly matched, one remains among humans.*

The ascending *hun* brings nobility; the descending *po* brings baseness. The numinous *hun* brings worthiness; the malevolent *po* brings foolishness. The light *hun* brings brightness; the heavy *po* brings darkness.

If one performed many good deeds in a past life, the ascending *hun* brings wealth and nobility in this life. Hence it is said: *The ascending hun brings nobility.*

If one performed many evil deeds in a past life, the descending *po* brings poverty and baseness in this life. Hence it is said: *The descending po brings baseness.*

If one possessed wisdom and understood principle in a past life, the numinous *hun* brings worthiness in this life. Hence it is said: *The numinous hun brings worthiness.*

If one lacked wisdom in a past life, being foolish, obstinate, and violent, the malevolent *po* brings foolishness in this life. Hence it is said: *The malevolent po brings foolishness.*

If one cultivated wisdom and goodness in a past life, the light *hun* brings intelligence in this life. Hence it is said: *The light hun brings brightness.*

If one lacked wisdom and practiced evil in a past life, the heavy *po* brings dullness and darkness in this life. Hence it is said: *The heavy po brings darkness.*

The yang *hun* becomes winged creatures; the dull *po* becomes furred creatures. The luminous *hun* becomes spirits; the obscure *po* becomes ghosts.

If one was frivolous and committed evil in this life, at death the yang *hun* becomes a creature with wings. Hence it is said: *The yang hun becomes winged creatures.*

If one was dull and committed evil in this life, at death the dull *po* becomes a creature with fur. Hence it is said: *The dull po becomes furred creatures.*

If one was intelligent, upright, detested evil, and delighted in goodness in this life, at death the luminous *hun* becomes a spirit. Hence it is said: *The luminous hun becomes spirits.*

If one favored darkness, practiced deceit and theft, and committed all manner of evil in this life, at death the obscure *po* becomes a ghost. Hence it is said: *The obscure po becomes ghosts.*

Their forms and dwellings, their perceptions and preferences—all are bound to the Five Phases. Whether noble or base, wise or foolish, luminous or obscure, winged or furred, spirits or ghosts, though their shapes and abodes differ, all arise from the karmic seeds of their perceptions and desires, each reaping its corresponding fruit. Those who cultivate the Five Constants as their cause receive the fruit of becoming the five celestial ministers; those who violate the Five Constants receive the fruit of being judged by the Five Peaks. Hence it is said: *Their forms and dwellings, their perceptions and preferences—all are bound to the Five Phases.*

The generative and consummate numbers of the Phases are not uniform, which is why the myriad things are so abundant that they fill the space between Heaven and Earth without cease. Water’s generative number is one, its consummate number six; fire’s generative number is two, its consummate number seven; wood’s generative number is three, its consummate number eight; metal’s generative number is four, its consummate number nine; earth’s generative number is five, its consummate number ten. Hence it is said: *The numbers of the Phases are not uniform.*

The Five Phases and yin-yang mutually impel and agitate, intermingling and transforming. From the vast to the minute, the flying and the hidden, the moving and the still—all teem and surge without end. In their countless multitudes, they fill the space between Heaven and Earth, ceaselessly generating and transforming, giving rise to diverse kinds, never resting. Hence it is said: *This is why the myriad things are so abundant that they fill the space between Heaven and Earth without cease.*

The Five Affairs correspond to the Five Phases, and the Five Phases give rise to the Five Creatures—how could one exhaust their enumeration? The Five Affairs are all functions: seeing, hearing, eating, breathing, and thinking. Their correspondence to the Five Phases is as follows: the eyes are the outward manifestation of the liver, and the liver belongs to wood, so seeing pertains to wood; the ears are the outward manifestation of the kidneys, and the kidneys belong to water, so hearing pertains to water; the tongue is the outward manifestation of the heart, and the heart belongs to fire, so eating pertains to fire; the nose is the outward manifestation of the lungs, and the lungs belong to metal, so breathing pertains to metal; the mind is the spirit of the spleen, and the spleen belongs to earth, so thinking pertains to earth. Hence it is said: *The Five Affairs correspond to the Five Phases.*

The Five Phases are wood, fire, earth, metal, and water. The Five Creatures are scaled, feathered, naked, furred, and armored. The east, with its celestial stems *jia* and *yi*, belongs to wood and gives rise to 360 kinds of scaled creatures, with the dragon as their chief. The south, with its celestial stems *bing* and *ding*, belongs to fire and gives rise to 360 kinds of feathered creatures, with the phoenix as their chief. The center, with its celestial stems *wu* and *ji*, belongs to earth and gives rise to 360 kinds of naked creatures, with the sage as their chief. The west, with its celestial stems *geng* and *xin*, belongs to metal and gives rise to 360 kinds of furred creatures, with the qilin as their chief. The north, with its celestial stems *ren* and *gui*, belongs to water and gives rise to 360 kinds of armored creatures, with the spirit-turtle as their chief. Hence it is said: *The Five Phases give rise to the Five Creatures*—how could one exhaust the discussion of moving and growing things? *Sheng* means "to bear," and *yan* means "to discourse."

For example, when divining with tortoise shells or milfoil stalks, if sincerity is fully present, the Five Phases respond; but if sincerity is lacking, no matter how one divines or calculates, not a single response will come.

The divination with tortoise shells and milfoil stalks is the second chapter of the "Two Pillars of Interpretation." All that has been discussed earlier—nobility and baseness, wisdom and foolishness, clarity and obscurity, feathered and furred creatures, spirits and ghosts, and the karmic causes of the Five Constants—is like making sincere prayers and drilling the tortoise shell or casting the milfoil stalks: the Five Phases respond with auspicious or inauspicious omens. A person’s form, dwelling, knowledge, and preferences all correspond to the Five Phases in the same way. Thus it is said: *For example, when divining with tortoise shells or milfoil stalks, if sincerity is fully present, the Five Phases respond.*

If, when drilling the tortoise shell or casting the milfoil stalks, the heart is not fully sincere, not a single omen of fortune or misfortune will respond. When the human heart has no attachments, severs its desires, remains pure and unstained, departs from all extremes, and does not fall into cause and effect, the cycle of rebirth is likewise. Thus it is said: *If sincerity is lacking, no matter how one divines or calculates, not a single response will come.*

The sage borrows things to wander through the world, and the Five Phases cannot but correspond. The sage employs the forms of the Four Great Elements and the virtue of the Five Constants to universally benefit all beings, making wondrous use of them. Having taken the Five Constants as their wondrous function, dwelling and wandering in the world to universally benefit all beings, they assign benevolence to wood, righteousness to metal, propriety to fire, wisdom to water, and trustworthiness to earth. Since these are the Five Constants, they cannot but correspond to the Five Phases. Thus it is said: *The sage borrows things to wander through the world, and the Five Phases cannot but correspond.*

This chapter clarifies that when the heart is stained with attachments, it falls into cause and effect; when the heart is free from attachments, it escapes the cycle of rebirth.

**Chapter Six**
Master Guan Yin said: The three possess the *hun*-soul. The *hun* is consciousness; the eye is essence; color is spirit. That which perceives them belongs to the *hun*—ears, mouth, nose, heart, and the like.

The *hun* possesses this consciousness, essence, and spirit—these three. Thus it is said: *The three possess the *hun*-soul.* The *hun* dwells in the eye by day; the eye sees and distinguishes the five colors. It also possesses consciousness, essence, and spirit, and only then can it see and distinguish. Thus it is said: *The *hun* is consciousness; the eye is essence; color is spirit.* That which distinguishes color with the eye is the *hun*’s consciousness; the eye’s spirit-water is its essence and spirit.

Not only is seeing so, but hearing sounds, tasting flavors, smelling fragrances, and the heart contemplating objects all similarly possess consciousness, essence, and spirit before they can distinguish sounds, flavors, fragrances, and objects. Thus it is said: *That which perceives them belongs to the *hun*—ears, mouth, nose, heart, and the like.* "The like" means "the same."

In this life, love is essence and the root of the father for the next life; observation is spirit and the root of the mother for the next life. Though love and observation differ, both arise from the same consciousness. In this life, the body is the father for the next; the love that clings to the mother is essence—essence belongs to water. The mother for the next life is the observation that clings to the father—observation is spirit, and spirit belongs to fire. The intermingling of the father’s and mother’s essence and spirit is the root of this life’s body. Thus it is said: *In this life, love is essence and the root of the father for the next life; observation is spirit and the root of the mother for the next life.* Though the father’s love and the mother’s observation differ, both use consciousness and emotion to intermingle and give birth to the child—they are the same. Thus it is said: *Though love and observation differ, both arise from the same cause.*

The root of the next life’s birth. In the next life, the first is the father, so one receives *qi* from the father’s *qi*, which is water. The second is the mother, so one receives blood from the mother’s blood, which is fire. The father and mother of the next life are the root of its birth. The father’s essence belongs to water, and water’s number is one—thus the first is the father. At the beginning of this body’s birth, it first receives the father’s essence-*qi* to form the embryo’s origin—essence-*qi* belongs to water. Thus it is said: *The root of the next life’s birth. In the next life, the first is the father, so one receives *qi* from the father’s *qi*, which is water.* The mother’s blood belongs to fire, and fire’s number is two—thus the second is the mother. At the beginning of this body’s birth, it next receives the mother’s blood-*qi* to form the embryo’s foundation—blood-*qi* belongs to fire. Thus it is said: *The second is the mother, so one receives blood from the mother’s blood, which is fire.* With a father and a mother, the next life is born. With a father and a mother, yin and yang intermingle, and the father and mother of the next life give birth without end. Thus it is said: *With a father and a mother, the next life is born.*

Only when love is without consciousness, like the intertwining of a lock, and observation is without consciousness, like the illumination of a lamp—when my consciousness does not sprout—how can my birth exist? Only by being perfectly impartial, universally benefiting all without consciousness, seeking no reward, and not abiding in the traces of benevolent action—though engaging with the world like the intertwining of a lock’s rings, without the heart of emotional consciousness—can this be so. Thus it is said: *Only when love is without consciousness, like the intertwining of a lock.* The benevolent embrace all with love; their perfect clarity, concentration, and wisdom are like a great mirror, reflecting things without heart, like a lamp dispelling darkness, illuminating things without consciousness. Thus it is said: *Observation is without consciousness, like the illumination of a lamp.* As described above, being perfectly impartial, universally benefiting all without heart, with concentration and wisdom perfectly clear, responding to things without consciousness—when my consciousness does not sprout, how can my birth and death exist? Thus it is said: *When my consciousness does not sprout, how can my birth exist?* This chapter clarifies that consciousness is the seed of birth and death; without consciousness, there is no cycle of birth and death.

**Chapter Seven**
Master Guan Yin said: It is like striking a drum with a stick. The drum’s form is what I possess; the drum’s sound is my response. The stick has already gone, yet the lingering sound remains—ultimately, it too will cease to exist.

Wandering through the world, when things come to interact, it is like a drumstick striking a drum—only after being struck does the sound arise. Thus it is said: *It is like striking a drum with a stick.* What I possess—my essence and spirit—is like the drum’s form; my response is like the drum’s sound. Thus it is said: *The drum’s form is what I possess; the drum’s sound is my response.* The stick has already gone, yet the drum’s lingering sound remains.

As the stimulus that aroused me has already passed, yet the awareness of my essence, spirit, soul, and vitality lingers unresolved—turning the light inward to shatter this awareness and forget it is what ultimately renders it nonexistent. Thus it is said: *The stick has already gone, yet the lingering sound remains—ultimately, it too will cease to exist.* The drum’s form is like my essence; the drum’s sound is like my spirit; its lingering resonance is like my soul and vitality.

My vivid clarity is like the drum’s form; my stillness, unmoving, yet responsive and all-pervading, is like the drum’s sound. Thus it is said: *The drum’s form is like my essence; the drum’s sound is like my spirit.* The awareness of my soul and vitality, unceasing in its response to external stimuli, is like the stick that has already gone, yet the drum’s lingering resonance remains. Thus it is said: *The lingering sound is like the soul and vitality.*

Know that what comes and goes in an instant—how can the qi of the Five Phases belong to me? If one awakens to the true emptiness of original nature, perceiving the fleeting comings and goings of external phenomena while remaining ever still in response, then how can the qi of Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth pertain to true emptiness? Thus it is said: *Know that what comes and goes in an instant—how can the qi of the Five Phases belong to me?* This chapter clarifies that true emptiness does not pertain to the Five Phases.

**Chapter Eight**
Master Guan Yin said: The kernel within a fruit must await the presence of Water, Fire, and Earth—only then can it generate endlessly. Without all three, as in great drought, great flood, or barren clods, none suffice to bring forth life.

Within the fruit, the kernel buried in the soil must await the nourishment of rain and moisture, the warmth of sunlight and fire—only then does the kernel sprout, the sprout grow into a tree, and the tree bear fruit once more. The kernel is planted again, and with the nurturing warmth of Water, Fire, and Earth, it sprouts, grows into a tree, and bears fruit in an unending cycle. Thus it is said: *The kernel within a fruit must await the presence of Water, Fire, and Earth—only then can it generate endlessly.*

If the kernel is not buried in soil, lacking the nourishment of rain and sunlight, how can it sprout and grow into a tree? Without Water, Fire, and Earth, as in times of severe drought, flooding, or parched earth, nothing can take root and grow. Thus it is said: *Without all three, as in great drought, great flood, or barren clods, none suffice to bring forth life.* "Flood" means excess; "suffice" means attainment.

Essence-Water, Spirit-Fire, and Mind-Earth do not originally interact—only when humans unite them through the root do they appear to give rise to phenomena. Essence pertains to Water, Spirit to Fire, and Mind to Earth. These three—essence, spirit, and mind—do not inherently generate phenomena, but when a man and woman unite their roots, essence, spirit, and awareness merge in the fleeting moment of conception, illusorily perceiving the form of this embryonic event. Thus life arises endlessly, just as a kernel, with Water, Fire, and Earth, sprouts, grows into a tree, and bears fruit in an unceasing cycle. Thus it is said: *Essence-Water, Spirit-Fire, and Mind-Earth do not originally interact—only when humans unite them through the root do they appear to give rise to phenomena.*

It is like a sorcerer who, from utter nothingness, conjures manifold phenomena. Before the union of man and woman, each abides in the true emptiness of original nature, fundamentally pure and devoid of phenomena. Yet through the union of their roots, essence, spirit, and mind give rise to a child—much like a sorcerer or shaman who, from utter nothingness, summons spirits and illusorily perceives strange transformations. Thus it is said: *It is like a sorcerer who, from utter nothingness, conjures manifold phenomena.* This chapter clarifies that originally, all is pure and still, fundamentally free from birth and death. It is human delusion and desire that plunge one into the cycle of birth and death.

**Chapter Nine**
Master Guan Yin said: The soul is Water; the root of Wood lies in winter Water, yet its blossoms appear in summer Fire. Thus, a person’s soul is stored in the night’s essence and manifests in the day’s spirit.

The soul is Wood. In winter, Wood returns to its root to renew its life, nourished by winter Water; in summer, warmed by Fire, it blooms. Thus it is said: *The soul is Wood; the root of Wood lies in winter Water, yet its blossoms appear in summer Fire.* Essence pertains to Water, and the soul to Wood—Water generates Wood. Thus it is said: *A person’s soul is stored in the night’s essence.* Night pertains to yin, and yin to Water; day pertains to yang, and yang to Fire. The spirit pertains to Fire, and the soul, dwelling in the eyes by day, reveals its radiance through the spirit. Thus it is said: *And manifests in the day’s spirit.*

United with essence, what is perceived is uniquely mine—for essence has never known another. United with spirit, what is perceived is shared by all—for spirit has never known a self. Essence pertains to Water, and Water has no other; thus, essence also has no other. United with pure essence, one’s clarity stands solitary, unaccompanied by the myriad phenomena. Thus it is said: *United with essence, what is perceived is uniquely mine—for essence has never known another.* United with pure spirit, one’s awareness is profound and unfathomable, responsive only after being stirred, answering to others without a self. Spirit pertains to Fire, and Fire has no self; thus, spirit also has no self. Thus it is said: *United with spirit, what is perceived is shared by all—for spirit has never known a self.* This chapter clarifies that one who embodies pure essence and pure spirit transcends both self and other.

**Chapter Ten**
Master Guan Yin said: Realize that this body is like a body in a dream, appearing as one’s emotions dictate—then one may send forth the spirit as oneself and wander the Great Purity.

To "realize" is to awaken. If one awakens to the truth that this body, composed of the four great elements, is like dust gathering into foam—ephemeral, dreamlike, and insubstantial—an illusory and unreal form shaped by deluded emotions into the notion of a self, then, with such awakening and thorough penetration of true emptiness, one may soar with the spirit’s function, swift without haste, arriving without traveling, and wander the realm of the Great Purity without cease.

The Great Purity is the wondrous Dao of true emptiness—thus it is said: "Realize that this body is like a body in a dream, appearing as one’s emotions dictate, then one may send forth the spirit as oneself and wander the Great Purity." Realize that these phenomena are like objects in a dream, shaped by one’s emotions—then one may condense essence into phenomena and traverse the Eight Wastes.

Phenomena are functions. If one awakens to the sixfold spiritual penetrations as likewise dreamlike and insubstantial, and thoroughly penetrates true emptiness, one may concentrate the luminous clarity of refined essence to manifest wondrous function, riding the Eight Wastes in an instant’s journey. Thus it is said: "Realize that these phenomena are like objects in a dream, appearing as one’s emotions dictate, then one may condense essence into phenomena and traverse the Eight Wastes."

The Eight Wastes lie beyond the Four Seas, called the Eight Chords; beyond the Eight Chords lie the Eight Expanses; beyond the Eight Expanses lie the Eight Regions; beyond the Eight Regions lie the Eight Poles; beyond the Eight Poles lie the Eight Wastes; beyond the Eight Wastes lies the Vast Primeval, the boundless frontier.

Moreover, the Great Purity is the heaven where the Supreme resides, above the Four Celestial Realms of humankind—it is the immortal realm of the Great Purity. Those of great attainment take true emptiness and the Great Void as their body, reaching as far as beyond the Eight Wastes and as near as the space between their brows. Yin and yang cannot fathom it; ghosts and spirits cannot glimpse it. Without relying on external things, they traverse the immortal realm of the Great Purity beyond the Eight Wastes in an instant’s journey.

At times, they manifest transformations to awaken the deluded; at others, they rouse sincerity and faith in the Dao. Sometimes they project the spirit as a body beyond the body, riding the void and treading emptiness to ascend and wander the immortal realm of the Great Purity. At other times, they condense essence into dragons and tigers, simurghs and phoenixes, tortoises and cranes, beams and carp, winnowing baskets and towers, riding them to roam beyond the Eight Wastes in playful excursion.

This Dao can preserve spirit to prolong life, or forget spirit to transcend life. *This* refers to this Dao—the inconceivable Dao. When one awakens to the wondrousness of this inconceivable Dao, the body becomes like withered wood, the mind like cold ashes. Naturally, the spirit settles and essence congeals, reversing aging to restore youth, extending life and preserving vision—like Guangchengzi, who lived twelve hundred years without ever declining. Thus it is said: "This Dao can preserve spirit to prolong life." When body and mind are like withered wood and cold ashes, one forgets spirit and far transcends birth and death. Thus it is said: "It can forget spirit to transcend life."

Inhale qi to nourish essence, like metal generating water.
Qi is white and belongs to metal. There exists a minor practice of hygiene: inhaling through the nose, holding the breath briefly, and exhaling slowly to nourish essence and cure illness. Qi nurtures essence as metal generates water—qi pertains to metal, essence to water. Thus it is said: "Inhale qi to nourish essence, like metal generating water."

Inhale wind to nourish spirit, like wood generating fire.
There exists a minor practice to cultivate the heart: drawing in pure wind through the nose, allowing gentle ebb and flow, so the heart remains undisturbed and the spirit settled, thus receiving its nourishment. Wind pertains to wood, spirit to fire. Thus it is said: "Inhale wind to nourish spirit, like wood generating fire."

These methods borrow externals to extend spirit.
As mentioned above, inhaling qi and wind to nourish spirit borrows externals to retain and extend spirit, preventing its dissipation. Thus it is said: "These methods borrow externals to extend spirit."

Rinse with water to nourish essence, so essence never depletes.
There exists a minor practice: rinsing with saliva to preserve essence, preventing its leakage, so essence flourishes without end. Thus it is said: "Rinse with water to nourish essence, so essence never depletes."

Rub with fire to nourish spirit, so spirit never depletes.
There exists a minor practice: concentrating the mind to rub the navel wheel until it grows hot as fire, so spirit flourishes without end. Thus it is said: "Rub with fire to nourish spirit, so spirit never depletes."

These methods borrow internals to extend spirit.
As mentioned above, rinsing with the water of saliva and rubbing with the fire of the navel wheel do not rely on externals—they borrow internals to retain and extend spirit, preventing its dissipation. Thus it is said: "These methods borrow internals to extend spirit."

As for those who forget essence to transcend life, I have spoken of this before.
When one thoroughly penetrates true emptiness and spirit returns to its source, profound and unfathomable, one no longer knows of spirit to forget, nor of birth and death to transcend. I have often instructed disciples on this. As for other minor practices borrowing externals or internals, I have never spoken of them. Thus it is said: "As for those who forget essence to transcend life, I have spoken of this before."
