This chapter clarifies that the Dao is realized through the awakening of the heart—minor practices cannot compare.

**Chapter Eleven**
Master Guan Yin said: One who diligently practices ritual keeps the spirit from scattering and may gather it.
Ritual pertains to fire in the Five Phases, as does spirit. Ritual is reverence and care. When the heart is devoted to vigilance and sincerity, never deceiving even in solitude, the spirit does not gallop outward but gathers and settles in stillness. Thus it is said: "One who diligently practices ritual keeps the spirit from scattering and may gather it." *Diligence* means focus.

One who diligently cultivates wisdom prevents essence from leaking and may refine it.
Wisdom pertains to water in the Five Phases, as does essence. Wisdom discerns truth from falsehood, right from wrong, and is devoted to preserving truth and forgetting falsehood—thus names follow right and shun wrong. In this way, essence does not leak outward but may be refined and nurtured. Thus it is said: "One who diligently cultivates wisdom prevents essence from leaking and may refine it."

Gathering means nurturing. Benevolence pertains to yang and brightness, and may lighten the *hun*-soul. Righteousness pertains to yin and darkness, and may govern the *po*-soul. Benevolence corresponds to wood in the Five Phases, as does the *hun*-soul. Benevolence nurtures the self through stillness and benefits others through compassion. Compassion and goodness belong to yang; stillness brings clarity. Thus the *hun*-spirit becomes light and pure, limpid and deep. Hence it is said: "Benevolence pertains to yang and brightness, and may lighten the *hun*-soul." Righteousness corresponds to metal in the Five Phases, as does the *po*-soul. Profoundly comprehending true emptiness, it unites with the Dao in authenticity. Concealed in yin’s hidden depths, it causes the *shi-po* to rest in stillness and become clear and penetrating. Hence it is said: "Righteousness pertains to yin and darkness, and may govern the *po*-soul." To govern means to cause to rest. This chapter elucidates the wondrous functions of the Five Constants as the Dao.

---

**Chapter Twelve**
Master Guan Yin said: The dung beetle rolls a pellet. When the pellet is formed, it concentrates its spirit upon it, and a white grub comes to dwell within. Soon it sheds its husk and becomes a cicada. The dung beetle rolls dung into a pellet. Once the pellet is formed, it burrows into the earth, embracing the pellet with single-minded focus. The beetle’s spirit and qi enter the pellet, transforming into a white grub. Nurtured through autumn, winter, and spring, warmed by summer’s fire-like qi, it emerges from the soil and climbs grasses, trees, or walls. In an instant, its back splits open; it sheds its husk and becomes a cicada, flying among the trees, sipping wind and dew as it sings. Hence it is said: "The dung beetle rolls a pellet. When the pellet is formed, it concentrates its spirit upon it, and a white grub comes to dwell within. Soon it sheds its husk and becomes a cicada." If the beetle does not concentrate its spirit, how could the grub come to be? All transformations follow deluded thoughts, and form changes accordingly. Thus it is said: "If the beetle does not concentrate its spirit, how could the grub come to be?" *Xi* means "how." This chapter clarifies that the transformations of life and death all arise from deluded attachment.

---

**Chapter Thirteen**
Master Guan Yin said: A cook was preparing mantis shrimp. He left one leg upon the table, and though the shrimp had been cooked, the discarded leg still twitched. Life and death are but the gathering and dispersal of a single qi. When a cook prepares mantis shrimp, leaving one leg upon the table, the shrimp may be cooked, yet the discarded leg still moves. From this we know that life and death are but the gathering and dispersal of a single qi. When qi gathers, life arises; when qi disperses, death follows. That the discarded leg still moves shows the qi has not yet completely dissipated. Hence it is said: "A cook was preparing mantis shrimp. He left one leg upon the table, and though the shrimp had been cooked, the discarded leg still twitched. Life and death are but the gathering and dispersal of a single qi." That which is neither born nor dies—yet people falsely conceive of birth and death. *Heng* means delusion. The original face of true emptiness, the inherent nature, existed before the Five Ultimates. Before the Five Ultimates, there was not yet a single qi. From this we know that the nature of true emptiness does not belong to qi. Not belonging to qi, it has no gathering or dispersal. Without gathering or dispersal, there is no birth or death. Thus, those who fail to awaken to this nature see only the gathering of qi into form as birth and the dispersal of form as death—how could this not be a deluded conception? Hence it is said: "That which is neither born nor dies—yet people falsely conceive of birth and death." This chapter clarifies that the nature is fundamentally without birth or death; the deluded fear it out of ignorance.

---

**Chapter Fourteen**
Master Guan Yin said: There are those who die standing, those who die sitting, those who die suddenly, those who die from illness, and those who die from poison. Though their deaths differ, there is no distinction between them. In the world, some depart while standing, some while sitting, some in an instant, some from grave illness, and some from ingesting poison. Though the manner of death—standing, sitting, sudden, illness, or poison—varies, death itself is no different. There is no distinction between one death and another. Hence it is said: "There are those who die standing, those who die sitting, those who die suddenly, those who die from illness, and those who die from poison. Though their deaths differ, there is no distinction between them." The one who knows the Dao does not perceive birth, and thus does not perceive death. *Zhi* means awakening. The awakened one, having penetrated true emptiness, is free from birth and death in the heart. Turning inward, they see the four great elements as a temporary assemblage, like dust gathering and dispersing, like a thatched hut briefly lodging a traveler. They do not perceive birth or death as real. Hence it is said: "The one who knows the Dao does not perceive birth, and thus does not perceive death." This chapter clarifies that the Dao is without birth or death, and those who have realized the Dao perceive no birth or death.

---

**Chapter Fifteen**
Master Guan Yin said: Those who loathe life and death and seek to transcend them all suffer from a grave affliction. *Huan* means illness. The deluded, failing to realize that the Dao is without birth or death and that one can be free from them, cling to life and fear death. Before awakening, they conceive an aversion to life and death and seek to transcend them directly—this is the great affliction of the Dao-seeker. Hence it is said: "Those who loathe life and death and seek to transcend them all suffer from a grave affliction." For example, if a transformed being harbored such aversion and desire, it would be called a demon, not the Dao. A *hua-ren* is one who teaches and transforms others. For instance, if a teacher instructed the deluded to cultivate aversion to life and death and seek to transcend them, this would be teaching demonic and inauspicious practices—how could it be called teaching the Dao? Hence it is said: "For example, if a transformed being harbored such aversion and desire, it would be called a demon, not the Dao." This chapter clarifies that those who have realized the Dao perceive no life or death to loathe or transcend.

Chapter Sixteen

Master Guan Yin said: Those who speculate on life and death hold various views. Some say that after death, one continues to exist; some say that after death, one ceases to exist; some say that after death, one both exists and does not exist. Those who recklessly speculate on life and death may claim that the body is like a rented dwelling—when the dwelling crumbles, the person remains; when the body dies, the nature endures. Thus it is said: some say that after death, one continues to exist.

Others may say: a person lives for a lifetime, as grass lives for a season; when a person dies, it is like a lamp extinguished—once dead, nothing remains. Thus it is said: some say that after death, one ceases to exist.

Still others may say: when a person dies, no trace is left, yet some spirits attach to others and communicate, leaving one to doubt whether death is both existence and non-existence. Thus it is said: some say that after death, one both exists and does not exist.

Some say one should rejoice in death; some say one should fear it; some say one should accept it; some say one should transcend it. Such shifting perceptions and restless emotions race on without end.

Some may say: in life, one is not numinous, but in death, one becomes so; in life, one finds no joy, but in death, one does. The spirit having form is like form having a wart—if the wart were absent, what could not be achieved? Thus is death not cause for rejoicing? Hence it is said: some say one should rejoice in death.

Others may say: when a person dies, they become a ghost, dwelling in endless darkness where the three luminaries do not shine, where no homeland offers shelter, where no familiar faces provide solace, where no food or drink sustains them. The dark, shadowy netherworld is truly fearful. Hence it is said: some say one should fear death.

Still others may say: life and death come and go in an instant—neither joy nor fear is fitting; one should simply let them follow their natural course. Hence it is said: some say one should accept death.

And some may say: transcending life and death is not difficult—with single-minded devotion to the Dao, one may directly transcend them. Hence it is said: some say one should transcend death.

The preceding reckless speculations on life and death—whether death entails existence, non-existence, or both, whether one should rejoice, fear, accept, or transcend it—these eight views only multiply deluded perceptions, causing the emotions to gallop ceaselessly. Hence it is said: such shifting perceptions and restless emotions race on without end.

Yet they do not realize that my life and death are like a horse’s hand or an ox’s wing—fundamentally non-existent, and thus neither existent nor non-existent. It is like fire and water: though one may encounter fire and water, they cannot burn or drown one.

Those who recklessly speculate on life and death do not realize that life and death are originally non-existent. Their speculations are like imagining a horse has hands or an ox has wings and clinging to such delusions. Horses and oxen naturally have no hands or wings, yet they imagine them to exist. This analogy reveals that life and death are fundamentally neither existent nor non-existent. Hence it is said: they do not realize that my life and death are like a horse’s hand or an ox’s wing—fundamentally non-existent, and thus neither existent nor non-existent.

The original true emptiness and wondrous existence are one with the great void. Emptiness merging with emptiness is like fire meeting fire—its brightness is not questioned, for fire does not burn fire. Water meeting water—its clarity is not questioned, for water does not drown water. Hence it is said: it is like fire and water—though one may encounter fire and water, they cannot burn or drown one.

This chapter clarifies that when perceptions and consciousness do not arise, life and death are fundamentally non-existent.

Chapter Seventeen concludes *Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing*, Volume Four.

**Chapter Eighteen**

1. "Five Duties" (*wudang*) is an error for "Five Constants" (*wuchang*).
2. "Abandoning resolve" (*qu yi*) is incorrect; both the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Wondrous Dao* read "the gate sheds its husk" (*men qu qiao*).
3. The passage beginning "Only water as a thing..." does not appear in either the *Guan Yinzi* or the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Wondrous Dao*.
4. This line should read: "Within humans, the Five Spirits become spirit" (*zai ren wu shen ze wei shen ye*); the characters "then the human Five Spirits" (*ze ren wu shen*) are superfluous.
5. "King Spirit" (*wang shen*) is an error for "Five Spirits" (*wu shen*).
6. This line appears in both the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Wondrous Dao* as: "All are gathered in a single breath" (*jie she zhi yi xi*).
7. This line appears in both the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Wondrous Dao* as: "Know that things are non-things" (*zhi wu wu wu*).
8. "Return to that" (*fan bi*) is likely an error for "returned to by" (*fan bei*).
9–10. These two lines appear in both the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Wondrous Dao* as: "Only the numerology of the Five Phases" (*wei wu xing zhi shu*).
11. Based on the preceding classical text, this line should read: "All share the same consciousness of arising" (*jie tong shi sheng ye*).
12. This line appears in the *Guan Yinzi* as: "The hun-soul is Wood" (*hun zhe mu ye*).
13. "As for forgetting essence" (*ruo fu wang jing*) appears in both the *Guan Yinzi* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Wondrous Dao* as: "As for forgetting spirit and essence" (*ruo fu wang jing shen*).
14. "What the eight people say" (*ba ren suo yan*) is likely an error for "what all people say" (*ren ren suo yan*).

*Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing*, Volume Five
Direct Explication by Shenfeng Xiaoyaozi Niu Daochun

**Chapter Five: The Five Mirrors**

A mirror is the heart; this chapter contains twenty sections.

Master Guan Yin said: When the heart is obscured by fortune and misfortune, numinous ghosts seize it. When the heart is obscured by male and female, lewd ghosts seize it. When the heart is obscured by hidden sorrow, drowning ghosts seize it. When the heart is obscured by reckless pursuit, mad ghosts seize it. When the heart is obscured by oaths and curses, strange ghosts seize it. When the heart is obscured by medicines and elixirs, material ghosts seize it.

Obscuration means fixation and delusion. When a person’s heart is singularly fixated on fortune and misfortune, the Five Phases, and yin and yang, then ghosts of numinous penetration seize and delude it—hence it is said, “When the heart is obscured by fortune and misfortune, numinous ghosts seize it.” When a person’s heart is singularly fixated on the entanglements of husband and wife, the arts of bedchamber union, and sexual combat, then lewd ghosts seize and delude it—hence it is said, “When the heart is obscured by male and female, lewd ghosts seize it.” When a person’s heart is singularly fixated on dark sorrow and melancholy, drowning ghosts seize and delude it—hence it is said, “When the heart is obscured by hidden sorrow, drowning ghosts seize it.” When a person’s heart is singularly fixated on reckless hunting and wandering, mad ghosts seize and delude it—hence it is said, “When the heart is obscured by reckless pursuit, mad ghosts seize it.” When a person’s heart is singularly fixated on oaths, vows, and curses, strange ghosts seize and delude it—hence it is said, “When the heart is obscured by oaths and curses, strange ghosts seize it.” When a person’s heart is singularly fixated on compounding and consuming medicines and elixirs, material ghosts seize and delude it—hence it is said, “When the heart is obscured by medicines and elixirs, material ghosts seize it.”

Such ghosts may take yin as their bodies, or darkness as their bodies, or wind as their bodies, or qi as their bodies, or clay effigies as their bodies, or painted images as their bodies, or old beasts as their bodies, or broken vessels as their bodies.

As stated above, the six kinds of ghosts—numinous, lewd, drowning, mad, strange, and material—seize and delude the six kinds of people fixated on obscurations. Thus it is said, “Such are these ghosts.”

Ghosts have no form or appearance; they must attach to things to take shape. Some attach to shadows to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take yin as their bodies.” Some attach to darkness to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take darkness as their bodies.” Some attach to whirlwinds to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take wind as their bodies.” Some attach to yin qi to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take qi as their bodies.” Some attach to clay effigies to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take clay effigies as their bodies.” Some attach to painted figures to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take painted images as their bodies.” Some attach to old beasts to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take old beasts as their bodies.” Some attach to broken vessels to form bodies and appear to delude people—hence it is said, “Some take broken vessels as their bodies.”

Theirs is their essence; ours is our essence. When the two essences clash, the spirit responds.

“Theirs” refers to the ghosts that attach to the eight things—yin, darkness, wind, qi, clay effigies, painted images, old beasts, and broken vessels. “Ours” refers to the six kinds of people whose hearts are obscured by fortune and misfortune, male and female, hidden sorrow, reckless pursuit, oaths and curses, and medicines and elixirs. When such people are singularly fixated on these six matters, their essence becomes unyielding. The essence of the attaching ghosts and the essence of the person clash, and the ghosts manifest numinous and wondrous responses. Thus it is said, “Theirs is their essence; ours is our essence. When the two essences clash, the spirit responds.”

Those seized by ghosts may explain strange matters, extraordinary matters, or auspicious matters. Such people grow arrogant, claiming not that ghosts possess them but that the Dao possesses them.

People whose hearts are fixated and deluded by the six ghosts—numinous, lewd, drowning, mad, strange, and material—may explain rare and wondrous matters, strange and uncanny matters, or auspicious and prophetic matters. Their predictions invariably come true, and the multitude reveres them as sages. Yet those who explain such strange and auspicious matters grow arrogant, refusing to admit that ghosts possess them, insisting instead that the supreme Dao has granted them such numinous penetration. Thus it is said, “Those seized by ghosts may explain strange matters, extraordinary matters, or auspicious matters. Such people grow arrogant, claiming not that ghosts possess them but that the Dao possesses them.”

In time, they may die by wood, die by metal, die by rope, or die by well.

Those seized by ghosts, who arrogantly believe themselves to have attained the Dao after explaining strange and auspicious matters, may in time die by crashing into trees, by cutting their throats with blades, by hanging themselves with ropes, or by throwing themselves into wells. Thus it is said, “In time, they may die by wood, die by metal, die by rope, or die by well.”

Only the sage can spiritually engage with spirits without being ensnared by them, commanding the myriad spirits while grasping their mechanism. They can gather them, disperse them, or master them, responding to the ten thousand things while their heart remains serene.

Only the sage who has attained the Dao, who thoroughly comprehends the wondrous interplay of emptiness and existence, who awakens to the spirit that is not spirit, whose yin and yang are unfathomable, whose transformations pervade the dust-like worlds, whose wondrous functions are boundless, and whose subtlety remains concealed—only such a sage can spiritually engage with spirits without being ensnared by them. Thus it is said, “Only the sage can spiritually engage with spirits without being ensnared by them.”

The enlightened sage who has attained the Dao can summon wind and rain, command the myriad spirits, and grasp their mechanism—*grasp* meaning to hold and master. Not only can such a sage summon wind and rain and command the myriad spirits, but they can also gather them when they may be assembled, disperse them when they may be divided, and restrain them when they may be controlled. Thus it is said, “They can gather them, disperse them, or master them.”

The heart of the enlightened sage is like the moon reflected in all waters, like the wind resounding through the myriad sounds, like a mirror suspended to reflect all forms, like an empty valley transmitting all echoes—responding to change while ever still and luminous, ever pure and tranquil. Thus it is said, “They respond to the ten thousand things while their heart remains serene.”

This chapter clarifies that any heart bound by attachments is a deluded heart, while the unbound heart is the Dao.

**Chapter One**
Master Guan Yin said: Do not fixate on oneness, for the five senses will race in parallel—if the heart cannot be one, it is because oneness cannot be fixed. Do not seek emptiness, for the five phases will all arise—if the heart cannot be empty, it is because emptiness cannot be forced. Do not cling to stillness, for the ten thousand transformations will shift imperceptibly—if the heart cannot be still, it is because stillness cannot be grasped.

*Do not* is a prohibitive term, for the wondrous heart is originally non-dual. It warns the learner not to single-mindedly pursue oneness. If the thought of fixating on oneness arises, that very thought will race alongside the five senses—sight, hearing, taste, breath, and thought—preventing true unity. Through this understanding, one knows the heart cannot be forced into oneness. Thus it is said, “Do not fixate on oneness, for the five senses will race in parallel—the heart cannot be one.”

The wondrous heart is originally empty of all things, equal to the great void. It warns the learner not to single-mindedly pursue emptiness. If the thought of forcing emptiness arises, it will align with the five phases—joy as fire, anger as wood, thought as earth, sorrow as metal, and fear as water—all arising together, preventing true emptiness. Through this understanding, one knows the heart cannot be forced into emptiness. Thus it is said, “Do not seek emptiness, for the five phases will all arise—the heart cannot be empty.”

The wondrous heart is originally pure and still, like empty space, without movement or disturbance. It warns the learner not to single-mindedly cling to stillness. If the thought of fixating on stillness arises, it will be swept away by the imperceptible shifts of the ten thousand transformations, preventing true stillness. Through this understanding, one knows the heart cannot be forced into stillness. Thus it is said, “Do not cling to stillness, for the ten thousand transformations will shift imperceptibly—the heart cannot be still.”

To borrow oneness is to create duality; to borrow emptiness is to invite fullness; to borrow stillness is to invite movement. The wondrous heart is originally non-dual. If one fixates on oneness, that fixation becomes a duality opposing the non-dual heart. Thus it is said, “To borrow oneness is to create duality,” explaining why the heart cannot be one.

The wondrous heart is originally empty of all things, equal to the great void. If one fixates on emptiness, that fixation fills and obstructs the empty heart. Thus it is said, “To borrow emptiness is to invite fullness,” explaining why the heart cannot be empty.

The wondrous heart is originally still, like empty space, without movement or disturbance. If one fixates on stillness, that fixation itself becomes movement, disturbing the heart. Thus it is said, “To borrow stillness is to invite movement,” explaining why the heart cannot be still.

Only the sage can gather the ten thousand existences into a single breath, so that not a single thing can obscure their clarity. They can disperse a single breath into the ten thousand existences, so that not a single thing can inquire into their actions.

The heart of the enlightened sage is like the great void, all-embracing. Their transformed bodies number in the billions, yet in a single breath, they return to the source—like all water-moons under the one true moon, which governs the flowing waves of water-moons without any water ever drowning the true moon’s clarity. Thus it is said, “Only the sage can gather the ten thousand existences into a single breath, so that not a single thing can obscure their clarity.”

The enlightened sage, in a single breath, scatters their transformed bodies throughout the dust-like worlds—like the one true moon appearing in all waters. The ceaseless flow of water-waves cannot interrupt the true moon’s luminous actions and radiance. Thus it is said, “They can disperse a single breath into the ten thousand existences, so that not a single thing can inquire into their actions.”

This chapter clarifies that the wondrous heart is originally non-dual, empty, and still—responding to change without hindrance.

**Chapter Two**
Master Guan Yin said: A fire may burn for a thousand years, yet it can be extinguished in an instant. Consciousness may persist for a thousand years, yet it can be dissolved in an instant.

Fire has no self; it takes form through oil and fuel. Though it may burn for a thousand years, if the oil and fuel are suddenly exhausted, the fire will be extinguished. Thus it is said, “A fire may burn for a thousand years, yet it can be extinguished in an instant.” This begins the analogy.

The deluded mind races with karmic consciousness, without any true foundation. Trapped in the cycle of rebirth for countless eons, it cannot escape—all due to this consciousness.

Consciousness also has no self; it arises dependent on conditions. Though it may persist for ten thousand years, if one is suddenly awakened by a teacher’s guidance and directly realizes the wondrous heart—originally pure and still, equal to the great void—then in an instant, conditions vanish and consciousness dissolves, like a fire extinguished when the fuel is gone. Thus it is said, “Consciousness may persist for a thousand years, yet it can be dissolved in an instant.” This completes the analogy.

This chapter clarifies that consciousness is the root of birth and death, and the key to escaping the cycle lies in forgetting conditions and dissolving consciousness.

**Chapter Three**
Master Guan Yin said: What flows is the boat, but what causes it to flow is the water, not the boat. What moves is the cart, but what causes it to move is the ox, not the cart. What thinks is the heart, but what causes it to think is intention, not the heart.

People see only the boat’s movement, unaware that what truly moves it is the flowing water—the boat cannot move itself. Thus it is said, “What flows is the boat, but what causes it to flow is the water, not the boat.”

People see only the cart’s movement, unaware that what truly moves it is the ox’s pulling— the cart cannot move itself. Thus it is said, “What moves is the cart, but what causes it to move is the ox, not the cart.” These two analogies of boat and cart are borrowed comparisons.

People know only the heart’s thoughts, unaware that what enables thought is the intention and consciousness— the inherently wondrous heart does not think of itself. Thus it is said, “What thinks is the heart, but what causes it to think is intention, not the heart.” This is the combined analogy.

Not knowing the cause yet seeing the effect, only by not knowing the cause can one see the effect. Thus its coming has no source, its going no abode. The inherently wondrous heart takes true emptiness as its essence and wondrous existence as its function. Essence and function are thus, responding to change without end. Spirits and ghosts cannot fathom it; yin and yang cannot measure it— not knowing the cause yet seeing the effect.

Not knowing the cause yet seeing the effect, the inherently wondrous heart originally existed before the Five Ultimates, prior to the appearance of the Lord of Creation. Radiantly clear and luminous, its coming is unknown— whence it arises. Profoundly hidden and obscure, its going is unknown— where it resides. Thus it is said, “Not knowing the cause yet seeing the effect.” Only by not knowing the cause can one see the effect, so its coming has no source, its going no abode.

Its coming has no source, its going no abode, thus it can unite with the primordial origin of heaven and earth, neither ancient nor modern. The preceding two lines mean that the inherently wondrous heart, not knowing whence it arises, profoundly hidden in its going, yet unknown in its abode— thus heaven and earth share the same origin with me. The wondrous heart gives rise to the primordial origin, yet the wondrous heart does not belong to time, hence neither ancient nor modern. Thus it is said, its coming has no source, its going no abode, so it can unite with the primordial origin of heaven and earth, neither ancient nor modern.

This chapter clarifies that the inherently wondrous heart is endowed from before the Five Ultimates, beyond the reach of discursive thought.

**Chapter Five**
Master Guan Yin said: Know that the heart is without thing, then know that things are without thing. Know that things are without thing, then know that the Dao is without thing. Know that the Dao is without thing, thus one does not esteem lofty and peerless conduct nor marvel at subtle and wondrous words.

The inherently wondrous heart takes the Dao as its essence and things as its function. Awakening to the heart of the Dao, it is fundamentally without a single thing, equal to empty space. Essence and function are thus, responding to change yet ever tranquil. Thus it is said, “Know that the heart is without thing, then know that things are without thing. Know that things are without thing, then know that the Dao is without thing.”

“Know” means awakening. “Things without thing” means responding to change yet ever tranquil. Penetratingly awakening to the heart of the Dao, its essence and function without thing, thus one does not esteem lofty and peerless conduct. Though conduct may be lofty and peerless, it does not depart from traces, yet the Dao is originally without trace, hence one does not esteem it. Nor does one marvel at subtle and wondrous words. Though words may be profound and wondrous, they do not depart from names, yet the Dao is originally without name, hence one does not marvel at them. Thus it is said, “Know that the Dao is without thing, thus one does not esteem lofty and peerless conduct nor marvel at subtle and wondrous words.”

This chapter clarifies that the heart of the Dao responds to change yet remains ever tranquil, beyond the reach of words and conduct.

**Chapter Six**
Master Guan Yin said: When self and things interact, the heart arises— just as fire is born from two sticks rubbing. The heart is originally without heart; it appears through things. When self and things interact, the heart becomes manifest, like fire born from two sticks rubbing together. Thus it is said, “When self and things interact, the heart arises; two sticks rubbing produce fire.”

It cannot be said to be within me, nor within that, nor as not-me, nor as not-that. To cling to self and other is foolish.

The self is originally without self; when things come to interact, the heart suddenly appears— it is not that the heart arises from the self. Thus it cannot be said to be within me. When things come to interact, though the heart appears, the heart is like empty space, unimpeded by those things. Thus it cannot be said to be within that. If there were no self, though things came to interact, the heart would not arise. Since the heart has arisen, it cannot be said to be not-me. Thus it cannot be said to be not-me. If that thing did not come to interact, the heart would also not arise. The heart does not originally arise; it arises through interaction. Thus it cannot be said to be not-that. Thus it cannot be said to be not-that.

Deluded people, not awakening to the absence of self and heart, unimpeded by things, falsely establish a self and set it against things. Clinging to self and other, they encounter obstacles with things— what is this if not foolishness? Thus it is said, “To cling to self and other is foolish.” “And” refers to you; clinging to self and other without forgetting is the mark of a deluded person.

This chapter clarifies that the heart is originally without self and other, unimpeded by things. Deluded people falsely establish self and other, setting them against things.

**Chapter Seven**
Master Guan Yin said: Do not rely on what you call benefit and harm, right and wrong. As for what you call benefit and harm, right and wrong— do they truly attain benefit and harm, right and wrong?

“Do not” is a prohibitive term; “rely” means to depend upon. It means one should not depend upon what you call benefit and harm, right and wrong. Thus it is said, “Do not rely on what you call benefit and harm, right and wrong.” What you call benefit and harm, right and wrong are all deluded feelings without measure. How can benefit be definitively benefit, harm definitively harm, right definitively right, wrong definitively wrong? Benefit and harm, right and wrong change with deluded feelings— how can they be fixed? Since they cannot be fixed, they are merely what deluded feelings calculate at the time— how can they truly attain benefit and harm, right and wrong? Thus it is said, “As for what you call benefit and harm, right and wrong— do they truly attain benefit and harm, right and wrong?”

The sage goes so far as to be without knowledge or awareness— how much more so for you?

The one who moves and transforms benefit and harm, right and wrong is the Creator. The Creator is profound and obscure—none can fathom its workings. Even the sage of great wisdom, who fully penetrates the profound obscurity of the Creator, goes so far as to be without knowledge or awareness—how much more so for you, an ordinary person? Thus it is said, “The sage goes so far as to be without knowledge or awareness—how much more so for you?” This chapter clarifies that benefit and harm, right and wrong are moved and transformed by the Creator, and the ordinary cannot predict them.

**Chapter Seven**

Master Guan Yin said: The dreams of the night may extend longer than the night itself. The heart has no time. "Longer than the night" should be understood as "longer than the day," which makes sense in meaning—this is likely an error in transcription. The deluded person, when dreaming at night, may dream of a lifetime passing in a day. From this, we know that the originally wondrous heart is not bound by the reckoning of time. Thus it is said, "The dreams of the night may extend longer than the day—the heart has no time."

Those born in Qi see only the land of Qi in their hearts. Later, when they go to Song, to Chu, to Jin, to Liang, what their hearts hold differs— the heart has no direction. There are those born and raised in the state of Qi, whose hearts see only the realm of Qi. "And" (而) means "you," and "go" (之) means "to go." Once you go to the state of Song, then to the three states of Chu, Jin, and Liang, the boundaries of the four states differ, and what the heart holds also differs. From this, we know that the originally wondrous heart is not bound by direction. Thus it is said, "Those born in Qi see only the land of Qi in their hearts. Later, when they go to Song, to Chu, to Jin, to Liang, what their hearts hold differs—the heart has no direction." This chapter clarifies that the originally wondrous heart is not bound by the reckoning of time or direction.

**Chapter Eight**

Master Guan Yin said: The skilled archer takes the bow as his teacher, not Yi. The skilled boatman takes the boat as his teacher, not Jie.

Yi was the title of an ancient master archer. In former times, Emperor Yao bestowed upon Yi a bow and arrows, appointing him as the official in charge of archery. He dwelled in the land of Qiongshi. By the time of Emperor Yao, ten suns appeared at once, and Yi was commanded to shoot them, striking nine and bringing them down. Later, he usurped the throne of King Xiang of Xia, becoming the ruler of the state of Youqiong, named after Yi. His minister, Han Zhuo, killed him, took his wife, and gave birth to Jie. Jie was immensely strong, able to push a boat across dry land. Later, he was killed by Shaokang, the son of King Xiang of Xia.

"Teacher" means "model." The skilled archer takes the bow as his model to hit the mark. Since hitting the mark is the principle, why take Yi as the model? Thus it is said, "The skilled archer takes the bow as his teacher, not Yi." The skilled boatman takes the boat as his model, ensuring smooth passage without obstruction. Since the boat’s unobstructed passage is the principle, there is no need to take Jie as the model. Thus it is said, "The skilled boatman takes the boat as his teacher, not Jie." This is an analogy.

The skilled heart takes the heart as its teacher, not the sage. One who skillfully awakens to the originally wondrous heart, which is inherently pure, still, and free, untainted and unattached, takes awakening as the principle—there is no need to take the sage as the model. Thus it is said, "The skilled heart takes the heart as its teacher, not the sage." This is the synthesis of the analogy. This chapter clarifies that the heart takes awakening as its principle, thereby transcending the nearness of the sage.

**Chapter Nine**

Master Guan Yin said: Right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, success and failure, fullness and emptiness—these are moved by the Creator, yet all arise from clinging to private perceptions.

Taking the good and beautiful as right and lovely, and the evil and base as wrong and ugly; taking the flourishing of things as success and fullness, and their decline and decay as failure and emptiness—how could one know that right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, success and failure, fullness and emptiness are all moved by the natural Creator? How could I presume to intervene? Yet the deluded cling to them as if they were real, not realizing they all arise from one’s own private, deluded perceptions. Thus it is said, "Right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, success and failure, fullness and emptiness—these are moved by the Creator, yet all arise from clinging to private perceptions."

Then, when one tries to dispel them with "non-being," the traces still remain. When one tries to dispel them with "neither being nor non-being," the traces still remain. There is no day when one does not think of "neither being," no day when one does not think of "neither non-being"—the traces remain, like revisiting a place one once traveled and recalling it vividly.

Once one knows that right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, success and failure, fullness and emptiness all arise from clinging to private perceptions, then when one tries to dispel these perceptions with "non-being," the perception of "non-being" still lingers. Thus it is said, "Then, when one tries to dispel them with 'non-being,' the traces still remain." When one further tries to dispel the perception of "non-being" with "neither being nor non-being," the perception of "neither being nor non-being" still lingers. Thus it is said, "When one tries to dispel them with 'neither being nor non-being,' the traces still remain." There is no day when one does not think of "neither being," no day when one does not think of "neither non-being"—the perception of a vague, undifferentiated state still lingers. Thus it is said, "There is no day when one does not think of 'neither being,' no day when one does not think of 'neither non-being'—the traces remain."

Trying to dispel perception with perception is like washing mud with mud. Trying to dispel perception with perception is like revisiting a place one once traveled and suddenly recalling it—the old perception remains vivid and cannot be forgotten. Thus it is said, "Like revisiting a place one once traveled and recalling it vividly."

This cannot be forgotten, nor can it be dispelled. One who skillfully eliminates perception transforms perception into wisdom. This perception cannot be forgotten, nor can it be dispelled. One who skillfully eliminates perception thoroughly understands the true emptiness of the heart’s essence, transforming perception into wisdom, which becomes the wondrous function of the heart. Thus it is said, "This cannot be forgotten, nor can it be dispelled. One who skillfully eliminates perception transforms perception into wisdom."

Do you understand the saying about transforming perception into wisdom?
He said: Imagining ghosts makes the heart tremble; imagining thieves makes the heart fear. Perception is like mistaking millet for sorghum, or jade for stone—all are floating, illusory images without foundation.

Here is the translation:

---

"Stable" means level and steady. Regarding the earlier explanation of transforming perception into wisdom—do you yet understand it? Thus it is said, "Do you understand the saying about transforming perception into wisdom?" This is a rhetorical question, to which the answer is given: "Perceiving ghosts is like a person imagining spirits in this place, causing the heart to shudder and tremble in fear; imagining bandits is like a person thinking of robbers here, causing the heart to quail and shrink in terror." Thus it is said, "Perception is like thinking of ghosts, and the heart trembles; thinking of bandits, and the heart fears." "Discernment" is like a person mistakenly taking millet for sorghum—though sorghum resembles millet, it is black—or like one mistakenly taking jade for stone. This deluded, false perception and discernment are all floating, illusory images without form, without any place of true awakening. The wondrous heart’s original essence is the realm of true emptiness, level and steady, where it comes to rest. Thus it is said, "Discernment is like taking millet for sorghum, jade for stone—all are floating, illusory images without any place to settle."

"Without" means non-existent; "settling" means coming to rest. For example, seeing a strange object gives rise to strange perceptions, which in turn give rise to strange discernments. These perceptions and discernments do not take root in me. As previously explained, perceptions and discernments are like a person encountering an unusual thing, which then generates perceptions and discernments of that thing. These strange perceptions do not originally exist in the heart; they arise only upon seeing the unusual thing. Thus it is said, "For example, seeing a strange object gives rise to strange perceptions, which give rise to strange discernments—these perceptions and discernments do not take root in me."

"Perception" is the heart’s thinking; "discernment" is the heart’s distinguishing and differentiating. For instance, today is merely today; as for tomorrow’s perceptions and discernments, they cannot yet be foretold. But when tomorrow arrives, the myriad perceptions and discernments arise in response to events. All are conditioned by arising. Earlier, it was said that these perceptions and discernments do not take root in me. Fearing that one might not fully believe this, another analogy is given to clarify: For example, from dawn to dusk today, the perceptions and discernments of today’s affairs are already known. As for tomorrow’s perceptions and discernments, they cannot be predicted or foreknown. Thus it is said, "For example, today is merely today; as for tomorrow’s perceptions and discernments, they cannot yet be foretold." When tomorrow arrives, perceptions and discernments arise in chaotic profusion in response to events, showing that perceptions and discernments do not take root in me but arise spontaneously in response to circumstances. Thus it is said, "When tomorrow arrives, the myriad perceptions and discernments all arise conditioned by existence." "Conditioned" means "because of."

Perception and discernment are like a rhinoceros gazing at the moon: the moon’s form enters its horn, and only because of discernment does the moon’s form appear. Yet the true moon was never in the horn. The wondrous heart originally has no perceptions or discernments. Fearing that one might not fully believe this, another analogy is given: For example, the rhinoceros’s horn originally has no moon’s form; only because the rhinoceros gazes at the moon does the perception and discernment of the moon arise, and the moon’s form enters the horn. Yet the true moon in the sky was never in the horn. Thus it is said, "Perception and discernment are like a rhinoceros gazing at the moon: the moon’s form enters the horn, and only because of discernment does the moon’s form appear. Yet the true moon was never in the horn."

The myriad things in the heart’s realm are likewise. One who understands this teaching sees nothing externally and feels no emotions internally. The perceptions of the myriad things in the heart’s realm are like the rhinoceros gazing at the moon, with the moon’s form entering its horn. Thus it is said, "The myriad things in the heart’s realm are likewise." One who comprehends the above analogies externally sees no things and internally feels no emotions. This chapter clarifies that the wondrous heart originally has no emotions or discernments; all arise from the heart’s delusion, which falsely generates emotions and discernments.

**Chapter Ten**
Guan Yinzi said: Things arise from earth and ultimately return to earth. Affairs arise from intention and ultimately return to intention. The myriad things arise from earth, and when they finally perish, they transform back into earth. Thus it is said, "Things arise from earth and ultimately return to earth." This is the analogy. Earth corresponds to the intention of the spleen’s spirit. The myriad affairs arise from intention, and when all are forgotten, they ultimately return to intention. Thus it is said, "Affairs arise from intention and ultimately return to intention." This is the application of the analogy.

Knowing that all depends on intention, then in an instant it affirms, in an instant it denies, in an instant it deems good, in an instant it deems evil. Intention changes, but the heart does not change; intention is aware, but the heart is not aware. As explained in the above analogies, one knows that the existence or non-existence of all affairs depends solely on this root of intention. Knowing the affairs generated by this root of intention, in a fleeting moment, non-being transforms into being, being into non-being, evil into good, good into evil. Thus it is said, "Knowing that all depends on intention, then in an instant it affirms, in an instant it denies, in an instant it deems good, in an instant it deems evil." The root of intention undergoes change, but the wondrous heart originally does not change; the root of intention has awareness, but the wondrous heart originally has no awareness. Thus it is said, "Intention changes, but the heart does not change; intention is aware, but the heart is not aware."

Only my singular heart exists, for intention is merely the coming and going of dust, and affairs are merely the swift arising and ceasing of phenomena. My heart abides in the great constancy. The wondrous heart is originally non-dual, thus it is said, "Only my singular heart exists." "Dust" refers to phenomena. The root of intention can generate and extinguish the myriad phenomena: when extinguished, it departs; when generated, it arrives. Thus it is said, "Intention is merely the coming and going of dust." If the root of intention does not generate affairs, it is like fire burning firewood: it flares up swiftly, and when the firewood is consumed, it swiftly ceases. Thus it is said, "Affairs are merely the swift arising and ceasing of phenomena." "Swift" means rapid. The "great constancy" is the true emptiness of the wondrous heart’s essence, boundless and without limit, transcending past and present without change. What else could abide but this? Thus it is said, "My heart abides in the great constancy."

This chapter elucidates the great constancy of the heart governing the minor transformations of intention.

**Chapter Eleven**
Master Guan Yin said: Emotions arise from the heart, the heart arises from nature. Emotions are waves, the heart is the current, nature is water. Human delusion and emotions are born from the heart, and the heart is born from nature—thus it is said, "Emotions arise from the heart, the heart arises from nature." When people are deluded, emotions resemble the waves of water; the heart that pursues external states is like flowing water; the original nature of humans is like the source of water. Waves, currents, and source have three names but no dual substance, for waves, currents, and source are all water. Emotions, heart, and nature have three names but no dual substance, for emotions, heart, and nature are all true—thus it is said, "Emotions are waves, the heart is the current, nature is water."

What comes to disturb me is like the fleeting spark of flint. If received with nature, the heart does not arise, and phenomena float lightly. When the myriad things come to disturb me, it is but a momentary flash, like the spark of flint or lightning. If received with the true emptiness of nature, the heart does not arise, and the perception of phenomena floats lightly, like clouds in the vast sky, unobstructing the void, or like an echo in an empty valley, responding yet ever tranquil—thus it is said, "What comes to disturb me is like the fleeting spark of flint. If received with nature, the heart does not arise, and phenomena float lightly."

This chapter elucidates the non-duality of heart and nature, responding without obstruction.

**Chapter Twelve**
Master Guan Yin said: Worthies and fools, the true and the false—some recognize them, some do not. In the world, there are true worthies and true fools, false worthies and false fools. Some people can discern them, while others cannot—thus it is said, "Worthies and fools, the true and the false—some recognize them, some do not." Though they may be worthy or foolish, true or false, what is called worthy or foolish, true or false, is bound to my perception. Though the world may have worthies and fools, the true and the false, the distinction between them depends on my deluded perception—thus it is said, "Though they may be worthy or foolish, true or false, what is called worthy or foolish, true or false, is bound to my perception." Knowing that all are formed by perception, even what is true is thereby false. If one knows that the distinction between worthies and fools, the true and the false, is formed by deluded perception, and perception itself is delusion, then even what is true is named false and unreal—thus it is said, "Knowing that all are formed by perception, even what is true is thereby false."

This chapter elucidates that deluded perception is not the true heart.

**Chapter Thirteen**
Master Guan Yin said: When the heart responds to phenomena, it does not generate the heart but generates emotions. When phenomena interact with the heart, they do not generate phenomena but generate perception. Phenomena are not yet real—how much less perception? Perception is not yet real—how much less emotions? The originally wondrous heart is inherently pure and still. When moved by phenomena, the heart itself does not arise—what arises are emotions—thus it is said, "When the heart responds to phenomena, it does not generate the heart but generates emotions." When all phenomena come to interact with the heart, what the heart transforms in response to phenomena is not phenomena but the heart’s perception—thus it is said, "When phenomena interact with the heart, they do not generate phenomena but generate perception." All phenomena are illusory arising and ceasing, still fundamentally unreal—how much more perception? How could it not be delusion and falsehood?—thus it is said, "Phenomena are not yet real—how much less perception?" Since perception is delusion and falsehood, how much more emotions? How could they not be delusion?—thus it is said, "Perception is not yet real—how much less emotions?"

Yet those deluded people, in the midst of utmost emptiness, cling to it as existence; in the midst of utmost change, they cling to it as constancy. Those deluded and confused people, in the midst of the originally true and wondrous heart where not a single thing exists, suddenly generate deluded perception, stubbornly grasping it as real, clinging and unable to relinquish, failing to illuminate the original heart—thus it is said, "Yet those deluded people, in the midst of utmost emptiness, cling to it as existence." Those deluded and confused people, in the midst of perception that ceaselessly arises and ceases, chasing external states and endlessly transforming, stubbornly grasp it as the great constancy of the original heart—alas! Little do they know that this deluded perception is the seed of samsara—thus it is said, "In the midst of utmost change, they cling to it as constancy."

One deluded emotion, when recognized, accumulates into ten thousand emotions; ten thousand emotions, when recognized, accumulate into ten thousand things. Phenomena come endlessly, yet my heart has bounds. From the originally pure and wondrous heart, a single deluded emotion suddenly arises, unrecognized as false and taken as real. Emotions then multiply endlessly, one after another, accumulating into billions of emotions—thus it is said, "One deluded emotion, when recognized, accumulates into ten thousand emotions." The deluded person, amidst billions of emotions, still fails to recognize their falsity, taking delusion as truth, clinging to phenomena, thoughts shifting ceaselessly, waves of emotions vast and boundless, accumulating into billions of phenomena—thus it is said, "Ten thousand emotions, when recognized, accumulate into ten thousand things." Bounds refer to boundaries. The originally wondrous heart is like empty space, boundless and without limit. Yet deluded perception and emotions usurp control, falsely dividing the void, establishing boundaries and limits, deludedly clinging to the myriad phenomena, endlessly chasing their transformations—thus it is said, "Phenomena come endlessly, yet my heart has bounds."

Thus my good heart is controlled by emotions, and my original emotions are controlled by phenomena. They can be made to depart, they can be made to arrive. The heart, deluded and chasing emotions, establishes boundaries, not following the enlightened and wondrous heart but driven solely by deluded emotions—thus the originally good and wondrous heart is instead controlled by deluded emotions, like a minister rebelling to control the ruler—thus it is said, "Thus my good heart is controlled by emotions." My original true emotions, once deluded and clinging to the myriad phenomena, are instead driven by them—thus the original true emotions are controlled by phenomena, like the people rebelling to control the minister—thus it is said, "My original emotions are controlled by phenomena."

The heart, deluded, chases emotions; emotions, deluded, cling to phenomena. Deluded emotions falsely cling to the realm of phenomena, binding one until death, then drawn by the pull of love and attachment to phenomena, reborn where attachment is heaviest. Thus, life after life, death after death, for hundreds of thousands of kalpas in the wheel of rebirth, with no end in sight, ceaselessly departing and arriving—hence it is said, "They can be made to depart, they can be made to arrive."

Yet this departing and arriving does not originate from me; the workings of creation enslave them, without respite. The five emotions of humans correspond to the five phases: joy belongs to fire, anger to wood, thought to earth, sorrow to metal, and fear to water. Since the five emotions correspond to the five phases, and the five phases with yin and yang create and shape the myriad things of heaven and earth, they drive and enslave the cycles of death and life, departure and arrival, without end. Investigating the cause, all this arises from the delusion of the five emotions—had the five emotions not yet arisen, how could there be death and life, departure and arrival? From this, one knows that this departing and arriving does not originate from me but arises from deluded emotions falsely clinging, falling into the molding of the five phases and creation, enslaved in the wheel of rebirth without respite—hence it is said, "Yet this departing and arriving does not originate from me; the workings of creation enslave them, without respite."

Little does one know that though heaven and earth are vast, they can enslave the formed but cannot enslave the formless; though yin and yang are wondrous, they can enslave the qi-endowed but cannot enslave the qi-less. The deluded, falsely clinging to realms of attachment, are enslaved by the workings of heaven and earth and yin and yang, endlessly revolving—yet little do they know that before deluded emotions arose, the originally wondrous heart was like empty space, formless. Though heaven and earth and the workings of yin and yang are vast, they can only enslave deluded, formed emotions—how could they enslave the wondrous heart like empty space? Hence it is said, "Little does one know that though heaven and earth are vast, they can enslave the formed but cannot enslave the formless."

Once deluded emotions arise, primordial qi follows, and one falls into the enslavement of yin-yang creation and the wheel of rebirth. Before deluded emotions arise, primordial qi returns to its root, becoming one with true emptiness—though yin and yang and creation are wondrous, how could they enslave true emptiness? Hence it is said, "Though yin and yang are wondrous, they can enslave the qi-endowed but cannot enslave the qi-less."

Where the heart goes, qi follows; where qi goes, form responds. "Goes" means to proceed. Where the heart proceeds, qi follows in proceeding—like a general, the heart commands, and qi, like an army, obeys, following the general. Thus it is said, "Where the heart goes, qi follows." When the heart is deluded by false love, clinging to various realms, qi follows, giving rise to this rebirth-bound body and form—hence it is said, "Where qi goes, form responds."

It is like the great void: from perfect nothingness, it transforms into one qi; within that one qi, it transforms into the myriad things. Yet that one qi is not called the great void. The Dao is originally nameless and formless, like the great void, yet it moves one qi. One qi divides into yin and yang, and from yin and yang, heaven and earth and the myriad things transform. Once named one qi, it is no longer called the great void—what is formless is named the Dao, let alone the myriad things. Hence it is said, "It is like the great void: from perfect nothingness, it transforms into one qi; within that one qi, it transforms into the myriad things. Yet that one qi is not called the great void." This is the analogy.

My single heart can transform into qi and form, yet my heart is without qi and without form. My originally wondrous heart takes true emptiness as its essence. Before thoughts arise, its essence is like the great void; once thoughts arise, where it goes, qi follows, and where qi goes, form responds—like the great void transforming into one qi, and within that one qi, transforming into the myriad things. Hence it is said, "My single heart can transform into qi and form." Before thoughts arise, the originally wondrous heart is fundamentally without qi and without form—hence it is said, "Yet my heart is without qi and without form."

Knowing that my single heart is without qi and without form, then heaven and earth and yin and yang cannot enslave it. "Knowing" means awakening. Awakening to the originally non-dual wondrous heart is like the great void before it transformed into qi and form, before there were heaven and earth and yin and yang—whom could they enslave? Even if there were heaven and earth and yin and yang, how could they enslave empty space? Hence it is said, "Knowing that my single heart is without qi and without form, then heaven and earth and yin and yang cannot enslave it."

This chapter clarifies that when the heart is deluded and falls, it is molded by yin and yang and ceaselessly revolves; when the heart awakens and transcends the molding of yin and yang, it is forever free from the wheel of rebirth.

**Chapter Fourteen**

Guan Yinzi said: In a person’s ordinary days, if the eyes suddenly see extraordinary things, it is always because essence has congealed with them to bring about such changes. In a person’s ordinary, peaceful days, if the eyes suddenly see extraordinary and strange phenomena, it is always because spirit has congealed with those phenomena, suddenly causing such strange transformations—hence it is said, "In a person’s ordinary days, if the eyes suddenly see extraordinary things, it is always because essence has congealed with them to bring about such changes."

In a person’s sick days, if the eyes suddenly see extraordinary things, it is always because the heart is deficient and brings about such changes. In a person’s feverish, ailing days, burned by heat and seeing illusions, if the eyes suddenly see extraordinary and strange phenomena, it is always because the heat of illness burns and the heart’s deficiency causes it—hence it is said, "In a person’s sick days, if the eyes suddenly see extraordinary things, it is always because the heart is deficient and brings about such changes." "Deficient" means insufficient.

If one knows that my heart can manifest being from non-being, then one knows that my heart can manifest non-being from being.

If "if" means recognition, then "know" means awakening. As stated above, whether in ordinary days or sick days, when the eyes suddenly see extraordinary things, one truly awakens to the fact that the wondrous original heart is fundamentally empty of any thing, equal to the great void—all extraordinary existences are manifested from non-being. Through this awakening, one realizes that even these extraordinary existences reveal the wondrous heart originally empty of things. Thus it is said: "If one knows that my heart can manifest being from non-being, then one knows that my heart can manifest non-being from being."

Yet if one does not believe, it naturally loses its numinosity. Some may ask: "If one’s consciousness is already clouded, who can avoid belief?" I reply: Just as a snake-catcher’s heart does not fear snakes, though he dreams of them, he feels no dread. If in ordinary days or sick days the eyes suddenly see extraordinary ghosts, spirits, or strange phenomena, one should awaken to the fact that the original heart is fundamentally pure and empty of any thing—all forms of existence are illusory. Thus one becomes clear and does not believe or cling, and those strange phenomena naturally lose their numinous power. Hence it is said: "If one does not believe, it naturally loses its numinosity." "Clouded" means obscured; "who" means which person. Someone may ask: "If one’s consciousness is already darkened and deluded, seeing extraordinary spirits and strange phenomena, who can avoid belief?" This is a rhetorical question. The answer is: Just as a snake-catcher, who uses methods to control snakes, is unafraid when catching them, though he dreams of snakes in sleep, he feels no fear. Through this clarity, one knows that if one does not believe, it naturally loses its numinosity. Thus it is said: "I reply: Just as a snake-catcher’s heart does not fear snakes, though he dreams of them, he feels no dread." This answers the above question.

Therefore, the Yellow Emperor said: "The Dao has no ghosts or spirits; it moves alone, comes and goes alone." Using the snake-catcher as a metaphor to answer the questioner, fearing they might not fully grasp it, the text also cites the words recorded in the Yellow Emperor’s book as evidence. In the Yellow Emperor’s book, it is said that the Dao has no ghosts or spirits—ghosts belong to yin, spirits to yang, but the wondrous Dao does not belong to yin or yang, hence it has no ghosts or spirits. Even if ghosts and spirits exist, they cannot fathom the wondrous Dao. The wondrous Dao operates and turns, with yin and yang coming and going, yet yin and yang cannot pair with the Dao. Thus it is said: "Therefore, the Yellow Emperor said: 'The Dao has no ghosts or spirits; it moves alone, comes and goes alone.'" This chapter clarifies that by following the flow, one attains the wondrous heart and awakens to the wondrous Dao, which ghosts and spirits cannot measure.

**Chapter Fifteen**
Guan Yinzi said: "My thoughts change daily, driven by something—not by me, but by fate. If one knows it is only fate, externally one does not see the self, and internally one does not see the heart." I am originally without self, but false consciousness establishes it. Thoughts bustle about, changing daily in myriad ways—this is not the self without self, but the transformations of fate, which arise without knowing why. Thus it is said: "My thoughts change daily, driven by something—not by me, but by fate." "Fate" means arising without knowing why; "driven" means transformation. If one truly awakens to the fact that things arise without knowing why, this is the Dao of true emptiness. Fate is another name for the Dao. Awakening to the Dao of true emptiness, externally there is no self to be seen, and internally there is no heart to be seen. Thus it is said: "If one knows it is only fate, externally one does not see the self, and internally one does not see the heart." This chapter clarifies that the heart is originally empty, and the Dao cannot be reached by thoughts.

**Chapter Sixteen**
Guan Yinzi said: "For example, the two eyes can see all things under heaven, but if the light is momentarily withdrawn, nothing is seen." The true empty essence of the heart is fundamentally without a single thing, profound and unfathomable. When false thoughts suddenly arise, what is seen becomes clear. Returning the light to true emptiness is like a person’s two eyes, which can see the forms of all things under heaven, but if the gaze is momentarily withdrawn, nothing is seen. Thus it is said: "For example, the two eyes can see all things under heaven, but if the light is momentarily withdrawn, nothing is seen." This chapter explains the previous chapter’s statement that thoughts change daily, driven by fate.

**Chapter Seventeen**
Guan Yinzi said: "The eyes that gaze upon carvings injure their clarity; the ears that hear harmonious sounds injure their acuity; the heart that contemplates the mysterious injures itself." Eyes that see clearly are called "bright"; if they fixate on intricate carvings of gold, jade, or fine patterns for too long, their brightness is increasingly harmed. The scriptures say: "The five colors blind the eyes." Thus it is said: "The eyes that gaze upon carvings injure their clarity." Ears that hear clearly are called "acute"; if they fixate on the harmonious sounds of the five tones for too long, their acuity is increasingly harmed. The scriptures say: "The five sounds deafen the ears." Thus it is said: "The ears that hear harmonious sounds injure their acuity." The mysterious and wondrous are dust. The heart is fundamentally pure and empty of any dharma—this is the source of the mysterious. Not awakening to the original heart, one fixates on the mysterious teachings of the ancients, not realizing that these mysterious teachings are all dust and defilement, increasingly polluting and harming the heart. The scriptures say: "Cleanse the mysterious mirror." Thus it is said: "The heart that contemplates the mysterious injures itself." This chapter clarifies that the wondrous original heart does not belong to the six dusts.

**Chapter Eighteen**
Guan Yinzi said: "Do not measure others with your own heart; measure others with their own hearts." "Do not" is a prohibitive term; "measure" means to estimate or gauge. This admonishes those who study the Dao and wander in the world: do not use your own heart to estimate others. When employing people, one should respond to their worthiness with worthiness and to their foolishness with foolishness. In this way, one advances or retreats with others without losing their hearts. Thus it is said: "Do not measure others with your own heart; measure others with their own hearts."

One who comprehends this teaching may perfect all affairs, practice virtue universally, penetrate the Dao, befriend all people, and forget the self. When one knows this heartless state and responds to others’ worthiness or foolishness with their own measure—advancing or retreating without intent—then worthiness and foolishness, advance and retreat, arise without losing the hearts of others. Thus, what affair would not be perfectly managed? What virtue would not be universally practiced? What Dao would not be fully penetrated? What person would not become a friend? What self would not be forgotten? For in the single method of illuminating the heart, all is perfectly good and beautiful. Therefore it is said: "One who comprehends this teaching may perfect all affairs, practice virtue universally, penetrate the Dao, befriend all people, and forget the self." This chapter clarifies that dwelling in the world without intent brings self-benefit and benefits others, and all excellences follow therefrom.

**Chapter Nineteen**

Guan Yinzi said: "The principles of the world begin with the small that, if unchecked, grow to the great; the great, if unchecked, grow to the ungovernable." All matters and principles under heaven, like water and fire, if a trickle is not stopped, gradually becomes a river; if a spark is not extinguished, it blazes beyond control. Just as stacked wood forms a forest and piled stones become a mountain, accumulated evil becomes disaster—how can one not be cautious! Therefore it is said: "The principles of the world begin with the small that, if unchecked, grow to the great; the great, if unchecked, grow to the ungovernable."

Thus, one who can govern a single emotion may cultivate virtue; one who can forget a single emotion may merge with the Dao. "Thus" connects the preceding to what follows; "govern" means to regulate. If one can regulate an emotion at its first arising, directing it toward goodness, one may thereby perfect virtue—hence it is said: "One who can govern a single emotion may cultivate virtue." If one can clearly perceive and dissolve an emotion at its first arising, one merges with the boundless wondrous Dao—hence it is said: "One who can forget a single emotion may merge with the Dao." This chapter clarifies that the distinction between the ordinary and the sage lies in the delusion or awakening of a single emotion.

**End of Wenzhi Zhenjing Zhu, Scroll Five**

**Wenzhi Zhenjing Zhu, Scroll Six**
*Direct Explication by Shenfeng Xiaoyaozi Niu Daochun*

**Scroll Six of the Wenzhi Zhenjing Zhu**
*Direct Explication by Shenfeng Xiaoyaozi Niu Daochun*

**Chapter Six: The Ladle**

The ladle (匕) is for eating; eating sustains form. This chapter comprises sixteen sections.

Master Guan Yin said: The people of the world distinguish self-thought from other-thought, other-thought from self-thought, thereby dividing self and other. Yet they do not realize that in dreams, too, self-thought differs from other-thought, and other-thought differs from self-thought—who then is the self, and who the other? "Other" refers to people.

The deluded of the world take their own thoughts as distinct from others' thoughts, believing others' thoughts differ from their own. Thus they separate self and other, vainly generating views. Yet they do not know that such vain distinctions of thought between self and other are but the celestial truth following deluded thoughts to fabricate dreams. Not only are nighttime sleep-dreams dreams—even in broad daylight, when nature follows deluded thoughts, all is dream. How can one claim that the thoughts and dreams of the dreaming differ from the objects of thought and dream, when the very act of thinking and dreaming remains unchanged? From this, who is the self, and who the other?

Thus it is said: The people of the world distinguish self-thought from other-thought, other-thought from self-thought—who then is the self, and who the other?

The people of the world distinguish their own pain from others' pain, others' pain from their own, thereby dividing self and other. Yet they do not realize that in dreams, too, their own pain differs from others' pain, and others' pain differs from their own—who then is the self, and who the other?

The deluded of the world take their own pain as distinct. For instance, when two people receive moxibustion, though the sites differ, the pain is the same. How can one vainly distinguish self and other by pain? Yet they do not know that in dreams, too, pain is taken as distinct. Upon awakening, however, there is no other person. Thus one realizes that dream-pain is deluded perception, and daytime pain is likewise deluded perception. From this, who is the self, and who the other?

Thus it is said: The people of the world distinguish their own pain from others' pain, others' pain from their own, thereby dividing self and other. Yet they do not realize that in dreams, too, their own pain differs from others' pain, and others' pain differs from their own—who then is the self, and who the other?

Nails and hair feel no pain; hands and feet form no thoughts—yet they are also the self. How can one distinguish self and other by thought and pain?

Having first explained that thought and pain are equally illusory and indistinct, lest one fail to believe, further analogies are given for clarity. For example, a person’s nails and hair feel no pain when cut, and hands and feet form no thoughts—yet nails, hair, hands, and feet all belong to the self. How can one vainly distinguish self and other by thought and pain?

Thus it is said: Nails and hair feel no pain; hands and feet form no thoughts—yet they are also the self. How can one distinguish self and other by thought and pain?

The people of the world take solitary visions as dreams and shared visions as wakefulness. Yet they do not know that when essence congeals, there are those who alone see visions in broad daylight; when spirits merge, there are those who share dreams at night. Both arise from the self’s spirit—who then is dreaming, and who is awake?

The deluded of the world take visions seen alone, unseen by others, as dreams, and visions seen by all as wakefulness. Thus it is said: The people of the world take solitary visions as dreams and shared visions as wakefulness.

Yet they do not know that when a person’s essence congeals with things, even in broad daylight, extraordinary visions may appear, unseen by others, visible only to the self. From this, one knows that not only night-dreams are dreams. Thus it is said: Yet they do not know that when essence congeals, there are those who alone see visions in broad daylight.

When one’s spirit merges with another’s, two people may share a dream at night. From this, one knows that shared visions are not necessarily wakefulness. Thus it is said: When spirits merge, there are those who share dreams at night.

Essence congealing produces solitary visions; spirits merging produce shared visions. Both arise from the self’s spirit transforming, vainly generating distinctions of sameness and difference. From this, who is dreaming, and who is awake? Dreams are deluded dreams; wakefulness is deluded wakefulness. Both dream and wakefulness are delusion—what distinction is there between self and other?

Thus it is said: Both arise from the self’s spirit—who then is dreaming, and who is awake?

The people of the world take fleeting visions as dreams and enduring visions as wakefulness. Yet they do not know that fleeting visions arise from the qi of yin and yang, and enduring visions likewise arise from the qi of yin and yang. Both arise from the self’s yin and yang—who then is dreaming, and who is awake?

The deluded of the world take nightly fleeting visions as dreams and daily enduring visions as wakefulness. Thus it is said: The people of the world take fleeting visions as dreams and enduring visions as wakefulness.

Yet they do not know that the fleeting visions of night-dreams are transformations of the spirit, hun, po, and the qi of yin and yang, while the enduring visions of daylight are likewise deluded perceptions of form and dust arising from the spirit, hun, po, and the qi of yin and yang. Thus it is said: Yet they do not know that fleeting visions arise from the qi of yin and yang, and enduring visions likewise arise from the qi of yin and yang.

Fleeting and enduring visions both arise from the transformations of the spirit and the qi of yin and yang. Since perceptions are deluded, neither wakefulness nor dreams are real. From this, who is dreaming, and who is awake? Only one who has attained true wakefulness knows this deluded wakefulness and dreaming.

Thus it is said: Both arise from the self’s yin and yang—who then is dreaming, and who is awake?

This chapter clarifies that the deluded take both dreams and wakefulness as delusion.

**First Section**
Master Guan Yin said: Those who delight in benevolence often dream of pines, wutong, peaches, and plums; those who delight in righteousness often dream of weapons, blades, gold, and iron; those who delight in propriety often dream of ritual vessels and sacrificial stands; those who delight in wisdom often dream of rivers, lakes, streams, and marshes; those who delight in trustworthiness often dream of mountains, peaks, plains, and fields. Bound by the five phases, there has never been an exception.

**Second Section**

Virtue pertains to wood; those who devote themselves to benevolence often dream in their spirit-awareness of pines, wutong, peaches, and plums—hence it is said that those who delight in benevolence often dream of pines, wutong, peaches, and plums. Righteousness pertains to metal; those who devote themselves to righteousness often dream in their spirit-awareness of weapons, blades, gold, and iron—hence it is said that those who delight in righteousness often dream of weapons, blades, gold, and iron. Propriety pertains to fire; propriety is ritual order. Those who devote themselves to ritual conduct often dream in their spirit-awareness of raging wildfires or of sacrificial vessels and ritual implements—hence it is said that those who delight in propriety often dream of *gai*, *jian*, *peng*, and *dou*. The *gai* is made of bamboo, round both inside and out; the *jian* is made of bamboo, round outside and square within; the *peng* is square outside and round within, used to hold dry offerings; the *dou* is made of wood, used to hold wet offerings. All four are lacquered red, corresponding to the color of fire. Wisdom pertains to water; those who devote themselves to wisdom to discern truth and falsehood often dream in their spirit-awareness of rivers, lakes, and marshes—hence it is said that those who delight in wisdom often dream of rivers, lakes, streams, and marshes.
