Trustworthiness pertains to earth; those who devote themselves to sincerity and truthfulness often dream in their spirit-awareness of mountains, peaks, plains, and fields—hence it is said that those who delight in trustworthiness often dream of mountains, peaks, plains, and fields. Thus, those who devote themselves to benevolence (wood), righteousness (metal), propriety (fire), wisdom (water), and trustworthiness (earth) are bound by the Five Constants and dream of these Five Phases in their partiality—hence it is said, "thus."

In dreams, one may hear of some matter or think of some matter, and the dream follows accordingly—the Five Phases cannot be rigidly confined. By day, one indulges in deluded thoughts; by night, in deluded dreams. In the midst of dreaming, one may suddenly hear of some matter or think of some matter, and as the mind chases after shifting circumstances, the dream follows accordingly. This is because the mind is partial to the Five Phases; if the mind were not partial, the Five Phases could not be rigidly confined. Hence it is said: "In dreams, one may hear of some matter or think of some matter, and the dream follows accordingly—the Five Phases cannot be rigidly confined."

The sage governs things with the mind and restrains the mind with nature, so that the mind merges with the workings of creation, and the Five Phases cannot be rigidly confined. To "govern things" is to employ them. The sage takes the true emptiness of nature as the substance of the mind and the wondrous existence of things as its function. Substance and function are thus in harmony, responding to change while remaining ever tranquil. Above, the sage roams with the Creator; below, befriends those beyond birth and death, without beginning or end. Thus, yin and yang and the Five Phases cannot be rigidly confined. "Creation" refers to the Creator. Hence it is said: "The sage governs things with the mind and restrains the mind with nature, so that the mind merges with the workings of creation, and the Five Phases cannot be rigidly confined." To "govern things with the mind" is to employ the mind in governing things as its function; to "restrain the mind with nature" is to return the mind to the true emptiness of nature as its substance.

This chapter clarifies that when the mind is deluded, it is bound by the Five Phases; when the mind is enlightened, it transcends the confinement of the Five Phases.

**Third Section**

Master Guan Yin said: When you see those with the head of a serpent and the body of a human, or the arms of an ox and the scales of a fish, or the form of a ghost and the wings of a bird, do not be astonished. "You" refers to people in the world. Among the people of the world, some may see humans born with heads resembling serpents and human bodies, or arms like oxen with scales like fish, or forms like ghosts with wings like birds under their arms—let the people of the world not be astonished. For instance, the ancient Fu Xi had the body of a serpent and the head of a human; Shen Nong had the head of an ox and the body of a human; and the ghost-faced General Cen Peng—these are well known in the Central Kingdom. As Dongfang Shuo recorded of the barbarian peoples: In the northwest wastes, there are humans whose faces, eyes, hands, and feet resemble ours, yet they have wings under their arms and cannot fly. In the southwest wastes, there are humans with body hair and pig heads. In the northeast wastes, there are humans with red hair, serpent bodies, and human faces, but no hands or feet. In the southern wastes, there are humans with beaks like crows, wings, and the ability to fly, but no feet. In the western wastes, there are humans like tigers, with long fur, human faces, tiger feet, and wolf teeth, with tails eighteen feet long. In the northwest wastes, there are also humans shaped like tigers who eat people, with wings and the ability to fly, who understand human speech. All these arise from the chaotic and perverse qi of yin and yang, resulting in extraordinary forms—how could they be considered strange? Hence it is said: "When you see those with the head of a serpent and the body of a human, or the arms of an ox and the scales of a fish, or the form of a ghost and the wings of a bird, do not be astonished."

Such anomalies are not as strange as dreams; the strangeness of dreams is not as strange as wakefulness. With ears and eyes, hands and arms, the strangeness is even greater. The extraordinary forms mentioned above are not worth marveling at—such anomalies are not as strange as dreams. Consider this single person: when asleep, they dream of all manner of strange realms—people, heaven and earth, mountains and rivers, grasses and trees, towers and pavilions, birds and beasts—sometimes auspicious and wondrous, yet upon waking, all is empty. What is this thing that can conjure such dream realms? This is truly strange—hence it is said, "such anomalies are not as strange as dreams." Upon waking, one sees a person as a lump of flesh, with ears that can hear sounds, eyes that can see colors, hands that can grasp objects, and arms that can move—yet without any threads to pull them, who is in control? How can they act so precisely and appropriately? This strangeness is even greater, yet the people of the world do not find it strange. Hence it is said: "The strangeness of dreams is not as strange as wakefulness. With ears and eyes, hands and arms, the strangeness is even greater."

Great speech cannot be spoken; great wisdom cannot be thought. Tracing the source of the six senses, one finds the nameless, passionless Dao of true emptiness and wondrous existence. Nameless, thus great speech cannot be spoken; passionless, thus great wisdom cannot be thought. One must awaken to this before the arising of words or thoughts, when the mind opens in sudden clarity, and one understands that the great constancy governs all minor changes. Then even the anomalies previously considered strange are not truly so—hence it is said: "Great speech cannot be spoken; great wisdom cannot be thought."

This chapter elucidates how the great constancy governs all minor changes, yet the common people employ it daily without awareness.

**Chapter Three**
Master Guan Yin said: Someone asked me, "What is your clan, what is your family name? What is your given name, what is your courtesy name? What do you wear, what do you eat? Who are your friends, who are your servants? What zither do you play, what books do you read? What ancient principles do you follow, what present affairs do you attend to?"

At that time, I remained silent and did not utter a single word.

Another asked, "What is the surname of your lineage, what is your taboo name, what virtue does your courtesy name express?"—thus repeating the question: "What is your clan, what is your name, what is your courtesy name?"

Again, they asked: "What clothes do you wear, what food do you eat? With whom do you keep company, whom do you employ as servants? What kind of zither do you play, what kind of books do you read? Whose ancient principles do you emulate, whose present affairs do you follow?"

I remained silent and did not answer a single word, for this silently conveyed the teaching of non-utterance. Thus it is said: "What do you wear, what do you eat? Who are your friends, who are your servants? What zither do you play, what books do you read? What ancient principles do you follow, what present affairs do you attend to?" At that time, I remained silent and did not utter a single word.

When the questioner persisted without cease, I could not but respond: "You do not even see yourself—what could be mine?"

This subtly revealed the mechanism beyond, yet the person did not awaken. When pressed further, I could not but reply: "You do not even see yourself—what could truly belong to me in the realm of true emptiness?" Thus it is said: "You do not even see yourself—what could be mine?"

**Chapter Four**
Master Guan Yin said: Form may be divided or united, extended or concealed. A man and a woman may bear two children—form may be divided. A man and a woman may together produce one child—form may be united. Consuming *jusheng* prolongs life—form may be extended. On a moonless night without firelight, one cannot see me—form may be concealed.

Those who deeply attain the Dao have bones and flesh wholly fused, form and spirit perfectly merged. They may divide into countless millions without insufficiency, or unite into one without excess. Their form endures for eternal kalpas—thus form may be extended. Profound and unfathomable, their form may be concealed. Thus it is said: form may be divided or united, extended or concealed.

Fearing that people might not fully grasp this, I illustrate it with mundane analogies. In the world, a man and a woman may bear twins—this is the image of form being divided. A man and a woman may unite to produce one child—this is the image of form being united. Consuming *jusheng* seeds prolongs life—this is the image of form being extended. On a moonless night without firelight, one cannot see another—this is the image of form being concealed. Thus it is said: a man and a woman may bear two children—form may be divided; a man and a woman may together produce one child—form may be united; consuming *jusheng* prolongs life—form may be extended; on a moonless night without firelight, one cannot see me—form may be concealed.

*Jusheng*: The smaller variety is called sesame; the larger, *jusheng*. It grows in the wilds of Shangdang County in Youzhou, reaching over three feet in height. Its seeds resemble those of the *niubang* but are smaller, dark greenish-black. They are harvested in the eighth month. Immortals prepare them as food to attain longevity. In ancient times, Han Zhong consumed them and lived for a hundred years; with prolonged use, his lifespan extended to a thousand, and none knew its end.

The one qi generates the myriad things, like hair that may be replaced—thus form may be divided. The one qi unites the myriad things, like a cleft lip that may be mended—thus form may be united. Spirit preserves qi, qi preserves form—thus form may be extended. Uniting form with spirit, spirit with the formless—thus form may be concealed.

Do you wish to know this? Do you wish to practice it?

Having previously employed common analogies to enlighten the learner, I now reveal the true and wondrous workings of the Dao.

Those who deeply attain the Dao achieve perfection in both form and spirit, their transformations unfathomable. Through the one qi, they generate countless forms, like human hair that falls and regrows endlessly—this is the principle of form’s divisibility. Thus it is said: “With the one qi, the myriad things are born, like discarded hair that may be replaced—hence form may be divided.”

Gathering the countless forms born of the one qi, they reunite them into a single body, just as a cleft lip may be mended to restore wholeness—this is the principle of form’s reunification. Thus it is said: “With the one qi, the myriad things are united, like a cleft lip that may be mended—hence form may be united.”

By preserving the spirit in its non-spiritual state—empty, still, and profoundly serene—the primordial qi remains harmonious and undiminished. Over time, both form and spirit attain perfection, and one achieves immortality. This is the principle of form’s extension. Thus it is said: “Spirit preserves qi, qi preserves form—hence form may be extended.”

Uniting form with spirit, and spirit with the boundless, wondrous Dao, one becomes obscure and invisible, beyond the perception of gods or demons, beyond the reckoning of yin and yang. This is the principle of form’s concealment. Thus it is said: “Unite form with spirit, spirit with the formless—hence form may be concealed.”

O learner, do you wish to know this wondrous Dao? Do you delight in practicing it? This Dao cannot be grasped by knowledge, nor attained through deliberate action—it lies solely in the heart’s silent comprehension. Thus it is said: “Do you wish to know it? Do you wish to practice it?” This chapter clarifies that for the enlightened, division, union, extension, and concealment of form present no obstacle.

**Chapter Five**
Guan Yinzi said: There is nothing that cannot be seen—thus nothing is not my seeing. There is nothing that cannot be heard—thus nothing is not my hearing.

The forms of all things in the world are but the dust of color. When the eye of Dao opens wide, nothing remains unseen, and every seeing is the Dao. Thus it is said: “There is nothing that cannot be seen—thus nothing is not my seeing.”

The sounds of all things in the world are but the dust of noise. When the ear of heaven opens wide, no sound goes unheard, and every hearing is the Dao. Thus it is said: “There is nothing that cannot be heard—thus nothing is not my hearing.”

The five grains nourish the form, and nothing is not my form. The five flavors nourish qi, and nothing is not my qi. Therefore, my form and qi are the ten thousand things of heaven and earth.

The five phases shift and transform, yin and yang create and evolve, giving rise to the five grains—awned, husked, bearded, pointed, and eared—to nourish the human form. The forms of the ten thousand things in heaven and earth arise from the creation and transformation of yin, yang, and the five phases, and my form likewise arises from them. From this perspective, nothing in heaven and earth is not my form. Thus it is said: “The five grains nourish the form, and nothing is not my form.”

Through the qi of the five phases, the five flavors—sour, salty, sweet, acrid, and bitter—are created and transformed. When people consume them, they preserve and nourish the qi of the five viscera. All things arise from the qi of the five phases, and my body likewise arises from it. From this perspective, nothing is not my qi. As stated above, the ten thousand things of heaven and earth are my form and my qi. Thus it is said: “The five flavors nourish qi, and nothing is not my qi.” Therefore, my form and qi are the ten thousand things of heaven and earth.

This chapter clarifies that heaven and earth are born together with me, and the ten thousand things are one with me.

**Chapter Six**
Guan Yinzi said: The plowman who works with oxen becomes coarse; the hunter who works with tigers becomes brave; the fisherman who works with water becomes adept at sinking; the warrior who works with horses becomes agile. The ten thousand things can become me.

Coarseness (*guang*) means wildness—fierce, reckless, and foolish. The plowman who drives oxen for long, staying close to them, adopts their fierce and reckless nature. Thus it is said: “The plowman who works with oxen becomes coarse.”

The hunter who pursues and kills tigers and leopards for long, staying close to them, adopts their brave and fearless nature. Thus it is said: “The hunter who works with tigers becomes brave.”

The fisherman who catches aquatic creatures, staying close to water for long, becomes adept at sinking into it to seize his prey. Thus it is said: “The fisherman who works with water becomes adept at sinking.”

The warrior who rides fine horses for long, staying close to them, adopts their swift and agile nature. Thus it is said: “The warrior who works with horses becomes agile.”

Human nature is originally pure and still, but through long habituation, it becomes stained and attached to the natures of things. As in the examples above—habituation to oxen, tigers, water, and horses—one becomes coarse, brave, adept at sinking, or agile. Is this not the nature of things shaping my own nature? Thus it is said: “The ten thousand things can become me.”

Within my body, internal transformations give rise to *nao* and *hui* worms, while external influences breed lice and fleas. Sores may produce turtles or fish, and ulcers may yield rats. Thus I can become the ten thousand things.

*Nao* are fine worms in the abdomen. *Hui* are large worms in the abdomen, also called *laohao*. They arise from the interaction of yin and yang qi in the foods consumed. Lice and fleas arise from the warmth of clothing and the steam of sweat.

Sores (*ju*) are ulcers. Once, a man developed a sore on his back, from which a turtle gradually emerged, stretching its neck to gnaw at his flesh until he died in agony. Another man developed a sore on his waist, from which a fish emerged, causing unbearable pain with every movement.

Ulcers (*huan*) are also sores. Once, a man developed a sore on his neck, from which a fleshly rat emerged. A monk, annoyed by mantises crawling along the railing, burned them all to ashes. Soon after, he developed a sore that, when lanced, revealed only mantises.

Within the human body, the *nao* and *hui* worms transform; beyond the body, steam and warmth breed lice and fleas. Afflictions give rise to sores, which may become turtles or fish, rats or mantises—these seven creatures. From this we know that I, too, may become all things.

Thus it is said: within my body, *nao* and *hui* arise; without, lice and fleas are born. Sores (*ju*) become turtles and fish; ulcers (*huan*) become rats and mantises. I may become all things.

This chapter clarifies that when there is an "I," things become mine, and I become things; when there is no "I," creation cannot alter me.

---

**Chapter Seven**
Guan Yinzi said: My "I" is like gold in ash—yet not as gold in ore. Break the ore to obtain gold; sift the sand to obtain gold. But sift the ash for a lifetime, and no gold will be found.

The "I" is the mind that grasps subject and object. The mind is like fire, yet without an "I." It manifests through oil and wick, revealing the form of fire. When oil and wick are exhausted, the fire turns to ash. The mind is originally without an "I"; it appears through conditions. Forget the mind, extinguish the "I" that grasps subject and object—thus it is said, "My 'I' is like gold in ash." If there is no gold in the ash, then the ashen mind has no "I."

Ore is unrefined gold; ore and sand are likened to the six dusts. The six dusts are form, sound, scent, taste, touch, and dharma. "Not as gold in ore" is a metaphor for my mind. To obtain gold, one must seek it in ore and sand—smelt the ore, wash away the sand, and gold is found. But if one merely sifts the ash, even a lifetime of effort yields no gold.

This is a metaphor for the mind, originally without an "I," appearing through the six dusts. To see the mind, observe the six dusts with clarity—awakening follows the flow, and the source is found in the stream. If the six dusts are purified, the mind returns to true emptiness. Even the celestial eye or dragon’s gaze cannot perceive it, let alone ordinary vision. Thus it is said: "Not as gold in ore. Break the ore to obtain gold; sift the sand to obtain gold. But sift the ash for a lifetime, and no gold will be found."

This chapter clarifies that the "I" is originally without an "I," falsely established through conditions.

---

**Chapter Eight**
Guan Yinzi said: A single bee, though tiny, may roam and observe heaven and earth. A single shrimp, though tiny, may frolic in the vast sea.

The bee and shrimp are metaphors for humans; heaven and earth and the great sea are metaphors for the great Dao. Though the bee and shrimp are small, they may roam heaven and earth and frolic in the sea. Though humans are small, they may comprehend the great Dao—boundless, transcending the finite and the sage, far beyond yin and yang.

Thus it is said: "A single bee, though tiny, may roam and observe heaven and earth. A single shrimp, though tiny, may frolic in the vast sea." To roam and observe, to frolic—both are self-attained ease.

This chapter clarifies that though humans are small, they may comprehend the great Dao.

---

**Chapter Nine**
Guan Yinzi said: When clay figurines are formed, some are noble, some base, some male, some female—yet their substance is earth, their dust the same as man’s.

Clay figurines are human images molded from mud. Once shaped, they may be officials or servants, men or women—all are forms made of earth. Human perception, following appearances, distinguishes noble from base, male from female.

Alas! They are all but earth—why falsely divide noble and base, male and female? This is a metaphor for the human body, though divided into noble and base, male and female, being but a temporary aggregation of the four great elements—earth, water, fire, and wind—like dust gathering into foam. Why falsely divide noble and base, male and female?

Thus it is said: "When clay figurines are formed, some are noble, some base, some male, some female—yet their substance is earth, their dust the same as man’s."

This chapter clarifies that humans, deluded by temporary aggregates, falsely divide noble and base, male and female.

---

**Chapter Ten**
Guan Yinzi said: The eye sees of itself, yet the eye has no color. The ear hears of itself, yet the ear has no sound. The tongue tastes of itself, yet the tongue has no flavor. The mind contemplates of itself, yet the mind has no thing.

The eye is a sense organ; color is dust. When the eye departs from dust and returns to reflection, it awakens to the eye’s original purity, fundamentally without color-dust. Thus it is said: "The eye sees of itself, yet the eye has no color."

The ear is a sense organ; sound is dust. When the ear departs from dust and returns to listening, it awakens to the ear’s original purity, fundamentally without sound-dust. Thus it is said: "The ear hears of itself, yet the ear has no sound."

The tongue is a sense organ; flavor is dust. When the tongue departs from dust and returns to tasting, it awakens to the tongue’s original purity, fundamentally without flavor-dust. Thus it is said: "The tongue tastes of itself, yet the tongue has no flavor."

The mind is a sense organ; things are dharmas, and dharmas are dust. When the mind departs from dust and returns to self-contemplation, it awakens to the mind’s original purity, fundamentally without thing-dharma-dust. Thus it is said: "The mind contemplates of itself, yet the mind has no thing."

Ordinary people, attached to the six dusts, pursue external affairs and lose their true nature. Thus it is said: "The multitude chases the external." The worthy depart from external affairs and cling to inner principle. Yet in grasping and clinging to inner principle, they create a barrier of principle and stray from the Dao. Thus it is said: "The worthy cling to the internal." The sage neither stains the six dusts externally—awakening to the falsity of the obstacle of affairs—nor grasps or clings to principle internally—awakening to the falsity of the obstacle of principle. Thus it is said: "The sage sees all as false," for they do not dwell in the three times or either extreme.

This chapter clarifies that the mind is originally pure, not dwelling in the three times or the middle and extremes.

---

**Chapter Eleven**
Guan Yinzi said: The qi of the five phases within my body—yet the nature of the qi of the five phases is one thing.

The qi of the five viscera in the human body: the qi of the liver belongs to wood and conceals the *hun*; the qi of the heart belongs to fire and conceals the spirit; the qi of the lungs belongs to metal and conceals the *po*; the qi of the kidneys belongs to water and conceals essence; the qi of the spleen belongs to earth and conceals intention. When spirit, *hun*, *po*, essence, and intention return to their nature, the five qi gather at the origin.

**Chapter Twelve**

Thus it is said: "The qi of the five phases within my body"—yet the nature of the five phases’ qi is one thing. When the five spirits return to their nature and the five qi gather at the origin, this is non-duality. Wondrous nature! Things are the wondrous function of nature. For example, a single place may yield fire, yield water, give birth to wood, congeal metal, or transform earth—its nature contains and embraces all, originally without distinction.

Since the five spirits return to their nature and the five qi gather at the origin, this single nature can again generate the spirit-fire, essence-water, *hun*-wood, *po*-metal, and intention-earth. When the five spirits return to their nature and the five qi gather at the origin, this is nature containing and embracing the five phases, originally without distinction. Thus it is said: "For example, a single place may yield fire, yield water, give birth to wood, congeal metal, or transform earth—its nature contains and embraces all, originally without distinction."

Therefore, when feathered creatures flourish, furred creatures do not thrive; when furred creatures flourish, scaled creatures do not thrive. One who understands the mutual generation of the five phases may forget the self. Of the five creatures, three are mentioned—the other two may be inferred. The five creatures are: scaled, feathered, furred, shelled, and armored. Feathered creatures belong to fire; when fire is at its peak, metal does not generate, hence "when feathered creatures flourish, furred creatures do not thrive." *Thrive* means to be born. Furred creatures belong to metal; scaled creatures belong to wood. When metal is at its peak, wood does not generate, hence "when furred creatures flourish, scaled creatures do not thrive." From this, we know that when scaled creatures flourish, armored creatures do not thrive—armored creatures belong to earth, and when wood flourishes, earth does not generate. When armored creatures flourish, shelled creatures do not thrive—shelled creatures belong to water, and when earth flourishes, water does not generate. When shelled creatures flourish, feathered creatures do not thrive—when water flourishes, fire does not generate.

Thus, when the heart does not generate, intention is not born; when intention does not generate, the *po* is not born; when the *po* does not generate, essence is not born; when essence does not generate, the *hun* is not born; when the *hun* does not generate, the spirit is not born; when the spirit does not generate, the heart is not born. When the heart does not generate, there is no self to forget—hence "one who understands the mutual generation of the five phases may forget the self." This chapter clarifies that the Dao is the ancestor of the five phases, and nature is the source of the five spirits.

**Chapter Thirteen**

Guan Yinzi said: A withered tortoise shell, though without self, can reveal great knowledge; a lodestone, though without self, can reveal great power; bells and drums, though without self, can reveal great sound; boats and carts, though without self, can reveal far-reaching movement.

Without self means without mind. A withered, dried tortoise shell has no mind, yet when one bores into it with sincerity, it reveals omens of future fortune or misfortune—what is this if not great knowledge? Thus it is said: "A withered tortoise shell, though without self, can reveal great knowledge." A lodestone has no mind, yet when a needle draws near, it attracts and clings to it—what is this if not great power? Thus it is said: "A lodestone, though without self, can reveal great power." Bells and drums have no mind, yet when struck or beaten, their sound resonates far—what is this if not great sound? Thus it is said: "Bells and drums, though without self, can reveal great sound." Boats and carts have no mind, yet by means of water or oxen, they carry goods to distant places—what is this if not far-reaching movement? Thus it is said: "Boats and carts, though without self, can reveal far-reaching movement."

Therefore, though this body of mine possesses knowledge, power, sound, and movement, it has never possessed a self. The sage is without mind—without knowledge yet knowing all, penetrating the principles of things; without power yet all-powerful, so that no demon dares to invade; without sound yet all-sounding, so that every tone is wondrous; without movement yet moving everywhere, traversing the ten directions in an instant—yet never possessing a self or a mind. Thus it is said: "Therefore, though this body of mine possesses knowledge, power, sound, and movement, it has never possessed a self." This chapter clarifies that the Dao of no-self is perfectly good and perfectly beautiful.

**Chapter Fourteen**

Guan Yinzi said: The *yu* shoots at shadows and can kill me—know that the unknowing is also myself, and beneath the vast heavens, I am nowhere absent.

The *yu* is the name of a water creature, also called *duanhu* or *shuinu*. Its form resembles a crab, with three legs and a length of three to five inches. It is commonly found in the south. It spits sand to shoot at a person’s shadow, which can cause death. Thus it is said: "The *yu* shoots at shadows and can kill me." From this, we know that the shadow itself is unknowing, yet the *yu* merely shoots the shadow and can kill a person—thus the unknowing is also myself. If one knows that the unknowing is also myself, then beneath the vast heavens, whether sentient or insentient, there is nothing that is not myself. This "I" is the self of no-self, its substance identical to empty space, nowhere absent. Thus it is said: "Know that the unknowing is also myself, and beneath the vast heavens, I am nowhere absent." This chapter clarifies that the self of no-self is the wondrous Dao, and the wondrous Dao is nowhere absent.

**Chapter Fifteen**

Guan Yinzi said: One whose mind is absorbed in recollection may forget hunger; one whose mind is consumed by anger may forget cold; one whose mind is nurtured may forget illness; one whose mind is roused may forget pain.

When a person’s mind is fixated on something, they may go all day without feeling hunger—thus it is said: "One whose mind is absorbed in recollection may forget hunger." When, in the midst of freezing ice and snow, a person’s mind is suddenly enraged and their inner fire blazes forth, they may strip bare and stand in the open, sweat soaking their body, yet forget the cold—thus it is said: "One whose mind is consumed by anger may forget cold." A noble person who cultivates the nourishment of the mind may face all manner of dangers or contract severe illness yet remain unmoved in heart or troubled in spirit—thus they may forget their ailment. Thus it is said: "One whose mind is nurtured may forget illness." When two people mutually provoke each other, their minds clash in fury, their limbs are injured, yet they feel no pain—thus it is said: "One whose mind is roused may forget pain."

If one inhales qi to nurture harmony, who can afflict them with hunger? If one preserves spirit to nourish warmth, who can afflict them with cold? If one cultivates the five viscera with the Five Phases, there will be no injury—who can afflict them with illness? If one returns the five viscera to the Five Phases, there will be no awareness—who can afflict them with pain?

Earlier, it was said that ordinary people, though possessing minds, may through recollection, anger, nurturance, or arousal forget hunger, cold, illness, and pain—how much more so for those who have attained the Dao and transcended the mind? Having attained the Dao and transcended the mind, their primordial qi flows in harmonious equilibrium; their breath, soft and continuous, enables them to eat a hundred meals in a day or go a hundred days without food yet never hunger. Thus it is said: *If one inhales qi to nurture harmony, who can afflict them with hunger?* "Who" here means "how."

Having attained the Dao and transcended the mind, their spirit and water are firm, their true fire steaming and suffusing, able to withstand the bitterest cold. Thus it is said: *If one preserves spirit to nourish warmth, who can afflict them with cold?*

Having attained the Dao and transcended the mind, their hundred vessels flow smoothly, their five viscera rest in harmony, their five spirits are clear, their five qi intermingle and merge—no illness arises. Thus it is said: *If one cultivates the five viscera with the Five Phases, there will be no injury—who can afflict them with illness?*

Having attained the Dao and transcended the mind, heart, liver, spleen, lungs, and kidneys are aligned with Fire, Wood, Earth, Metal, and Water; all discriminating knowledge is forgotten. Even when a blade is pressed to their neck, they feel no fear or pain—like trimming hair or removing dirt, what pain could there be? Thus it is said: *If one returns the five viscera to the Five Phases, there will be no awareness—who can afflict them with pain?*

This chapter clarifies that hunger, cold, illness, and pain are hard to escape when one clings to the self, but having attained the Dao and transcended the mind, one rises above the suffering of hunger, cold, illness, and pain.

---

**Chapter Fifteen**

Guan Yinzi said: People should not take the absence of knowledge and action as the absence of self. Though one may possess knowledge and action, it does not impair their absence of self.

Earlier, it was said that the withered tortoise, lodestone, bell, drum, boat, and cart have no self, lest one mistakenly equate the absence of knowledge and action with the absence of self. Another caution is given to learners: do not take the absence of knowledge and action, like dead matter, as the absence of self—otherwise, one would be no different from those who toil in the ghostly caverns beneath Black Mountain.

Little do they know that the absence of knowledge is not without knowledge, the absence of action is not without action. Still and unmoving, yet responsive and penetrating—though possessing knowledge and action, how could this obstruct the absence of self? Thus it is said: *People should not take the absence of knowledge and action as the absence of self. Though one may possess knowledge and action, it does not impair their absence of self.*

For example, fire is restless and ceaseless, yet it has never possessed a self. Having explained that possessing knowledge and action does not impair the absence of self, lest one fail to grasp this fully, another analogy is given to clarify.

Fire, though inherently without self, takes form through kindling. When fire blazes, it burns restlessly and ceaselessly, yet fire has never possessed a self—just as true emptiness responds to change, ever still and without self. Thus it is said: *For example, fire is restless and ceaseless, yet it has never possessed a self.*

This chapter clarifies that true emptiness and the absence of self are not bound by knowledge or action, nor by existence or non-existence.

---

**End of Commentary on the Sixth Scroll of the *Wenshi Zhenjing***

#1 These two lines appear in the *Master Guan Yin* as: *"The world’s people take what is briefly seen as dream, and what is long seen as waking."*

#2 This line appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Dao* as: *"There has never been an exception."*

#3 *"People who are devoted to practicing righteousness"* should read *"Those who are devoted to practicing righteousness."*

#4 This line appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Dao* as: *"Yet in dreams, one may hear of certain matters."*

#5 This line appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Dao* as: *"Even in dreams, struggles shift accordingly."*

#6 The line *"I and others"* appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Dao* as: *"Others do not see me."*

#7 This line appears in both the *Master Guan Yin* and the *Wenshi Zhenjing of the Supreme Dao* as: *"The myriad things may become me,"* without the character *"with."*

**End of Commentary on the Seventh Scroll of the *Wenshi Zhenjing***
*Direct Explication by Xiaoyaozi Niu Daochun of Shenfeng*

**The Seventh Cauldron Scroll**

The cauldron signifies transformation, comprising thirteen chapters in all.

Master Guan Yin states:
The Dao is fundamentally utter non-being. Those who return affairs to the Dao attain it in a single breath. Affairs are fundamentally utter being. Those who employ the Dao to manage affairs encompass them in a hundred acts.

Non-being is true emptiness. The supreme Dao is originally true emptiness; when function returns to essence, enlightenment dawns in a single breath. Thus it is said: *The Dao is fundamentally utter non-being. Those who return affairs to the Dao attain it in a single breath.*

Affairs are function; being is wondrous existence. To employ wondrous existence as function is to unite essence and function, responding and transforming without end. Thus it is said: *Affairs are fundamentally utter being. Those who employ the Dao to manage affairs encompass them in a hundred acts.*

Those who attain the Dao in its nobility may assist the world. Those who attain the Dao in its singularity may establish the self.

Nobility is the utmost honor. Those who do not lose the Dao in its utmost honor may aid in bringing worldly peace and benefiting others. Thus it is said: *Those who attain the Dao in its nobility may assist the world.*

"Employ" is function; "singularity" is non-duality; "self" is the essence of true emptiness. Those who do not lose the Dao in its non-duality may gather the function of wondrous existence and restore the essence of true emptiness for self-cultivation. Thus it is said: *Those who attain the Dao in its singularity may establish the self.*

Those who know the Dao cannot be constrained by time may make one day into a hundred years, or a hundred years into one day.

"Know" is awakening; "Dao" is the inconceivable Dao of true emptiness and wondrous existence. Awakening to the inconceivable Dao of true emptiness and wondrous existence is like the great void—beyond the grasp of temporal measures. All that has form is bound by limits; the sea may dry, mountains may crumble, sun and moon may darken, heaven and earth may collapse. Only this Dao of true emptiness transcends time, allowing one to use a single day as a thousand myriad years, or a thousand myriad years as a single day. Thus it is said: *Those who know the Dao cannot be constrained by time may make one day into a hundred years, or a hundred years into one day.*

Those who know the Dao cannot be hindered by space may make one *li* into a hundred *li*, or a hundred *li* into one *li*.

The Dao is originally formless, unbound by the ten directions. The utmost spirit is selfless, unrestrained by the eight extremes. It is swift without haste, arrives without moving, its essence like the great void, its function likewise. Awakening to this, one may traverse a single *li* as if roaming beyond the eight wilds, or traverse the eight wilds as if moving a single *li*. Thus it is said: *Those who know the Dao cannot be hindered by space may make one *li* into a hundred *li*, or a hundred *li* into one *li*.*

Those who know the Dao as prior to qi and can direct existing qi may summon wind and rain.

Awakening to the Dao before the arising of the primordial qi, one may employ the Dao to move qi, transforming yin and yang into wind and rain. The profoundly realized may subtly wield the function of true emptiness, instantly summoning clouds and thunder, calling forth wind and rain. Thus it is said: *Those who know the Dao as prior to qi and can direct existing qi may summon wind and rain.*

Those who know the Dao as formless and can transform existing forms may alter birds and beasts.

The adept of the Dao possesses boundless spiritual powers, roaming as a companion to the Creator. They may transform formlessness into form, or form into formlessness, concealing or revealing at will. They may turn flying birds into running beasts, or running beasts into flying birds, manifesting wondrous transformations without end. Thus it is said: *Those who know the Dao as formless and can transform existing forms may alter birds and beasts.*

Those who attain the Dao in its purity are unburdened by things; their bodies become light, and they may ride phoenixes and cranes.

The Dao is neither pure nor impure, yet may be either. The adept of the Dao, pure and unstained like the void, cannot be encumbered by the myriad things. Body and spirit both wondrous, they unite with true emptiness, weightless as the void, traversing the ten directions in an instant without need of a mount. To manifest spiritual transformations and awaken the deluded, they may ride phoenixes or cranes, or ascend on colored clouds, revealing the truth to those of middling and lesser capacity and inspiring their sincerity in the Dao. Thus it is said: *Those who attain the Dao in its purity are unburdened by things; their bodies become light, and they may ride phoenixes and cranes*—as did Wei Shuqing, who rode a white crane, or Wang Zijin, who ascended on a phoenix while playing his flute.

Those who attain the Dao in its undifferentiated state are unswayed by things; their bodies become obscure, and they may sit upon dragons and whales.

The adept of the Dao dwells in the world without defilement, harmonizing their light without display, utterly concealed. Thus it is said: *Those who attain the Dao in its undifferentiated state are unswayed by things; their bodies become obscure.* "Unswayed" means unstained; "obscure" means concealed. They may manifest spiritual powers, using dragons and whales as seats, riding them through the great void. Thus it is said: *They may sit upon dragons and whales*—as did the ancient Yu, who rode dragons to guide the hundred rivers; Li Taibai, who mounted a flood dragon; or Liu Zhigu, who rode a golden carp; or Qin Gao, who ascended on a red carp.

Being is non-being; non-being is being. Those who know this Dao may command spirits and gods.

"Being" is the function of wondrous existence; "non-being" is the essence of true emptiness. Function is essence; essence is function—essence and function are inseparable. Awakening to this Dao, one may subdue spirits and gods, employing them as servants. Thus it is said: *Being is non-being; non-being is being. Those who know this Dao may command spirits and gods*—as did Liu Gen, who skillfully employed spirits and gods, or the Han Celestial Master, who subdued them in Chengdu.

Reality is emptiness; emptiness is reality. Those who know this Dao may enter metal and stone.

Reality is true existence; emptiness is true void. True void and true existence are the non-duality of the Dao’s essence and function. Awakening to the non-duality of essence and function, emptiness and reality interpenetrate, allowing one to tread upon water and fire, enter metal and stone without obstruction. Thus it is said: *Reality is emptiness; emptiness is reality. Those who know this Dao may enter metal and stone.*

Above is the wondrous Dao before the Five Ultimates; below is the myriad virtues and practices of perfect power. The wondrous Dao is essence; virtue and practice are function. When essence and function share a single source, above and below are non-dual. Those who awaken to this Dao may command the ten thousand images and the forest of phenomena as their attendants. Thus it is said: *Above is below; below is above. Those who know this Dao may attend the stars and constellations.*

The ancient is principle; the present is phenomena. Principle is essence; phenomena are function. When essence and function are inseparable, those who awaken to this Dao remain quiescent and unmoving, yet respond with perfect resonance—like the divination of the yarrow stalks, where auspicious omens are certain, and sincerity is without falsehood. Thus it is said: *The ancient is the present; the present is the ancient. Those who know this Dao may divine with tortoise and millet.*

Others are the external realm; the self is the mind. When mind and realm are both forgotten, the Dao-eye opens in clarity. Those who awaken to this Dao can see all without seeing, perceiving the thoughts and intentions within others’ hearts and lungs—this is called *knowledge of others’ minds*. Thus it is said: *Others are the self; the self is others. Those who know this Dao may peer into others’ lungs and livers.*

Heaven and earth are born together with me; the ten thousand things and I are one. Those who awaken to this Dao may transform their liver-spirit into a dragon, their lung-spirit into a tiger, riding and mounting them to wander the Great Clarity. Thus it is said: *Things are the self; the self is things. Those who know this Dao may cultivate dragons and tigers within the abdomen.*

Knowing that images arise from the mind’s transformations, by contemplating the mind thus, one may cultivate the Maiden and the Infant. The realm of the ten thousand images arises from the transformations of the mind-spirit. Through this profound contemplation, the mind’s essence is true emptiness, yet it contains the wondrous function of existence. The spirit within the heart transforms into the Marvelous Maiden; the spirit within the kidneys transforms into the Infant Brother. When Maiden and Infant unite, nature and life merge, transcending birth and death. Thus it is said: *Knowing that images arise from the mind’s transformations, by contemplating the mind thus, one may cultivate the Maiden and the Infant.*

Knowing that qi arises from the mind, by absorbing spirit thus, one may refine the furnace and crucible. Where the mind directs, qi follows. Knowing that qi arises from the mind, when the mind realizes true emptiness—like still, limpid water—breathing the spirit-qi with unbroken continuity, the heart-fire descends and the kidney-water ascends. When water and fire intermingle, the Great Elixir is formed. Those who ingest it transcend the ills of birth and death. Thus it is said: *Knowing that qi arises from the mind, by absorbing spirit thus, one may refine the furnace and crucible.*

The later sages’ use of the elixir to metaphorically describe the Dao originates here. With this supreme power, tigers and leopards may be subdued; by harmonizing with things, one may enter water and fire. With this perfect Dao and perfect virtue, radiant majesty shines forth, and all things submit in reverence. Tigers and leopards bow in submission, serving as mounts. Thus it is said: *With this, one may subdue things; tigers and leopards may be tamed.* With this perfect Dao, the body merges with the Great Void—how could water drown or fire burn the void? Those who awaken to the Dao are no different from it, thus they may traverse vast floods without drowning, endure great droughts where metal and stone melt and mountains scorch without feeling heat. Thus it is said: *By harmonizing with things, one may enter water and fire.*

Only those who embody the Dao can achieve these feats, yet they may also choose not to act upon them. The eighteen wondrous powers described above—assisting the world and establishing the self, traversing a hundred *li* in a day or a hundred years in an instant, summoning wind and rain, transforming birds and beasts, riding phoenixes and cranes, resting upon whales, commanding spirits and gods, entering metal and stone, attending the stars and constellations, divining with tortoise and millet, perceiving others’ lungs and livers, cultivating dragons and tigers, the Maiden and the Infant, the furnace and crucible, taming tigers and leopards, entering water and fire—are all within the mastery of those who awaken to the Dao. Yet they may also choose not to employ them, for these are the miraculous transformations of those who can act yet refrain.

The Dao values authenticity and sincerity, and concealment is paramount. The sage does not perform wonders that startle the foolish or shock the vulgar—these eighteen marvels are such examples. Though the matters themselves are profoundly real, when the Dao governs them, they become as effortless as a hundred acts. Thus it is said: *Only those who embody the Dao can achieve these feats, yet they may also choose not to act upon them.*

This chapter elucidates the boundless transformations of the Dao’s essence and function, while the sage conceals their brilliance, never startling the foolish or vulgar.

**Chapter One**
Guan Yinzi said: Among human abilities, there are those that can seize the creative transformations of heaven and earth. Those who awaken to the Dao may wander as equals with the Creator, seizing the wondrous generative and transformative powers of heaven and earth’s yin and yang. Thus it is said: *Among human abilities, there are those that can seize the creative transformations of heaven and earth.*

For example, summoning thunder in winter or creating ice in summer, animating the dead or causing withered trees to bloom—those who awaken to the Dao can invert yin and yang, summoning thunder in winter or congealing ice in summer. As when Lao Chengzi studied under Yin Wen, mastering life and death at will, reversing the four seasons: thunder in winter, ice in summer, birds running and beasts flying, yet never erring in his transformations. Such marvels are unknown to the world. Thus it is said: *For example, summoning thunder in winter or creating ice in summer.*

Of old, the Most High cast the Great Mystery Life Talisman upon Xu Jia’s withered bones, and in that very moment they revived and could walk—thus it is said: *the dead corpse can walk.* The Pure Yang Patriarch revived a withered willow in Anzhou with the elixir, and likewise revived a withered locust tree in Laizhou, whereupon its branches and leaves flourished anew—thus it is said: *the withered tree can bloom.*

Summoning ghosts from beans: Once, the Perfected Lord Guo Pu scattered several hundred small beans beyond a wall, and they all transformed into red-clad ghosts. Using talismans as divine generals, he bound them one by one and cast them into a well—thus it is said: *summoning ghosts from beans.*

Fishing in a cup: Once, the Perfected Lord Zuo Ci drank with Cao Cao. He filled a bronze basin with water, cast hook and bait into it, and in an instant hooked a golden carp over a foot long, which he then scaled—thus it is said: *fishing for fish in a cup.*

A painted door can open: Emperor Taizong of Tang summoned Wu Daozi to splash ink upon the palace walls. After veiling it with curtains for a long while, the curtains were drawn back, and the emperor was invited to view the painting. Mountains and rivers, grasses and trees, human dwellings, birds and beasts—all were fully rendered. Suddenly, a cave entrance appeared beneath a cliff. Daozi pointed to the cave and said, “Within dwell immortals.” He struck it with his hand, and the cave door instantly opened. Daozi leapt inside and beckoned to the emperor, who dared not enter. In an instant, the door closed again and vanished—thus it is said: *a painted door can open.*

Clay ghosts can speak: Once, in a temple on Mount Lu, clay statues of gods and ghosts could foretell fortune and misfortune. The Perfected Lord Yue Ba exorcised them, revealing that they were possessed by a wild fox spirit. If even a wild fox spirit could make clay ghosts speak, how much more so the immortals?—thus it is said: *clay ghosts can speak.*

All these are the workings of pure qi, and thus they can transform the myriad things. As in the examples above—thunder in winter, ice in summer, the dead corpse walking, the withered tree blooming, summoning ghosts from beans, fishing in a cup, the painted door opening, clay ghosts speaking—these eight wonders all arise from purity without admixture, the wondrous Dao of true emptiness. They can move the single qi to transform the myriad things, which is precisely what was meant earlier by *among human abilities, there are those that can seize the creative transformations of heaven and earth.* Thus it is said: *All these are the workings of pure qi, and thus they can transform the myriad things.*

Now, the ceaseless flux of human emotions is also the work of qi, for qi is a thing that gathers and scatters. The hearts and passions of deluded people surge and flow like unceasing waves—this too is the work of strong yang qi. That strong yang qi, from birth to maturity, gathers and overflows; from maturity to old age, it scatters and declines. Thus it is said: *Now, the ceaseless flux of human emotions is also the work of qi, for qi as a thing gathers and scatters.*

The qi I cultivate has never gathered, nor has it ever scattered. The sage who awakens to the Dao of true emptiness attains the primordial qi’s origin, and the primordial qi’s origin has never known gathering or scattering. Thus it is said: *The qi I cultivate has never gathered, nor has it ever scattered.* “Origin” means source.

Where there is gathering, there is life; where there is scattering, there is death. The deluded person receives one catty of primordial qi. A man attains the number eight: at two eights, sixteen years, he begins to leak essence, unaware of how to preserve it. Every three years, he loses one ounce; in thirty years, he loses ten ounces. Three sixes make eighteen, and forty-eight years in total deplete the full sixteen ounces of primordial qi. After forty-eight years, adding another sixteen—eight eights, sixty-four—brings the hexagram count to its end. Once the primordial qi is exhausted, only the qi of the five grains sustains him. Though still called a man, in truth he is a ghost.

A woman receives fourteen ounces of primordial qi. A woman attains the number seven: at two sevens, fourteen years, the celestial waters descend, and the primordial qi begins to leak. Every two and a half years, she loses one ounce; in twenty-five years, she loses ten ounces, leaving four. In ten years, these are exhausted. Twenty-five plus ten years totals thirty-five, and adding another fourteen—seven sevens, forty-nine—brings the central yang number to its end. The celestial waters dry up, and only the qi of the five grains sustains her. Though still called a woman, in truth she is a ghost. Once the primordial qi of men and women is exhausted, they await only death. From this, we know that when qi gathers, there is life; when qi scatters, there is death. Thus it is said: *Where there is gathering, there is life; where there is scattering, there is death.* “Gathering” means to assemble.

That which has never gathered nor scattered is beyond life and death. Guests come and go, but the post station remains ever unchanged. The sage who awakens to the Dao attains the origin of primordial qi, which has never known gathering or scattering—thus we know there is no life, no death. The “post station” is the courier’s lodge; the “guest” is the traveling emissary. Guests come and go, but the lodge remains ever as it is, unmoving and unchanging, without departure or arrival. The guest is an analogy for qi; coming and going are analogies for scattering and gathering. The post station is an analogy for the Dao. The Dao moves primordial qi, ascending and descending, coming and going. When qi gathers, there is life; when qi scatters, there is death. Yet the Dao that moves qi is not qi. That which is not qi knows no gathering or scattering, no life or death, no coming or going. Thus the awakened one knows no gathering or scattering, no life or death, no coming or going. Thus it is said: *Guests come and go, but the post station remains ever unchanged.* “Unchanged” means thusness, unmoving and untransformed.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao is the origin of primordial qi, its transformations boundless, yet without coming or going, life or death.

---

Chapter Two

Guan Yinzi said: There are those who recite mantras, those who serve spirits, those who inscribe talismans, and those who manipulate fingers—all can command spirits, master qi, and transform the myriad things. The adepts of ritual registers transmit and receive whispered instructions, devoting themselves to prayer and recitation to achieve spiritual efficacy. For example, the Nine Characters of the Esoteric Tradition, when recited 108,000 times, respond to the will like an echo—thus it is said: *there are those who recite mantras.*

Guan Yinzi said: There are those who erect images, focusing their reverence in single-minded devotion, praying and supplicating according to their hearts—nothing fails to respond. Thus it is said: *there are those who serve spirits.*

The masters of talismanic registers transmit and receive esoteric writs of divine descent, inscribing characters on paper and ink to reveal future fortune or misfortune. Their verifications are never in error. Thus it is said: *there are those who inscribe talismans.*

The masters of ritual registers transmit and receive hand seals of divine response, directing the afflicted to extend their hands over the incense burner. The master summons the incantation, calling forth the spirits for a long while. The fingers grow or shrink, rise or fall, and the master discerns the cause of the ghostly or spiritual affliction. Their verifications are never mistaken. Thus it is said: *there are those who manipulate fingers.*

These four practices—reciting mantras, serving spirits, inscribing talismans, and manipulating fingers—all can command ghosts and spirits, direct the flow of primordial qi, and transform the myriad things, thereby manifesting divine efficacy. Thus it is said: *all can command spirits, master qi, and transform the myriad things.*

Yet those lacking sincerity find it difficult to trust themselves and readily trust external objects. Thus, these methods are employed as expedients. Every person possesses the wondrous Dao of true emptiness, with boundless majesty and radiance. All is complete within, nothing is lacking in its use. With a single awakened thought, one’s station equals that of the sages—able to command ghosts and spirits, summon wind and rain, and wield limitless spiritual powers. Every person possesses such extraordinary potential. Yet when told this, people lack faith. Since they struggle to believe in themselves, they easily believe in external things. Out of compassion, the sages employ the sincerity of reciting mantras, serving spirits, inscribing talismans, and manipulating fingers to demonstrate spiritual efficacy and awaken the heart of faith in the Dao. Thus it is said: *those lacking sincerity find it difficult to trust themselves and readily trust external objects, hence these methods are employed as expedients.*

If one truly understands sincerity, there is no need to rely on such practices. *If* here means *sincerity*. If one knows that reciting mantras, serving spirits, inscribing talismans, and manipulating fingers yield spiritual efficacy—all arising from sincerity—then, understanding that only sincerity moves the spirits, one need not depend on reciting mantras, serving spirits, inscribing talismans, or manipulating fingers to command ghosts and spirits. Thus it is said: *if one truly understands sincerity, there is no need to rely on such practices.*

This chapter clarifies that sincerity is the foundation of commanding spirits and the gateway to entering the Dao.

---

Guan Yinzi said: With every inhalation and exhalation, a person traverses four hundred thousand *li* in a day—transformation may indeed be called swift. One breath consists of one inhalation and one exhalation. In a day and night, there are thirteen thousand five hundred breaths. With each breath, qi travels six inches, so in a day and night, qi travels eight hundred ten *zhang*. This is fifty circuits of the celestial sphere. Ten hundreds make a thousand, ten thousands make a myriad, ten myriads make a *yi*, and ten *yi* make a *zhao*. In one breath, a person traverses four hundred thousand *li* in a day—meaning one breath covers four hundred million *li*. Ten breaths cover four *zhao* *li*, a hundred breaths cover forty *zhao* *li*, a thousand breaths cover four hundred *zhao* *li*, ten thousand breaths cover four thousand *zhao* *li*, three thousand breaths cover one thousand two hundred *zhao* *li*, and five hundred breaths cover two hundred *zhao* *li*. In total, a day and night sees a traversal of five thousand four hundred *zhao* *li*. From this, we know that the great transformation may indeed be called swift. Thus it is said: *with every inhalation and exhalation, a person traverses four hundred thousand* li *in a day—transformation may indeed be called swift.*

Only the sage remains unchanged. The sage, penetrating the Dao of true emptiness, is without self—neither existing nor not existing. True emptiness transcends past and present, without alteration. As the ancients said: *adding a year, subtracting a year—true emptiness remains unmoved.* Thus it is said: *only the sage remains unchanged.* *Unchanged* means *without self.*

This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to the great transformation but rather directs it.

---

Guan Yinzi said: The Blue Phoenix Master lived a thousand years and underwent a thousand transformations. Peach Master served five times and his heart changed five times. The Blue Phoenix Master was an ancient sage. The sage’s form changes with time, shifting and altering year after year until a thousand years pass, never ceasing to transform. Yet within these transformations, there is that which does not transform—the unchanging, eternally constant Dao. For example, a sage at sixty years of age has undergone sixty transformations, yet what began as right may end as wrong. Thus it is said: *the Blue Phoenix Master lived a thousand years and underwent a thousand transformations.* The Peach Master was an ancient worthy. Serving five times in office, his heart changed five times. Like Lian Boyu, who at sixty transformed sixty times, not knowing whether sixty was right or fifty-nine was wrong. As the ancients said: *a lamp’s flame in a jar—later light is not the earlier light. Borrowing form from a mirror—today’s reflection is not yesterday’s.* Thus it is said: *Peach Master served five times and his heart changed five times.*

The sage treats affairs as a guest and distances himself from things. How could he not wish to establish himself in the world? Yet he fears the unknowable nature of transformation in forms and numbers. *Affairs* means *use*. The sage treats affairs as a guest, not making use the primary concern. The body of the Dao is true emptiness, unattached to the realm of things, which naturally recedes. Thus it is said: *the sage treats affairs as a guest and distances himself from things.* The sage does not prioritize establishing wondrous functions but preserves the substance of true emptiness. Responding only when moved, his responses remain ever tranquil. How could he not wish to establish wondrous functions to benefit the world? Yet he does not make use the primary concern. If he were to prioritize establishing wondrous functions, treating the substance of true emptiness as secondary, he would fall into the traces of forms and numbers—the causes of minor karmic fruits in the human and heavenly realms, like a shadow following form. Though not substantial, once fallen into the traces of forms and numbers, he would be subject to the great transformation’s shifts and changes. From this, we see that those bound by forms and numbers fear being swept away by the great transformation. Those who study the Dao must awaken to this truth.

Thus it is said: how could he not wish to establish himself in the world? Yet those bound by forms and numbers fear the unfathomable transformations of the Dao. The unfathomable lies in this: as soon as knowledge sprouts, one falls into the great transformation. This chapter clarifies that the substance of the true must be primary, while establishing wondrous functions is secondary. With substance and function both present, transformation transforms yet does not transform.

**Chapter Five**
Master Guan Yin says: The myriad things undergo ceaseless change, now hidden, now manifest, yet their qi is but one. Only the sage knows the One and does not transform.
The myriad things arise and grow, age and die in an instant—there is no moment they do not change. In spring, Wood is hidden while Fire appears; in summer, Fire is hidden while Metal appears; in autumn, Metal is hidden while Water appears; in winter, Water is hidden while Wood appears. Earth is hidden and manifest through the four seasons. The Five Phases and four seasons alternately hide and appear, generating and transforming without end—all driven by the revolving of a single qi, mutually impelling and stirring to enact the workings of spirits. Thus it is said: though the myriad things undergo ceaseless change, now hidden, now manifest, their qi is but one. The sage, awakened to the wondrous Dao of non-duality, abides like empty space and does not shift with the great transformation. Thus it is said: the sage knows the One and does not transform. *Knowing* here means awakening. This chapter clarifies that the Dao moves the great transformation yet does not transform.

**Chapter Six**
Master Guan Yin says: The growth of nails, the lengthening of hair, the circulation of *ying* and *wei*—none ceases for even an instant. The multitude see only what is coarse, not what is subtle.
The nails on a person’s hands and the hair on their head grow and recede in constant flux, just as the blood’s *ying* and the qi’s *wei* circulate day and night without pause—all these, like the growth of nails and hair, never cease for even an instant. Thus it is said: the growth of nails, the lengthening of hair, the circulation of *ying* and *wei*—none ceases for even an instant. *Coarse* refers to overt traces. The multitude see only overt, manifest traces but cannot perceive the subtle growth of nails and hair or the circulation of *ying* and *wei*. Thus it is said: the multitude see only what is coarse, not what is subtle. The worthy see what is subtle yet cannot yield to transformation.
*(The following eleven characters are added in Guo Ziqian’s edition, which is well-ordered, so they are included here in the explication:)* The worthy perceive the subtle but cannot fathom the heart-mind, and thus cannot yield to the transformation that is no-transformation. Thus it is said: the worthy see what is subtle yet cannot yield to transformation. The sage yields to transformation, and thus there is no transformation. The sage awakens to the unchanging, independent substance and yields to the inexhaustible, ceaseless function. Yielding to the inexhaustible, ceaseless function is yielding to transformation; awakening to the unchanging, independent substance is no-transformation. Thus it is said: the sage yields to transformation, and thus there is no transformation. This chapter clarifies that the Dao is independent and unchanging, circulating without cease.

**Chapter Seven**
Master Guan Yin says: Within the chamber, one’s perceptions are constant. Then one goes to the gate, to the neighbors, to the village, to the district, then to the outskirts, to the mountains, to the rivers—perceptions differ, likes and dislikes follow, harmony and contention arise, gain and loss take shape.
The inner chambers of a hall are called *rooms*; five households make a *neighborhood*, five neighborhoods a *village*, five hundred households a *district*, and what lies beyond the city walls is called the *outskirts*. When one dwells within the chamber, the sights and sounds of people and things are taken as ordinary, daily perceptions. Thus it is said: within the chamber, one’s perceptions are constant. *Goes* means to proceed. First one leaves the room for the courtyard gate, then for the neighborhood, the village, the district, or further to the outskirts, the mountains, the rivers—eyes behold different forms and appearances, ears hear different sounds, so perceptions vary. Thus it is said: then one goes to the gate, to the neighbors, to the village, to the district, then to the outskirts, to the mountains, to the rivers—perceptions differ. As sights and sounds differ, some evoke fondness and desire, others aversion and dislike; some bring harmony and shared joy, others discord and contention; some can be grasped and gained, others lost and abandoned. Such circumstances shift with emotions and take shape accordingly. Thus it is said: likes and dislikes follow, harmony and contention arise, gain and loss take shape. Therefore the sage’s movements and stillness are governed by precepts.
*Stillness* means quietude; *precepts* means restraint. The sage instructs disciples that in every movement and stillness, they must restrain themselves from sensory indulgence, cautiously guarding their conduct and concealment, not allowing perceptions of sights and sounds to alter their course. Thus it is said: therefore the sage’s movements and stillness are governed by precepts. If these are precepts for disciples, what need has the sage for them? This chapter clarifies that the deluded are swayed by sights and sounds.

Master Guan Yinzi said: Just as the great sea can transform into countless dragons and fish, yet the water remains one and the same. The great sea is likened to the Dao that operates the great transformation; the dragons and fish are likened to the worthy and the foolish. The great sea can transform into countless dragons and fish, all born within the same water. The Dao can operate the great transformation, altering the myriad worthy and foolish, all born within the same Dao. Thus it is said: just as the great sea can transform into countless dragons and fish, yet the water remains one and the same. This serves as an analogy.

I and all things flourish abundantly, thriving within the great transformation, yet their nature remains one and the same. *Flourishing* describes the dense growth of grasses and trees; *thriving* depicts their lush abundance. I and all things flourish and thrive like untamed grasses and luxuriant forests, innumerable, all within the great transformation, their Dao-nature undivided. Thus it is said: I and all things flourish abundantly, thriving within the great transformation, yet their nature remains one and the same. This completes the analogy.

Those who fully awaken to the unconditioned, non-dual Dao see no faults in others beyond nature, nor do they perceive a self within. Their minds merge with the great void, free from annihilation and birth. Thus it is said: those who know that nature is one are without others and without self, without death and without birth. *Death* means annihilation. This chapter clarifies that the Dao-nature transcends self and others, and that death and birth do not belong to the great transformation.

---

Master Guan Yinzi said: Among the principles of the world, right may transform into wrong, and wrong may transform into right; kindness may turn into enmity, and enmity may turn into kindness. The principles of the world shift with human delusions. What aligns with one’s self is deemed right; yet when public affairs conflict with private desires, yesterday’s right may transform into today’s wrong. What differs from one’s self is deemed wrong; yet when private matters align with private desires, yesterday’s wrong may transform into today’s right. Thus it is said: among the principles of the world, right may transform into wrong, and wrong may transform into right.

The greatest kindness in the world is none other than that of ruler and father. Yet when public affairs and public principles conflict with private desires, yesterday’s greatest kindness may transform into today’s greatest enmity—such is the case with those who murder their rulers or fathers. The greatest enmity in the world is none other than that of rival states. Yet when private matters and private principles align with private desires, yesterday’s greatest enmity may transform into today’s greatest kindness—such is the case with rival states forming alliances and intermarrying. Thus it is said: kindness may turn into enmity, and enmity may turn into kindness.

Therefore, the sage abides in constancy while contemplating change. *Therefore* is a phrase connecting the preceding to the following. The sage instructs disciples to rest in the great constancy of nature while guarding against the petty changes of emotion, not yielding to selfishness. Thus it is said: therefore, the sage abides in constancy while contemplating change. *Abides* means to rest; *contemplates* means to reflect carefully and guard against negligence. This chapter clarifies that yielding to the petty changes of deluded emotions distorts right and wrong, kindness and enmity, while embracing the great constancy of true nature transcends distinctions of right and wrong, kindness and enmity.

---

Master Guan Yinzi said: In youth, one should heed the teachings of father and elder brothers; in maturity, one should comprehend the admonitions of friends; in old age, one should caution the young and mature with one’s counsel. Though the myriad transformations shift, they cannot constrain me.

In one’s youth, when father and elder brothers offer instructive words, one should inscribe them upon the body and follow them without forgetfulness. Thus it is said: in youth, one should heed the teachings of father and elder brothers. In maturity, when fellow disciples or like-minded friends offer admonitions, remedies, and moral guidance, one should fully understand and not shrink from correcting faults, daily renewing one’s virtue. Thus it is said: in maturity, one should comprehend the admonitions of friends.

In old age, having experienced much and discerned the principles of right and wrong, gain and loss, one should use these insights to caution the young and mature, tirelessly expounding and instructing—this is the heart of a benevolent person. Thus it is said: in old age, one should caution the young and mature with one’s counsel. As described above, in youth one follows the teachings of father and elder brothers, in maturity one comprehends the admonitions of friends, and in old age one cautions the young and mature. Thus one does not violate the principles of being human.

Abiding by principle and wandering through the world, though the myriad transformations subtly shift, one does not resist the Creator but follows it without peril. Thus it is said: though the myriad transformations shift, they cannot constrain me. This chapter clarifies that abiding by principle allows one to wander with the Creator without constraint.

---

Master Guan Yinzi said: Among the principles of the world, the light are easily transformed, while the heavy are difficult to transform. The principles of the world discard the light and pure qi, whose bearers possess keen roots and bright natures, making them easily transformed and awakened. Thus it is said: among the principles of the world, the light are easily transformed. Those who bear the heavy and turbid qi possess dull roots and obscure natures, making them difficult to transform and awaken. Thus it is said: the heavy are difficult to transform.

For example, wind and clouds vanish in an instant, while the nature of metal and jade endures unchanged over time. *Vanish* means to transform. For instance, the qi of wind and clouds changes and vanishes in a moment, disappearing without a trace. Thus it is said: for example, wind and clouds vanish in an instant. This illustrates the earlier point that the light are easily transformed. The nature of metal is firm, and the nature of jade is hard, enabling them to endure for a long time without alteration. Thus it is said: the nature of metal and jade endures unchanged over time. This illustrates the earlier point that the heavy are difficult to transform.

Those who are light and bright can transform along with creation without lingering, as if an untransformed essence remains.

Those endowed with the light and pure qi possess a nature that is bright and keen, their minds readily awakening to understanding. They can wander in unison with the transformations of creation without obstruction. Thus it is said: those who are clear and bright can transform along with creation without lingering. *Without lingering* means without obstruction. Having awakened to wander with the transformations of creation without obstruction, they follow change yet remain unchanged, undergoing minor transformations while preserving the great constancy. Thus it is said: as if an untransformed essence remains. *Remains* means preserving the great constancy. This chapter clarifies that those of keen nature easily awaken, following change yet remaining unchanged.

**Chapter Twelve**

Master Guan Yin said: When two children are fond of each other, by the time they reach adulthood, upon meeting they no longer recognize one another. When two adults are fond of each other, by the time they grow old, upon meeting they no longer recognize one another. Like the transformations of sparrows into clams, hawks into turtledoves—without past, without present.

When two children love each other, delighting in play and intimacy, after parting for many years and both reaching vigorous adulthood, their appearances altered and forms changed, upon meeting again, they cannot recognize each other as they did in childhood. Thus it is said: when two children are fond of each other, by the time they reach adulthood, upon meeting they no longer recognize one another. When two adults form an inseparable bond, delighting in shared studies, after parting for many years, their forms and faces transformed, hair turned white and complexions aged, skin slackened and wrinkled, upon meeting again, they cannot recognize each other as they did in their prime. Thus it is said: when two adults are fond of each other, by the time they grow old, upon meeting they no longer recognize one another.

The transformation of children into adults, of adults into the elderly, is like sparrows entering water and becoming clams, or turtledoves transforming into hawks. The *Book of Transformations* states: snakes transform into tortoises, sparrows into clams. The *Liezi* also states: sparrow hawks transform into cranes, cranes into cuckoos, and cuckoos back into sparrow hawks. The cuckoo is another name for the turtledove. Thus it is said: like the transformations of sparrows into clams, hawks into turtledoves—without past, without present. The sparrow is the past of the clam, the clam the present of the sparrow; when a sparrow becomes a clam, the clam is entirely the sparrow. When a turtledove becomes a hawk, the hawk is entirely the turtledove. Though their forms change, their nature knows no past or present. This chapter clarifies that form follows transformation without cease, while nature transcends past and present without alteration.

**End of Commentary on the *Wenshi Zhenjing*, Scroll Seven**

**#1** "Use" (*yong*)—based on the preceding scriptural text, this should read "pervade" (*zhou*).

**#2** This sentence appears in both the *Guanyinzi* and the *Wushang Miaodao Wenshi Zhenjing* as: "and is easier to trust than concrete objects."

**#3** According to the preceding scriptural text, this sentence should read: "travels four hundred thousand *li* in a day."

**Commentary on the *Wenshi Zhenjing*, Scroll Eight**
*Divine Peak’s* [Shenfeng] *Carefree Master Niu Daochun’s Direct Explication*

**The Eight Strategies**
*Strategy* (*chou*) means "thing" (*wu*). In all, six chapters.

Master Guanyin said: The ancients skilled in divination—whether casting stalks or scorching tortoise shells—could reveal the ancient within the present, the present within the ancient; the high within the low, the low within the high; the small within the great, the great within the small; the one within the many, the many within the one; the human within things, things within the human; the self within the other, the other within the self.
