**First Chapter on Scapulimancy and Pyromancy**
The ancients possessed skill in divining with milfoil stalks and scorching tortoise shells—like searing a hot iron awl to bore into the shell—interpreting the cracks to discern the auspices and inauspices of past, future, and present events. They could use present affairs to illuminate ancient principles, and in turn, apply ancient principles to reveal the gains and losses of present affairs. Thus it is said: the ancients skilled in divining with stalks and scorching tortoise shells could reveal the ancient within the present, the present within the ancient.

The *high* refers to Heaven, which pertains to *yang*—and *yang* pertains to auspice. The *low* refers to Earth, which pertains to *yin*—and *yin* pertains to inauspice. Through divination with stalks and scorched shells, they could reveal inauspice within auspice to inform people, and likewise, auspice within inauspice. Thus it is said: the high within the low, the low within the high.

The *small* refers to the individual body; the *great*, to the realm, state, and family. Through divination with stalks and scorched shells, they could reveal the fortunes and misfortunes of the realm, state, and family within the individual body, and conversely, the auspices and inauspices of the individual body within the realm, state, and family. Thus it is said: the small within the great, the great within the small.

The *one* refers to the sovereign; the *many*, to the common people. Through divination with stalks and scorched shells, they could reveal the fortunes and misfortunes of the common people to the sovereign, and conversely, the auspices and inauspices of the sovereign to the common people. Thus it is said: the one within the many, the many within the one.

*Things* refer to material wealth. Through divination with stalks and scorched shells, they could reveal the abundance or scarcity of wealth based on a person’s gains and losses, and conversely, a person’s gains and losses based on the abundance or scarcity of wealth. Thus it is said: the human within things, things within the human.

The *other* refers to parents, siblings, wives, and children. Through divination with stalks and scorched shells, they could reveal the survival, loss, abundance, or scarcity of parents, siblings, wives, and children based on the self’s Eight Characters, and conversely, the self’s nobility, baseness, fortunes, and misfortunes based on the survival, loss, abundance, or scarcity of parents, siblings, wives, and children. Thus it is said: the self within the other, the other within the self.

This Dao—its coming knows no present, its going knows no past; its height has no canopy, its depth has no foundation; its greatness has no beyond, its smallness has no within; its root is not one, its branches are not many; its exterior has no things, its interior has no people; its nearness has no self, its farness has no other.

*This* refers to the present. This true emptiness, this inconceivable Dao, is not like divination with stalks and scorched shells, which analyzes the six pairs of opposites—past and present, high and low, great and small, one and many, human and things, self and other.

The Dao does not belong to time; it comes and goes alone, without distinction between past and present. Thus it is said: this Dao—its coming knows no present, its going knows no past.

The Dao does not belong to form or position; it fills the void, pervading above and below, present everywhere—unlike Heaven and Earth, fixed in canopy and foundation. Thus it is said: its height has no canopy, its depth has no foundation.

The Dao does not belong to interior or exterior, center or periphery. In its greatness, it is boundlessly vast; in its smallness, it is invisible. Thus it is said: its greatness has no beyond, its smallness has no within.

The Dao does not belong to number; it cannot be fixed as one or many. The Dao is the root-source of the Five Ultimates, the Two Modes, and the myriad things. It circulates as a single *qi*, transforming and generating Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things. Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things never depart from the Dao; if they were to separate from it, they would collapse and perish. From this, we know that Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are the branches of the Dao. Thus it is said: its root is not one, its branches are not many.

This line—*"its root is not one, its branches are not many"*—appears in Guo Ziqian’s edition, where it fits well within the sequence of the scripture. Thus, it is also explained here.

The Dao does not belong to things or people; interior and exterior are not two. Thus it is said: its exterior has no things, its interior has no people.

The Dao does not belong to self or other; far and near are not two. Thus it is said: its nearness has no self, its farness has no other.

It cannot be divided, cannot be united; it cannot be described, cannot be conceived. Only in its undifferentiated wholeness is it the Dao.

The Dao is like empty space—without dispersion, without aggregation. Thus it is said: it cannot be divided, cannot be united.

The Dao has no likeness, no comparison, no sentience. It cannot be conveyed through speech or analogy, nor grasped through thought or understanding. Thus it is said: it cannot be described, cannot be conceived.

If one awakens to it, it is inherently complete, inherently manifest, inherently whole and true—requiring no cultivation, fabrication, or contrivance. Such is what we forcibly name the Dao. Thus it is said: only in its undifferentiated wholeness is it the Dao.

This chapter clarifies that the Dao does not belong to form or number and cannot be known through analysis.

**End of First Chapter**

Master Guanyin said: Water, being latent, stores the Five Essences; fire, being soaring, extends the Five Odors; wood, being flourishing, blooms into the Five Colors; metal, being firm, solidifies into the Five Sounds; earth, being harmonious, nourishes the Five Flavors.

Master Guanyin said: Essence is the essence of spirit; water’s nature delights in concealment within, thus it accumulates and becomes the essence of the Five Worms. The Five Worms are those with scales, feathers, fur, carapaces, and bare skin. Water, when stored in the viscera, is the kidneys; among the Five Spirits, it is essence. Thus it is said: water, being latent, stores the Five Essences.

Fire’s nature delights in soaring outward, thus it permeates the nose with the Five Odors—rank, putrid, fragrant, scorched, and rotten. Thus it is said: fire, being soaring, extends the Five Odors.

Wood’s nature delights in flourishing, thus it blooms into the Five Colors—blue-green, yellow, red, white, and black. Thus it is said: wood, being flourishing, blooms into the Five Colors.

Metal’s nature is firm and hard, thus when struck, it produces the Five Sounds—gong, shang, jue, zhi, and yu. When heard, these are the Five Sounds.

Earth’s nature is gentle and harmonious, thus it nourishes the Five Flavors—sour, salty, sweet, acrid, and bitter. Thus it is said: earth, being harmonious, nourishes the Five Flavors.

In their constancy, they are five; in their transformations, they are beyond reckoning. In their substances, they are five; in their admixtures, they are beyond reckoning.

Their constancy as five refers to the Five Constants: benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom, and trust. Benevolence pertains to wood, righteousness to metal, propriety to fire, wisdom to water, and trust to earth.

Their substances as five are metal, wood, water, fire, and earth.

Transformations are admixtures.

These Five Phases—metal, wood, water, fire, and earth—when observed above, become the Five Stars; when viewed below, become the Five Peaks; when positioned, become the Five Directions; when transformed, become the Five Colors; when struck, become the Five Sounds; when grouped, become the Five Surnames; when materialized, become the Five Metals; when vaporized, become the Five Odors; when concealed, become the Five Essences; when nourished, become the Five Flavors; when moved, become the Five Worms; when planted, become the Five Grains; when cultivated, become the Five Virtues; when corrupted, become the Five Thieves; within the body, become the Five Viscera; within the spirit, become the Five Spirits; within perception, become the Five Emotions.

Thus it is said: in their constancy, they are five; in their transformations, they are beyond reckoning. In their substances, they are five; in their admixtures, they are beyond reckoning.

Yet the myriad things between Heaven and Earth cannot be rigidly called ten thousand, nor rigidly called five, nor rigidly called one.

The Five Phases transform and give rise to the myriad things, teeming and bustling between Heaven and Earth. Their admixtures are beyond number—how could they be rigidly called ten thousand? Thus it is said: the myriad things between Heaven and Earth cannot be rigidly called ten thousand.

The myriad things belong to the Five Phases. In their constancy, they are five; in their transformations, they are beyond reckoning—how could they be rigidly called the Five Phases? Thus it is said: they cannot be rigidly called five.

The Five Phases belong to the One. The One unifies the billions, ceaselessly generating and transforming—how could it be rigidly called one? Thus it is said: they cannot be rigidly called one.

They cannot be rigidly called not ten thousand, nor rigidly called not five, nor rigidly called not one.

Between Heaven and Earth, the profusion of things—verdant and lush, teeming and bustling—how could they be merely called the myriad things? Thus it is said: they cannot be rigidly called not ten thousand.

Though the myriad things teem in abundance, they ultimately belong to the Five Phases. Thus it is said: they cannot be rigidly called not five.

Though the Five Phases transform beyond reckoning, they ultimately belong to the governance of the One Qi. Thus it is said: they cannot be rigidly called not one.

At times they unite, at times they separate—through this, form is necessarily determined; through this, number is necessarily determined; through this, qi is necessarily determined. Yet this is but self-exertion; things do not know me, nor do I know things.

At times the myriad things and Five Phases unite as One; at times the One Qi separates into the Five Phases and myriad things. Thus it is said: at times they unite, at times they separate.

Since there is union and separation, through this, the forms of the myriad things are necessarily determined. Thus it is said: through this, form is necessarily determined.

Through this, the myriad things necessarily belong to the numbers of the Five Phases. Thus it is said: through this, number is necessarily determined.

Through this, the Five Phases necessarily belong to the One Qi. Thus it is said: through this, qi is necessarily determined.

The prior distinctions of the myriad things, Five Phases, and One Qi all belong to deluded sentiment, having already fallen into the transformations of form, number, and qi. This is but self-exertion. If deluded sentiment returns to true nature, then things and self are thus, each unaware of the other, transcending the transformations of form, number, and qi. Thus it is said: yet this is but self-exertion; things do not know me, nor do I know things.

This chapter clarifies that as soon as deluded sentiment arises, one falls into the transformations of form, number, and qi. When deluded sentiment returns to its root, then form, number, and qi cannot be grasped or constrained.

**End of Second Chapter**

Master Guanyin said: Within my mind, the myriad things may arise. For where the mind goes, love follows; where love follows, essence follows.

The mind is fundamentally pure and quiescent, originally without a single thing, equal to the Great Void. Upon this pure mind, a deluded sentiment suddenly arises. Unaware of its delusion, sentiments follow one another in succession, accumulating into the myriad sentiments, and the myriad sentiments delude and stain the myriad things. As the scripture says: "One sentiment recognizes it, accumulating into the myriad sentiments; the myriad sentiments recognize it, accumulating into the myriad things." Thus it is said: within my mind, the myriad things may arise.

Where the mind’s deluded sentiment goes, it follows the beloved realm. Love pertains to water, and water pertains to essence. Where the mind’s sentiment loves a realm, the essence-spirit also follows. Thus it is said: where the mind goes, love follows; where love follows, essence follows.

"Goes" means to proceed.

Where the mind congeals, it first coagulates into water. When the mind longs for things, saliva flows; when the mind grieves for things, tears flow; when the mind feels shame for things, sweat flows.

The original qi pervades the body, manifesting in response to the mind’s feelings. Where the mind has attachments, it becomes bound by the realm to which it clings. Thus it is said: where the mind congeals.

When the mind first congeals and stagnates with things, the original qi transforms into water. If the mind longs for edible things it loves, the original qi within transforms into saliva and manifests. Thus it is said: it first congeals into water; when the mind longs for things, saliva flows. When the mind is moved by sorrow—such as the death of kin—grief suddenly arises, and the original qi within the eyes transforms into tears and manifests. Thus it is said: when the mind grieves for things, tears flow. When one commits unvirtuous deeds and shame suddenly arises upon seeing others, the original qi upon the face transforms into sweat and manifests. Thus it is said: when the mind feels shame for things, sweat flows.

There is no momentary state that does not endure, and no enduring state that does not change. "Momentary" means not enduring; "enduring" means constant stillness; "change" means movement. If one cannot awaken to the true emptiness and constant stillness of the mind’s essence due to fleeting thoughts of arising and ceasing, it is said: there is no momentary state that does not endure. If one cannot respond to the myriad movements of change with constant tranquility because the mind’s essence of true emptiness and stillness is not realized, it is said: there is no enduring state that does not change.

Water generates wood, wood generates fire, fire generates earth, earth generates metal, metal generates water—mutually engendering and overcoming without end. The heart viscus pertains to fire and houses the spirit; its emotional nature delights in joy. The liver viscus pertains to wood and houses the ethereal soul; its emotional nature delights in anger. The spleen viscus pertains to earth and houses intention; its emotional nature delights in contemplation. The lung viscus pertains to metal and houses the corporeal soul; its emotional nature delights in sorrow. The kidney viscus pertains to water and houses essence; its emotional nature delights in fear.

Essence generates the ethereal soul, so fear gives rise to anger—thus it is said: water generates wood. The ethereal soul generates the spirit, so anger gives rise to joy—thus it is said: wood generates fire. The spirit generates intention, so joy gives rise to contemplation—thus it is said: fire generates earth. Intention generates the corporeal soul, so contemplation gives rise to sorrow—thus it is said: earth generates metal. The corporeal soul generates essence, so sorrow gives rise to fear—thus it is said: metal generates water. The character "overcome" should be "generate," likely a scribal error; it should read "mutually generating and overcoming."

In mutual overcoming: water overcomes fire, fire overcomes metal, metal overcomes wood, wood overcomes earth, and earth overcomes water. The Five Phases mutually generate and overcome, giving rise to the myriad things of heaven and earth. The myriad things mutually generate and overcome in ways beyond calculation. Thus it is said: mutually generating and overcoming, without end.

The Infant and the Medicinal Maiden, the Golden Tower and the Crimson Palace, the Azure Dragon and the White Tiger, the Precious Cauldron and the Red Furnace—all are these things, yet there exists that which is not these things. The kidney viscus pertains to Kan (☵), and the yang line within Kan is called the Infant, the qi of original yang transformed from essence. The heart viscus pertains to Li (☲), and the yin line within Li is called the Medicinal Maiden, the spirit of the heart’s fluid. The lung viscus pertains to Dui (☱), which governs the twelve joints and is called the Golden Tower, also known as the Twelve-Storied Tower. The heart viscus pertains to fire, whose color is red, and is called the Crimson Palace. The liver viscus pertains to wood, houses the ethereal soul, and is green in color—called the Azure Dragon. The lung viscus pertains to metal, houses the corporeal soul, and is white in color—called the White Tiger. Thus it is said: the Infant and the Medicinal Maiden, the Golden Tower and the Crimson Palace, the Azure Dragon and the White Tiger.

The original qi within the body pertains to yang, and yang pertains to Qian (☰), which pertains to metal—thus it is called the Precious Cauldron. The spirit within the heart pertains to yin, and yin pertains to Kun (☷); the heart pertains to fire, whose color is red—thus it is called the Red Furnace. Body and mind are called the Qian-Kun Cauldron and Furnace. Since body and mind are the cauldron and furnace, spirit and qi are the medicinal substances. Thus it is said: the Precious Cauldron and the Red Furnace are all these things. "These things" refer to spirit and qi.

The Dao has no form or name; it is the ancestor of spirit and qi. The ancestor of spirit and qi is the inconceivable Dao. Thus it is said: there exists that which is not these things. This chapter clarifies how the Dao moves spirit and qi, transforming endlessly.

**Chapter Three**
Guan Yinzi said: Birds and beasts suddenly *yo-yo*, suddenly *xun-xun*, suddenly flee; plants and trees suddenly sprout, suddenly stand tall, suddenly wither. Heaven and earth cannot retain them, nor can the sage bind them.

*Yo-yo* is the sound of birds and beasts when their young are first born. *Xun-xun* describes birds and beasts growing large after several ten-day periods. "Flee" describes birds and beasts growing old and dying, vanishing like a person in flight. "Sprout" describes plants and trees first putting forth buds. "Stand tall" describes plants and trees growing large and erect. "Wither" describes plants and trees growing old and withering, their leaves fallen. It speaks of birds and beasts suddenly born, suddenly grown, and in an instant transformed into death. Thus it is said: birds and beasts suddenly *yo-yo*, suddenly *xun-xun*, suddenly flee. Plants and trees suddenly sprout, suddenly stand tall, and in an instant transformed into withering death. Thus it is said: plants and trees suddenly sprout, suddenly stand tall, suddenly wither.

Birds and beasts cannot but be born when it is time to be born, cannot but grow when it is time to grow, cannot but age when it is time to age, and cannot but die when it is time to die. These four—birth, growth, aging, and death—are the flowing of the great transformation. Even the vastness of heaven and earth and the spiritual powers of the sage cannot momentarily retain or bind them, preventing birth, growth, aging, and death. Thus it is said: heaven and earth cannot retain them, nor can the sage bind them.

Yet there is that which operates within. The Creator revolves the one qi, with yin-yang, the Five Phases, and the four seasons mutually pushing and stirring, generating and transforming without end. Thus it is said: there is that which operates within.

Being resides there; non-being resides here. The drum does not sound without being struck. Evenness resides there; oddness resides here. The mallet does not strike without the hand.

The workings of creation and transformation exist there, bound within the deluded emotions and the numerical patterns of form and qi—thus it is said: "Being resides there." The Way of true emptiness and nothingness abides here, in the heart that has awakened to clarity, merging obscurely with the Dao, no longer subject to form, qi, time, or numerical patterns. How then could the workings of creation transform it? Thus it is said: "Non-being resides here."

When the heart has awakened, false emotions do not arise, and the defilements of the external world are not clung to. Pure and still, it is like empty space—how could the Creator mold or cast empty space? When the heart is thus enlightened, it is like a drum that does not sound without being struck by the mallet—thus it is said: "The drum does not sound without being struck."

To be paired with the workings of creation is to dwell there in deluded emotion—thus it is said: "Evenness resides there." Not to be paired with the workings of creation is to stand alone and unmatched, abiding here in the awakened heart of true emptiness—thus it is said: "Oddness resides here." If the heart of true emptiness has not yet sprouted, how could there be deluded emotions or defilements of the external world? When true emptiness is thus, it is like a mallet that cannot strike the drum without the hand—thus it is said: "The mallet does not strike without the hand."

This chapter clarifies that to be deluded and defiled by the myriad things is to be unable to escape the great transformation, while to awaken to true emptiness is for the great transformation to be unable to alter one.

**Chapter Four**

Master Guan Yin said: It is all one thing.
The multitude are confused by names, seeing things but not the Dao. The worthy analyze its principles, seeing the Dao but not things.

The "one" is the undivided Dao. "Things" are the ten thousand things of heaven and earth. The ten thousand things of heaven and earth all rely on the undivided Dao for their existence—thus it is said: "It is all one thing." The Dao dwells within the ten thousand things of heaven and earth, omnipresent. The multitude are confused by the names of the ten thousand things, their hearts deluded and attached to things, seeing things but not the undivided Dao—thus it is said: "The multitude are confused by names, seeing things but not the Dao." The worthy can analyze affairs and principles, clearly and distinctly, perceiving the Dao while forgetting external things—thus it is said: "The worthy analyze its principles, seeing the Dao but not things."

The sage unites with heaven, seeing neither Dao nor things, for the one Dao is all Dao.
The sage, having awakened, obscurely harmonizes with the celestial truth, realizing the essence of the Dao and forgetting the name of the Dao—how much more so things? Thus it is said: "The sage unites with heaven, seeing neither Dao nor things." The origin of the Dao is undivided, and the Dao is omnipresent—thus it is said: "The one Dao is all Dao."

Not to grasp it is the Dao; to grasp it is a thing.
When the heart abides nowhere and the whole body is released, it obscurely harmonizes with the wondrous existence of true emptiness—thus it is said: "Not to grasp it is the Dao." When the heart abides somewhere, clinging to various extremes, it is deluded and pursues external things—thus it is said: "To grasp it is a thing."

This chapter clarifies that without thorough awakening, the worthy and the foolish alike remain deluded, while with complete clarity, the ordinary and the sage are not two.

**Chapter Five**

Master Guan Yin said: One who knows the falsity of things need not reject them. It is like clay oxen and wooden horses: though the emotions retain the names of oxen and horses, the heart forgets their reality.

"Knows" means to awaken. To awaken to the fact that all external things are false and illusory naturally prevents the heart from clinging, leaving it pure and still—there is no need to forcibly remove external things. Thus it is said: "One who knows the falsity of things need not reject them." To awaken to the falsity and illusoriness of all external things is to be naturally undefiled. It is like oxen made of clay or horses carved from wood: though the emotions retain the names and forms of oxen and horses, the heart naturally forgets their true reality—thus it is said: "It is like clay oxen and wooden horses: though the emotions retain the names of oxen and horses, the heart forgets their reality."

This chapter clarifies that awakening to the falsity of external things naturally leaves the heart pure and undefiled.

**Chapter Six**
*End of the Eighth Scroll of the Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing*

**Chapter Nine: The Nine Medicines**

The term "medicine" here denotes a comprehensive treatment, comprising thirty-one chapters in total.

Master Guan Yin said:
Do not take small matters lightly, for a tiny crack may sink a great ship;
Do not take small things lightly, for a tiny insect may poison the body;
Do not take petty men lightly, for petty men may plunder the state.

Those who study the Dao must accumulate even the slightest good, and eliminate even the smallest faults. Nothing should be treated lightly or neglected. Though a matter may seem trivial, it must not be dismissed with indifference. Just as a ship of ten thousand *hu*, though its hull may bear but a pinhole, will sink if the breach is not sealed, so it is said: "Do not take small matters lightly, for a tiny crack may sink a great ship."

"Must not" is an admonitory phrase, and "crack" refers to an opening. One must not treat small things with carelessness. For instance, a scorpion or a seven-inch snake, though small, may sting and poison a person if approached with undue confidence. Thus it is said: "Do not take small things lightly, for a tiny insect may poison the body."

One must not treat petty men with carelessness. Consider Huang Chao, the Red Eyebrows, and the Yellow Turbans—though they were but petty men, they brought ruin to the Han and Tang dynasties. How much more so for a state? Thus it is said: "Do not take petty men lightly, for petty men may plunder the state."

Only by attending to small matters can one accomplish great deeds;
Only by accumulating small things can one achieve great things;
Only by treating petty men with kindness can one align with great men.

"Matters" refer to application and practice. When one acts with impartiality and universal benefit, not neglecting even the smallest conduct, one may attain the supreme virtue of "non-virtue"—the ultimate virtue. This ultimate virtue is the great power of the Dao, hence it is said: "Only by attending to small matters can one accomplish great deeds."

"Small things" refer to minor deeds and subtle merits. One should protect even grasses, trees, and insects without harming them, benefiting all with wondrous existence like a timely rain that falls equally upon thorns, crops, pure and impure fields. Just as the great sea does not discriminate between clean and turbid waters but embraces all without distinction, so too does it become the king of all valleys. When such meritorious conduct accumulates to greatness, one becomes a sage or a worthy. Is this not the making of a great person? Thus it is said: "Only by accumulating small things can one achieve great things."

To treat all with benevolent virtue and universal benefit, without distinction between noble and petty men, and with equal compassion, is to win the hearts of people. All will revere and respect such a one, and thus one may align with the Dao of great men. Hence it is said: "Only by treating petty men with kindness can one align with great men."

Heaven cannot be relied upon to determine human affairs, nor can humans be relied upon to determine events. Only by relinquishing affairs and distancing oneself from others does the self remain within the self—whatever is fitting, let it be so.

A person’s nobility or baseness, wealth or poverty, longevity or early death, success or failure—these lie within the mandate of Heaven. How can one predetermine whether one will be good or evil, deluded or awakened, a petty man, a noble man, a worthy, or a sage? Thus it is said: "Heaven cannot be relied upon to determine human affairs."

"Affairs" refer to application. When applied to evil, one becomes a petty man; when applied to good, one becomes a noble man; when applied to virtue, one becomes a worthy; when applied to the Dao, one becomes a sage. How can human application be rigidly fixed? Thus it is said: "Nor can humans be relied upon to determine events."

If one abandons all entanglements, severs attachment to the appearances of others, and conceals all wondrous functions within non-action and purity, then the self will be unaware of its own existence. Life and death become spontaneous, neither acceptable nor unacceptable—free, unconstrained, and unbound. Thus it is said: "Only by relinquishing affairs and distancing oneself from others does the self remain within the self—whatever is fitting, let it be so."

There is no fixed rule for when to be elaborate or simple, when to be restrained or patient, when to be diligent or at ease.

There is no definitive method for establishing expedient means. When all practices are cultivated equally, without abandoning a single teaching yet sweeping away all excess to return to the root and practice simplicity, this is not permissible. Thus it is said: "There is no fixed rule for when to be elaborate or simple."

There is no fixed rule for when to combine vigilance with forbearance. To practice forbearance without vigilance is not permissible. Thus it is said: "When to be restrained or patient."

There is no fixed rule for when to be diligent in good conduct. To become lazy and cease diligence is not permissible. Thus it is said: "When to be diligent or at ease."

This chapter clarifies that by guarding against the subtle and blocking the gradual, and by not neglecting even the smallest conduct, one may become a worthy or a sage.

**End of Chapter One**

Master Guan Yin said:
The height of wisdom is to know that wisdom is ultimately insufficient to encompass all things, hence one becomes foolish;
The height of eloquence is to know that eloquence is ultimately insufficient to illuminate all things, hence one becomes reticent;
The height of courage is to know that courage is ultimately insufficient to overcome all things, hence one becomes timid.

Those of utmost wisdom can illuminate principles and penetrate the Dao, yet wisdom is truly insufficient to benefit all things. Knowing this, it is better to embrace the Dao through foolish obscurity. Thus it is said: "The height of wisdom is to know that wisdom is ultimately insufficient to encompass all things, hence one becomes foolish."

Those of utmost eloquence can clarify matters and principles, yet eloquence is truly insufficient to enlighten all beings in the world. It is better to nurture qi and spirit through reticence. Thus it is said: "The height of eloquence is to know that eloquence is ultimately insufficient to illuminate all things, hence one becomes reticent."

Those of utmost courage and strength may overcome ten or a hundred men, yet courage is truly insufficient to overcome all beings in the world. It is better to cultivate virtue through timidity, humility, and gentleness. Thus it is said: "The height of courage is to know that courage is ultimately insufficient to overcome all things, hence one becomes timid."

This chapter elucidates the flaws of wisdom, eloquence, and courage.

**End of Chapter Two**

Master Guan Yin said:
Among Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things, there is not a single thing that is mine. Things are not me, yet they cannot but respond; I am not me, yet I cannot but nurture myself.

**End of the Ninth Scroll of the Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing**

Qi circulates yin and yang, giving rise to Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things. Yet the source of qi was originally without thing. Since it was originally without thing, it is true emptiness. When things come to stir, it cannot but respond, yet in responding to things it remains ever quiescent. Thus it is said: "Among Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things, there is not a single thing that is mine." Things are not mine, yet they cannot but elicit response.

That which is I is the original. Qi circulates yin and yang, giving rise to the four great elements in temporary combination as this self. Yet the source of qi was originally without the four great elements in temporary combination as this self. Since it dwells in temporary combination, it cannot but rely on clothing and food to nurture it. Thus it is said: "I am not I, yet I cannot but nurture myself." That which is not I is the original.

Though it responds to things, there has never been any thing. Though it nurtures the self, there has never been any self. True emptiness and wondrous being—though it responds to things, it is like an echo in an empty valley: responding to change, it remains ever quiescent, and the valley has never held any sound. The response of true emptiness to things is likewise. Thus it is said: "Though it responds to things, there has never been any thing."

When one awakens to true emptiness, the four great elements are like a rented dwelling. Though one relies on clothing and food for nurture, within the twelve hours one abides solely in true emptiness, and true emptiness is without self. Thus it is said: "Though it nurtures the self, there has never been any self."

Do not say that one first externalizes things and then externalizes the self. Do not say that one first externalizes form and then externalizes the mind. The Dao is one and the same; it cannot be approached in stages.

To externalize is to forget. Since the body composed of the four great elements in temporary combination is like a rented dwelling, how is it different from things? Both are illusory and unreal. Once one understands that both are illusory, one forgets them all at once. One must not say that one first forgets the myriad things and then forgets the self. Thus it is said: "Do not say that one first externalizes things and then externalizes the self."

Form is the body of the four great elements. The mind is the mind of the five aggregates in false accumulation. Not only is the body composed of the four great elements no different from things, but the mind of the five aggregates in false accumulation is also no different from things. Both self and things, form and mind, are equally illusory and unreal. Once one awakens to their illusory nature, one forgets them all at once. One must not say that one first forgets the body and then forgets the mind of the five aggregates in false accumulation. Thus it is said: "Do not say that one first externalizes form and then externalizes the mind."

Since the Dao is not two, there is no division into inner and outer, things and self. How could it admit of sequential progression? Thus it is said: "The Dao is one and the same; it cannot be approached in stages."

The mind is the inner; form is the outer. This chapter elucidates the flaws of delusion and attachment to things, self, mind, and form.

**End of Chapter Three**

Master Guan Yin said:
One who scrutinizes the tip of a hair cannot see the vastness of Heaven and Earth. One who examines a faint sound cannot hear the crash of thunder.

To scrutinize is to examine closely. The tip of a hair refers to the fine point of an autumn hare’s fur. One who closely examines the tip of a hare’s autumn fur becomes attached to the subtle appearance of form and thus fails to perceive the vast appearance of Heaven and Earth. Thus it is said: "One who scrutinizes the tip of a hair cannot see the vastness of Heaven and Earth."

A faint sound refers to the sound of a gnat. The greatest sound of thunder is the crash of a thunderclap. One who closely examines the faint sound of a gnat thus fails to hear the great sound of thunder’s crashing roar. Thus it is said: "One who examines a faint sound cannot hear the crash of thunder."

One who sees the great cannot see the small. One who sees the near cannot see the far. One who hears the great cannot hear the small. One who hears the near cannot hear the far.

Not only does one who closely examines the tip of a hair fail to see the vastness of Heaven and Earth, but one who closely examines the vastness of Heaven and Earth also fails to see the tip of a hair. Thus it is said: "One who sees the great cannot see the small."

The near refers to what is close at hand. Not only does one who sees the great fail to see the small, but one who sees what is shallow and near also fails to see what is profound and distant—the true aspect of what lies before one. Thus it is said: "One who sees the near cannot see the far."

Not only does one who closely examines the sound of a gnat fail to hear the sound of thunder, but one who closely examines the sound of thunder also fails to hear the sound of a gnat. Thus it is said: "One who hears the great cannot hear the small."

Not only does one who hears the great fail to hear the small, but one who hears shallow, near sounds of indulgence also fails to hear the mighty sound beyond the kalpas. Thus it is said: "One who hears the near cannot hear the far."

The sage has nothing he sees, and thus can see all things; he has nothing he hears, and thus can hear all things.

What is seen is form; what is heard is sound. Thus, whether it be the tip of a hair or Heaven and Earth, though their forms differ in size, their nature as form is not different. Whether it be the sound of a gnat or thunder, though their sounds differ in magnitude, their nature as sound is not different. The sage has nothing he sees and nothing he hears because he does not cling to form or sound. One who does not cling to form or sound abides in true emptiness and luminous clarity, perceiving and hearing all without obstruction. Thus it is said: "The sage has nothing he sees, and thus can see all things; he has nothing he hears, and thus can hear all things."

This chapter elucidates the flaws of delusion and attachment to form and sound.

**End of Chapter Four**

Master Guan Yin said:
The eye sees countless things, yet some love gold and some love jade—this is to fixate on one form for the eye. The ear hears countless things, yet some love bells and some love drums—this is to fixate on one sound for the ear.

The eye beholds countless forms, yet roughly speaking, some love the yellow of gold, and some love the white of jade. If one loves the yellow of gold, one despises the white of jade; if one loves the white of jade, one despises the yellow of gold. Thus one must love and fixate on one form, resulting in the eye’s partial vision. Thus it is said: "The eye sees countless things, yet some love gold and some love jade—this is to fixate on one form for the eye."

The ear hears countless sounds, yet roughly speaking, some love the clear tones of bells, and some love the deep tones of drums. If one loves the clear tones of bells, one despises the deep tones of drums; if one loves the deep tones of drums, one despises the clear tones of bells. Thus one must love and fixate on one sound, resulting in the ear’s partial hearing. Thus it is said: "The ear hears countless things, yet some love bells and some love drums—this is to fixate on one sound for the ear."

Only the sage does not crave them, does not reject them, does not dwell in them. The sage who has realized the Dao sees through true emptiness, neither craving nor despising forms and sounds, nor needing to sweep them away. Why is this so? Because forms and sounds cannot abide in true emptiness. Thus it is said: Only the sage does not crave them, does not reject them, does not dwell in them. *Dwell* means to abide. This chapter cures the illness of delusion and attachment to love and aversion toward forms and sounds.

**End of Chapter Five**

Master Guan Yin said:
One who is good at the present can practice the ancient; one who is good at the branch can establish the root. *The present* refers to current human affairs; *the ancient* refers to the principles prior to the Five Ultimates. *The branch* refers to the Five Constants and the hundred practices; *the root* refers to the great root and great origin of the Dao. If one is skillful in applying current human affairs and the Five Constants and hundred practices with marvelous efficacy, one can respond to change without attachment, darkly accord with the principles prior to the Five Ultimates, and stand alone in the great root and great origin of the Dao. Thus it is said: One who is good at the present can practice the ancient; one who is good at the branch can establish the root. *Practice* means to be without attachment. This chapter cures the illness of delusion and attachment to the root and branch of affairs and principles, failing to awaken to the substance and function.

**End of Chapter Six**

Master Guan Yin said:
Cunning surpasses thieves and thus can capture thieves; courage surpasses tigers and thus can capture tigers. When the human heart’s cunning surpasses that of bandits, it can capture bandits. Thus it is said: Cunning surpasses thieves and thus can capture thieves. When human strength and courage surpass that of tigers and leopards, they can capture tigers and leopards. Thus it is said: Courage surpasses tigers and thus can capture tigers. This is a metaphor.

Only by overcoming the self can one perfect the self; only by overcoming things can one benefit things; only by forgetting the Dao can one possess the Dao. *Overcome* means to surpass; *the self* means selfishness. Only by skillfully overcoming one’s selfish desires can one perfect one’s supreme virtue. Thus it is said: Only by overcoming the self can one perfect the self. Only by skillfully overcoming deluded attachment to things and desires can one universally benefit beings and accomplish the marvelous practices of the ten thousand things. Thus it is said: Only by overcoming things can one benefit things. Only by skillfully forgetting the wisdom of realizing the Dao can one darkly accord with true emptiness and not lose the wondrous existence of the Dao of true emptiness. Thus it is said: Only by forgetting the Dao can one possess the Dao. *Possess* means not to lose wondrous existence. This chapter cures the illness of failing to forget the wisdom of realizing the Dao.

**End of Chapter Seven**

Master Guan Yin said:
If armor is hard, things will surely destroy it—what is rigid will break. If a blade is sharp, things will surely shatter it—what is keen will be blunted. *Armor* refers to chainmail. Though chainmail is hard, there are hard bows and strong crossbows whose arrows can pierce and destroy it. From this, we know that whatever is too rigid will be broken by something else. Thus it is said: If armor is hard, things will surely destroy it—what is rigid will break. If a blade’s edge is sharp, there are steel and stone that can damage its edge. From this, we know that whatever is too keen will be blunted by something else. Thus it is said: If a blade is sharp, things will surely shatter it—what is keen will be blunted. This is a metaphor.

The phoenix is revered for being rarely seen—thus the sage takes profundity as the root. The musk deer flees, leaving its fragrance uncaptured—thus the sage takes restraint as the guiding principle.

The two metaphors above—like the majestic phoenix, whose rarity and elusiveness make it wondrous—were it to appear frequently in the world, people would no longer revere it as rare and wondrous. Thus it is said: the phoenix is revered for being rarely seen. Following these metaphors, we understand that the sage conceals his radiance, aligning with the profound and obscure Dao, which is the root-source of the Five Ultimates, the Two Modes, the Five Constants, and the hundred practices. Thus it is said: the sage takes profundity as the root.

The musk deer, when pursued relentlessly, rips open its own musk sac and abandons it; seeing the deer without musk, people no longer hunt it. Thus it is said: the musk deer flees, leaving its fragrance uncaptured. From these successive metaphors, we understand that the sage restrains his sagely wisdom and spiritual powers, merging with true emptiness. Only when things come to move him does he respond of necessity, ever responding yet ever quiescent, never departing from the supreme Dao—like a net with its guiding cord, when drawn, all its meshes open in unison. Thus it is said: the sage takes restraint as the guiding principle. This is the synthesis of the metaphors.

This chapter elucidates the malady of being unable to subdue the strong or humble persuasions, as well as the affliction of failing to conceal sagely wisdom and spiritual powers.

**Chapter Eight**
Master Guan Yin said: A vessel has two openings. When filled with water and inverted, the water pours out; if one is sealed, the water does not flow down. For if it does not ascend, it does not descend. Though a well be a thousand *ren* deep, when drawn, the water rises—if it does not descend, it does not ascend.

The "vessel" is full. The vessel has openings at both ends. First, seal the lower opening and pour water into the vessel until it is full. Then, swiftly seal the upper opening, remove the lower seal, and invert it—the water does not flow out. Why? Because one opening is forcefully sealed, and the *qi* does not circulate. If the *qi* does not ascend and flow, the water does not descend. Thus it is said: a vessel has two openings; when filled with water and inverted, if one is sealed, the water does not flow down—for if it does not ascend, it does not descend.

A *ren* is seven *chi*. Though a well be seven thousand *chi* deep, when a bucket is lowered by rope and drawn, the water rises with it. Thus it is said: though a well be a thousand *ren* deep, when drawn, the water ascends. If the bucket is not lowered by rope, the water has no means to rise. Thus it is said: if it does not descend, it does not ascend. This is the metaphor.

Therefore, the sage does not precede things. From the foregoing metaphors—if it does not ascend, it does not descend; if it does not descend, it does not ascend—we understand that the sage does not precede things, and thus things do not precede the sage. "Does not precede" means the sage does not first exalt himself or act arrogantly toward others, and so others dare not act arrogantly toward the sage. Thus it is said: the sage does not precede things. "Things" refers to all beings. This chapter elucidates the malady of being unable to abide in the rear.

**Chapter Nine**
Master Guan Yin said: When people commit errors, though they suffer harm only after the error is made, in time, they secretly deliberate over what preceded the error. The deluded of the world, when employing their actions for unwholesome ends, err in their virtuous conduct. Though the harm of misfortune befalls them only after the loss of virtuous conduct, in time, they reflect and repent, privately pondering the good and ill, fortune and misfortune of their past actions before the error was made, and thereafter cautiously apply this understanding to their virtuous conduct.

Thus it is said: when people commit errors, though they suffer harm only after the error is made, in time, they secretly deliberate over what preceded the error. "Secretly" means privately. Only by not relying on one’s own intelligence but embracing the intelligence of others, naturally dissolving the self and embracing the selves of all under heaven, and practicing this throughout life, can one avoid error.

Only the enlightened person, having awakened to the wondrous Dao, does not rely on the intelligence of awakening to the wondrous Dao. Concealing its wondrous function, they incorporate the intelligence and use of others, adapting to what benefits all, and thus universally applying it. Naturally, there is no self. Thus it is said: only by not relying on one’s own intelligence but embracing the intelligence of others does the self naturally dissolve. In true emptiness, there is no self; responding to things, forms appear—like the moon universally reflected in all waters. The brightness of the moon in all waters is governed by the single moon in the sky. The intelligence of all under heaven is governed by the majestic light of the true emptiness of the Dao. With the majestic light of the true emptiness of the Dao, responding to change while abiding in constant quiescence, one remains unattached to any middle or extreme throughout life, enabling wondrous function and universal benefit without losing the Dao and virtue. Thus it is said: embracing the selves of all under heaven and practicing this throughout life, one can avoid error. "Practicing" means non-abiding; "applying" means using. This chapter elucidates the malady of being unable to dissolve the self and universally benefit others.

**Chapter Ten**
Master Guan Yin said: The customs of past and present differ; the customs of east, west, south, and north also differ. Even the virtues of a single household or individual vary—how could I cling to one pattern to constrain later generations?

From ancient times to the present, the customs of simplicity and decadence have varied. Thus it is said: the customs of past and present differ. The customs of the Eastern Yi, Western Rong, Southern Man, and Northern Di also vary. Thus it is said: the customs of east, west, south, and north also differ. Every household and every individual has its own preferences and virtues. Thus it is said: even the virtues of a single household or individual vary. The hearts and inclinations of people under heaven change with the times and differ in their directions—how could I cling to one fixed method and preemptively set a pattern for later generations? Thus it is said: how could I cling to one pattern to constrain later generations?

Only by adapting to the times and harmonizing with customs, anticipating the subtle and following events, relinquishing anger and restraining desires, simplifying things and forgiving others, weighing their relative importance and acting accordingly, can one naturally align with the unfathomable spirit and accord with the boundless Dao.

The greatly enlightened person alone can adapt to the times and change without self, harmonizing with the world without contrivance. First comprehending the living mechanism of true emptiness and wondrous existence, they respond only when things move them, ever responding yet ever quiescent. Thus it is said: only by adapting to the times and harmonizing with customs, anticipating the subtle and following events.

As stated above, having comprehended the living mechanism of true emptiness and wondrous existence, when things come to them, their heart harbors no anger; when assailed by the demon of beauty, their heart knows no attachment. Thus it is said: relinquishing anger and restraining desires. A heart without anger is to relinquish anger; a heart without attachment is to restrain desire. Thus it is said: restraining anger and blocking desire originate from this.

The greatly enlightened person does not indulge in external things but uses only what is necessary for sustenance, employing them only when unavoidable and not taking what is not righteous. Thus it is said: simplifying things. The greatly enlightened person, empty and preserving the true, pure yet accommodating all—like the vast sea, lowly in position, receiving both the clear and the turbid. Thus it is said: forgiving others.

Having embraced the capacity to accommodate all things and forgive others, their response to phenomena is like a mirror hung aloft—reflecting all forms without omitting beauty or ugliness. Their handling of affairs is like a balance scale—weighing all things without erring in lightness or heaviness. They act through non-action, yet nothing is left undone. Thus it is said: weighing the light and the heavy to act accordingly.

Having attained non-action wherein nothing is left undone, they employ the spirit that is not spirit, so that yin and yang cannot fathom them. Their essence is the unfathomable Dao, responding to change without fixed method. Thus it is said: naturally aligning with the spirit’s unfathomability, harmonizing with the Dao without fixed form.

This chapter elucidates the cure for the illness of failing to respond with living mechanism and adapt to the times.

---

**Chapter Eleven**
Master Guan Yin said: There are those who form bonds through the Dao, those who form bonds through virtue, and those who form bonds through affairs.

The greatly enlightened, having awakened to the wondrous Dao, seek those of like realization as inseparable companions. Thus it is said: those who form bonds through the Dao. Noble persons who practice myriad virtues together become friends beyond the constraints of age. Thus it is said: those who form bonds through virtue. Those who collaborate in public or private affairs may find mutual affinity and thus form bonds. Thus it is said: those who form bonds through affairs.

Bonds through the Dao are like father and son, transcending right and wrong, worthy and unworthy—thus they endure. Bonds through virtue may involve right and wrong, worthy and unworthy—thus they may unite or part. Bonds through affairs part when the affair ends.

Those who have together awakened to the Dao form inseparable bonds, like the intimacy of father and son, forgetting right and wrong, worthy and unworthy, and roaming beyond the realm of phenomena. Like water, their friendship never severs. Thus it is said: bonds through the Dao are like father and son, transcending right and wrong, worthy and unworthy—thus they endure. Noble persons who prioritize virtue and conduct may form bonds, yet cannot be without intention, thus distinguishing right from wrong, worthy from unworthy, and experiencing union or separation. Thus it is said: bonds through virtue may involve right and wrong, worthy and unworthy—thus they may unite or part. Those who do not hold the Dao and virtue in their hearts, collaborating in public or private affairs and forming bonds through worldly etiquette, may unite in affairs yet remain distant in sentiment. At the slightest discord, their bonds sever. Thus it is said: bonds through affairs part when the affair ends.

As *Zhuangzi* said: "The gentleman’s bonds are as bland as water; the petty person’s bonds are as sweet as wine. The gentleman’s blandness fosters closeness; the petty person’s sweetness leads to rupture."

This chapter elucidates the cure for the illness of failing to form bonds through the Dao.

Master Guan Yin said: Do not mistake clumsiness and coarseness for the substance of the Dao—one should delight in nimbleness and quickness. Do not mistake ignorance and obscurity for the obscurity of the Dao—one should delight in lightness and clarity. Do not mistake arrogance and disdain for the loftiness of the Dao—one should delight in harmony and unity. Do not mistake boundless dissipation for the vastness of the Dao—one should delight in essential urgency. Do not mistake secluded sorrow for the stillness of the Dao—one should delight in joy and ease.

The words of the ancients on learning are often flawed and must be remedied. One must not claim dullness, clumsiness, or coarseness as the plainness of the Dao—rather, one should delight in keen, swift, and penetrating awakening. Thus it is said: Do not mistake clumsiness and coarseness for the substance of the Dao—one should delight in nimbleness and quickness. One must not claim foolishness, ignorance, or darkness as the concealment of learning the Dao—rather, one should delight in lightness and clarity. Thus it is said: Do not mistake ignorance and obscurity for the obscurity of the Dao—one should delight in lightness and clarity. One must not claim arrogance and disdain toward others as the loftiness of virtue and power—rather, one should delight in humility, harmonious radiance, and untainted unity with the world. Thus it is said: Do not mistake arrogance and disdain for the loftiness of the Dao—one should delight in harmony and unity. One must not claim boundless dissipation and empty chaos as the vastness of the Dao—rather, one should delight in awakening to true emptiness as the essential and wondrous, and in the marvelous existence of responsive urgency. Thus it is said: Do not mistake boundless dissipation for the vastness of the Dao—one should delight in essential urgency. One must not claim dwelling in seclusion with a sorrowful heart as the stillness of learning the Dao—rather, one should delight in carefree joy and untainted purity. Thus it is said: Do not mistake secluded sorrow for the stillness of the Dao—one should delight in joy and ease.

These five—clumsiness, ignorance, arrogance, dissipation, and sorrow—are but the words of ancients who, in their enlightened clarity, feigned madness to blend with the world. Later learners, failing to awaken, took them as literal truth and followed them, turning them into the maladies of learning the Dao. These must be cured with the medicine of corrective words. Thus it is said: The words of the ancients on learning are often flawed and must be remedied. This chapter elucidates the cure for the illness of failing to awaken to the ancients’ words.

---

Master Guan Yin said: One must not condemn the world while affirming oneself, nor belittle others while exalting oneself. One must not slight the Dao while presuming one’s own attainment, nor slander virtue while claiming one’s own possession of it. One must not disparage others’ talents while boasting of one’s own.

Those who study the Dao must not fixate on others’ faults, opening the eye of illness and deeming the whole world wrong while relying on their own abilities to fuel self-righteousness. Thus it is said: One must not condemn the world while affirming oneself. Those who study the Dao must not regard others as base and lowly while elevating themselves. Thus it is said: One must not belittle others while exalting oneself. Those who study the Dao must not treat others with disdain and arrogance, assuming they have not awakened to the wondrous Dao while presuming their own attainment. Thus it is said: One must not slight the Dao while presuming one’s own attainment. Those who study the Dao must not incessantly slander others as devoid of virtue while claiming virtue for themselves. Thus it is said: One must not slander virtue while claiming one’s own possession of it. Those who study the Dao must not regard others as vulgar, foolish, and untalented while boasting of their own talents and learning. Thus it is said: One must not disparage others’ talents while boasting of one’s own. This chapter elucidates the cure for the illness of failing to forget the self, others, abilities, and possessions.

---

Master Guan Yin said: To confound the world’s wisest is not through wisdom but through foolishness. To exhaust the world’s most eloquent is not through eloquence but through inarticulateness. To subdue the world’s bravest is not through bravery but through timidity.

True emptiness and profound obscurity cannot be known or recognized through wisdom. Thus, those who confound the world’s wisest are not the wise but those who, like the enlightened, remain silent and concealed as if foolish—even the world’s wise cannot fathom them. Thus it is said: To confound the world’s wisest is not through wisdom but through foolishness. The Dao is originally nameless and cannot be grasped through speech or debate. The world’s most eloquent, when faced with this, are exhausted and left without words. Those who have awakened to the Dao find it difficult to explain to others; it is better to remain inarticulate and speechless. Thus it is said: To exhaust the world’s most eloquent is not through eloquence but through inarticulateness. Those who have awakened to the wondrous Dao preserve their inner being through non-action and stillness, and protect their outer being through humility, softness, and non-contention. None in the world who love to contend can contend with those who do not contend—thus, they become invincible. Through the virtue of non-contention, one can subdue the world’s bravest, not through contention. Thus it is said: To subdue the world’s bravest is not through bravery but through timidity. Timidity here means softness and weakness. This chapter elucidates the cure for the illness of failing to forget wisdom, eloquence, and bravery.

---

Master Guan Yin said: Heaven cannot make lotuses bloom in winter or chrysanthemums in spring—thus the sage does not defy the seasons. Nor can earth make oranges grow in Luoyang or foxes and raccoon-dogs thrive in the south—thus the sage does not defy customs.

Lotus flowers bloom in summer, chrysanthemums in autumn. Heaven cannot make lotuses bloom in winter or chrysanthemums in spring. Thus, the sage does not go against the four seasons of heaven or defy the openings and closings of time. Thus it is said: Heaven cannot make lotuses bloom in winter or chrysanthemums in spring—thus the sage does not defy the seasons.

Luoyang refers to the region of Song and Luo. Oranges are citrus fruits that thrive in the warmth of the south. When transplanted to the banks of the Luo River in Songshan, they transform into bitter oranges. The waters of Mount Wen form the Wen River, located in the Wu-Yue region. Foxes and raccoon-dogs live in the highlands and wilds north of the Yangtze, but perish when brought to the damp lowlands of the south. These differences arise from the earth’s qi. Since the earth cannot make oranges grow in Luoyang or raccoon-dogs thrive in the south, the sage does not defy the customs of the Yi, Di, Man, and Rong peoples or those of the Central States. Thus it is said: Nor can earth make oranges grow in Luoyang or foxes and raccoon-dogs thrive in the south—thus the sage does not defy customs.

The sage cannot make hands walk or feet grasp, thus the sage does not defy the strengths of the self. The sage cannot make fish fly or birds gallop, thus the sage does not defy the strengths of others. Digging is the strength of the hand, walking the strength of the foot—how could even a sage make hands walk or feet grasp, exchanging their functions? Thus the sage does not defy the six faculties’ inherent strengths. Hence it is said: The sage cannot make hands walk or feet grasp, thus the sage does not defy the strengths of the self. Strength lies in what one can do, weakness in what one cannot. Fish excel at swimming in water, birds at soaring in the sky, and galloping is the swiftness of horses—how could even a sage alter the swimming of fish, the flight of birds, or the gallop of horses? Thus the sage does not defy the strengths of others. Hence it is said: The sage cannot make fish fly or birds gallop, thus the sage does not defy the strengths of others.

Such is the Way: it may move or halt, conceal or reveal, yet remains unbound. This refers to the four principles mentioned above—where the sage does not defy the times, does not defy customs, does not defy the self’s strengths, and does not defy others’ strengths. Adhering to these four, one may act when the time is right without defying the time’s movement, rest when the time is right without defying the time’s stillness, conceal when the time is right without defying the time’s obscurity, and reveal when the time is right without defying the time’s clarity. Only by not resisting the transformations of creation and not clinging to rigid methods can one respond endlessly to change and embody the subtle Dao. Hence it is said: Such is the Way—it may move or halt, conceal or reveal, yet remains unbound.

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to adapt to the times.

---

**Chapter Sixteen**
Master Guan Yin said: Those who speak little are not resented by others; those who act little are not criticized by others; those who use little wisdom are not wearied by others; those who display little ability are not employed by others.

Those who study the Dao guard their words and moderate their speech to avoid faults, thus escaping the resentment of the multitude. Hence it is said: Those who speak little are not resented by others. In the arena of profit and fame, those who restrain their conduct and limit their actions avoid the envy and obstruction of others. Hence it is said: Those who act little are not criticized by others. Those who conceal their wisdom and use it sparingly avoid the toil imposed by others. Hence it is said: Those who use little wisdom are not wearied by others. Those who appear clumsy yet temper their skills avoid being enslaved by others. Hence it is said: Those who display little ability are not employed by others.

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to forget words, actions, wisdom, and ability.

---

**Chapter Seventeen**
Master Guan Yin said: Hold fast to sincerity, act with simplicity, respond with forbearance, and answer in silence—thus my Dao knows no end.

To "hold fast" is to maintain; "sincerity" is truthfulness; "simplicity" is absence of excess; "to act" is to proceed without attachment; "to respond" is to engage; "forbearance" is magnanimity and tolerance. Realizing the truth of emptiness and existence, one preserves and upholds without clinging to any extreme—thus one’s actions are simple and uncluttered. Hence it is said: Hold fast to sincerity, act with simplicity. When dwelling among others or leading the multitude, one responds with magnanimity and tolerance, overlooking others’ faults to preserve one’s own virtue. Hence it is said: Respond with forbearance. If someone comes seeking answers, one subtly gestures without resorting to verbal instruction, allowing the keen-minded to awaken instantly—thus the Dao one has realized knows no bounds. Hence it is said: Answer in silence—thus my Dao knows no end.

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to embody sincerity, simplicity, forbearance, and silence.

---

**Chapter Eighteen**
Master Guan Yin said: Plan according to affairs, decide according to principle, act through people, and succeed through Heaven. Model affairs on the present, model principles on the past; affairs align with others, but the Dao is one’s own.

When scheming for worldly matters, one judges by principle to ensure appropriateness. Hence it is said: Plan according to affairs, decide according to principle. The effort or lack thereof lies with human action, but success or failure rests with Heaven’s decree. Hence it is said: Act through people, succeed through Heaven. Model affairs on those of the present, and principles on those of the past. Hence it is said: Model affairs on the present, model principles on the past. Affairs are means—though one’s use of them may align with others, the realization of the subtle Dao, the constant stillness in response, and the untainted purity set one apart. Hence it is said: Affairs align with others, but the Dao is one’s own.

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to harmonize affairs and principles.

---

**Chapter Nineteen**
Master Guan Yin said: Gold and jade are hard to discard, while earth and stone are easy to abandon. Those who study the Dao, when encountering subtle words or wondrous deeds, must not cling to them—for they are to be practiced, not grasped. To grasp them is a malady of the heart, incurable by any medicine.

Gold and jade symbolize the words and deeds of sages, while earth and stone symbolize the words and deeds of ordinary people. Though gold and jade are precious, they become dust in the eye—the great Dao leaves no trace, and thus words and deeds cannot be equaled. Students of the Dao find it difficult to forget the words and deeds of sages, just as people find it hard to discard gold and jade. Hence it is said: Gold and jade are hard to discard. Students of the Dao easily forget the words and deeds of ordinary people, just as people readily abandon earth and stone. Hence it is said: Earth and stone are easy to abandon. When students of the Dao encounter the subtle words or wondrous deeds of sages, they must not cling to them. Hence it is said: Those who study the Dao, when encountering subtle words or wondrous deeds, must not cling to them.

The wondrous deeds of sages may be practiced to cultivate virtue, yet one must not cling to the traces of those wondrous deeds. Hence it is said: They may be practiced but not clung to. Those who cling to them are like one whose skin bears disease—easily cured—but if the illness lies in the heart and belly, even the physicians Lu and Bian Que cannot heal it. Those who cling to the traces of sages’ wondrous deeds are likewise afflicted. Hence it is said: Those who cling to them suffer a malady of the heart and belly for which no medicine exists. This chapter cures the ailment of being unable to forget words and deeds.

**Chapter Twelve**

Master Guan Yin said: Those who fail to recognize urgent matters and instead pursue many affairs, other affairs, or strange affairs will meet with poverty, distress, and misfortune. Urgent matters are the Dao and virtue; many affairs are the ten thousand dharmas; other affairs are peripheral arts and skills; strange affairs are the heterodoxies of other worlds. If students of the Dao do not understand that the Dao and virtue are the simplest and most pressing matters, but instead become mired in the ten thousand dharmas, or study peripheral minor arts, or learn the crafts of medicine, divination, and labor, or pursue the strange heterodoxies of other worlds—though their studies differ, their loss of the Dao is one and the same. Losing the Dao, they fall into the wheel of rebirth, drifting through kalpas of dust without hope of escape. No poverty, distress, or misfortune is greater than this. Hence it is said: Those who fail to recognize urgent matters and instead pursue many affairs, other affairs, or strange affairs will meet with poverty, distress, and misfortune. "Pursue" means to become mired; "meet with" means to fall into the wheel of rebirth.

Moreover, the Dao is nowhere absent—one cannot abandon this to seek that. "This" refers to the urgent matters of the body, mind, Dao, and virtue; "that" refers to many affairs, other affairs, and strange affairs. Students of the Dao must realize that the Dao is nowhere absent, that this very body and mind are the Dao. Since the Dao is nowhere absent, how could it not reside in the body and mind? The body and mind arise from the Dao as waves arise from water—thus the waves are water, and likewise this body and mind are the Dao. Once one awakens to the truth that this body and mind are the Dao, one must not abandon them to seek the Dao in many affairs, other affairs, or strange affairs. Hence it is said: Moreover, the Dao is nowhere absent—one cannot abandon this to seek that. This chapter cures the ailment of failing to make the Dao one’s primary pursuit.

**Chapter Twenty-One**

Master Guan Yin said: Among the principles of the world, to abandon the intimate for the distant, the root for the branches, the worthy for the foolish, the near for the far—these may be tolerated briefly, but over time they bring harm. The intimate is one’s nature and life; the distant is wealth and sensuality. The root is the Dao; the branches are the five constants, the hundred practices, and the ten thousand dharmas. The worthy are the virtues of wisdom; the foolish are delusion and confusion. The near is compassion and goodness; the far is the multitude of evils.

The principles of the world lead students of the Dao astray, causing them to abandon their nature, life, Dao, virtue, compassion, and goodness, and instead become mired in wealth, sensuality, the ten thousand dharmas, delusion, and the multitude of evils. This may bring fleeting pleasure to deluded emotions, but prolonged attachment without awakening leads to the wheel of rebirth, birth and death, wombs, eggs, moisture, and transformation, without end. No harm is greater than this. Hence it is said: Among the principles of the world, to abandon the intimate for the distant, the root for the branches, the worthy for the foolish, the near for the far—these may be tolerated briefly, but over time they bring harm. This chapter cures the ailment of failing to awaken to the Dao.

**Chapter Twenty-Two**

Master Guan Yin said: Those who discoursed on the Dao in ancient times described it as concentrated stillness, profound depth, limpid clarity, vast emptiness, or obscure darkness—do not encounter such descriptions and grow fearful or retreat. "Concentrated" means settled; "stillness" means quiescent; "profound" means secluded. The ancients who discoursed on the Dao sometimes spoke of it as perfect stability and tranquility, sometimes as deep and unfathomable, sometimes as clear and abyssal like water, sometimes as vast and boundless like empty space, or sometimes as hidden, obscure, and inscrutable. If students encounter these five descriptions—concentrated stillness, profound depth, limpid clarity, vast emptiness, or obscure darkness—they must not grow fearful, thinking the Dao too difficult to study, and thus become negligent. Hence it is said: Those who discoursed on the Dao in ancient times described it as concentrated stillness, profound depth, limpid clarity, vast emptiness, or obscure darkness—do not encounter such descriptions and grow fearful or retreat.

The supreme principle of the world ultimately transcends words and concepts. If one truly knows that it is neither words nor concepts but lies beyond those subtle words and wondrous meanings, one will accord with my teaching. The supreme principle of the world is the Dao; "ultimately" means in the end. The five descriptions previously mentioned—concentrated stillness, profound depth, limpid clarity, vast emptiness, and obscure darkness—all belong to the realm of nominal appearances and deluded emotions. The Dao itself is nameless and without emotion; when thoroughly investigated to its ultimate end, it ultimately transcends words and concepts. Hence it is said: The supreme principle of the world ultimately transcends words and concepts. If one truly knows that it cannot be reached by words or concepts, and that it lies before the subtle words and wondrous meanings of the ancients, one will suddenly awaken, thoroughly perceive the originally complete and self-sufficient Dao, which is inherently pure and requires no cultivation or external seeking. Such awakening accords with the meaning of my teaching. Hence it is said: If one truly knows that it is neither words nor concepts but lies beyond those subtle words and wondrous meanings, one will accord with my teaching. "Beyond" means before; "truly" means sincerely. This chapter cures the ailment of failing to understand the roots and dusts of perception.

**Chapter Twenty-Three**

Master Guan Yin said: The great words of sages are like gold and jade; their small words are like platycodon and plantain. When used appropriately, platycodon and plantain give life; when used inappropriately, gold and jade bring death.

**Chapter Twenty-Four**

Master Guan Yin said: The great words of sages, such as the *Dao De Jing* and the *Yin Fu Jing*, are as precious as gold and jade—hence it is said, "The great words of sages are gold and jade." Platycodon is the name of an herb that cures wind-dampness. Plantain, also called *cheqian* grass, treats red-and-white dysentery. Small words refer to later sages’ poems, lyrics, direct sayings, and recorded dialogues. If one awakens to the Dao through poems, lyrics, direct sayings, or recorded dialogues, it is like using platycodon and plantain to cure wind-dampness and dysentery, restoring health and life—hence it is said, "Small words are platycodon and plantain; when used appropriately, platycodon and plantain give life." "Used appropriately" means curing illness; "give life" means restoring vitality. Yet if one recites the *Dao De Jing* and the *Yin Fu Jing* as if flowing water but fails to awaken to the Dao, it is like taking gold and jade as medicine—unable to cure wind-dampness or dysentery, leading to death. Hence it is said, "When used inappropriately, gold and jade bring death." "Bring death" means losing life; "inappropriately" means failing to cure illness. This chapter cures the ailment of distinguishing between refined and coarse scriptural words.

**Chapter Twenty-Five**

Master Guan Yin said: When speaking of a certain matter, Person A says it is beneficial, Person B says it is harmful, Person C says it may be beneficial or harmful, and Person D says it is both beneficial and harmful—one must necessarily fall into one of these. But those who speak of the Dao do not speak. "Certain" is a placeholder term. People’s views differ when discussing matters: Person A (named *Jia*) argues that a matter benefits the world; Person B (named *Yi*) argues that another matter harms it—hence it is said, "When speaking of a certain matter, Person A says it is beneficial, Person B says it is harmful." Person C (named *Bing*) argues that a matter may benefit or harm the world—hence it is said, "Person C says it may be beneficial or harmful." Person D (named *Ding*) argues that if a matter is conducted rightly, it benefits the world, but if conducted wrongly, it harms it—hence it is said, "Person D says it is both beneficial and harmful." Though Persons A, B, C, and D differ in their judgments of benefit and harm, one of them must necessarily be correct and aligned with principle—hence it is said, "one must necessarily fall into one of these." "Fall" means to align. The Dao has no name and cannot be compared through words; it is realized only through awakening in the heart, unlike matters that can be compared in terms of benefit and harm—hence it is said, "Those who speak of the Dao do not speak." This chapter cures the ailment of failing to relinquish considerations of benefit and harm.

**Chapter Twenty-Six**

Master Guan Yin said: Matters have their place in matters, and words have their principle in words. The Dao has no place in the Dao, and words have no principle in words. One who knows that words have no principle finds every word to be the Dao; one who does not know that words have no principle, though holding the most profound words, becomes obstructed and blinded. Worldly affairs each have their proper place, and every matter discussed has its own principle—hence it is said, "Matters have their place in matters, and words have their principle in words." The Dao has no fixed place, nor does it belong to names, words, or principles. The words of sages are all wordless words, pointing to the meaning beyond words. If one awakens to the wordless words, realizing they do not belong to matters or principles, then though one speaks all day, one has never truly spoken, and every word is the Dao. "Knows" means awakens. If one does not awaken to the wordless words that transcend matters and principles, though holding the words of the most realized ones before the eye of the Dao, it is like an obstruction in the throat or dust in the eye—hence it is said, "One who does not know that words have no principle, though holding the most profound words, becomes obstructed and blinded." *Yi* should be *yi* (blindness). This chapter cures the ailment of failing to relinquish attachment to matters and principles.

**Chapter Twenty-Seven**
