Master Guan Yin said: It is easy not to trust foolish people, but difficult not to trust worthy ones. It is easy not to trust worthy people, but difficult not to trust sages. It is easy not to trust one sage, but difficult not to trust a thousand sages. "Not to trust" means not to cling or become attached. The words and deeds of foolish people are easily relinquished without attachment, but those of worthy people are harder to forget—hence it is said, "It is easy not to trust foolish people, but difficult not to trust worthy ones." It is also easy to relinquish attachment to the words and deeds of worthy people, but those of sages are harder to forget—hence it is said, "It is easy not to trust worthy people, but difficult not to trust sages." It is also easy to relinquish attachment to the words and deeds of one sage, but those of a thousand sages are even harder to forget—hence it is said, "It is easy not to trust one sage, but difficult not to trust a thousand sages." One who does not trust a thousand sages sees neither others nor self, neither the Dao above nor matters below. If a learner does not cling to the words and deeds of a thousand sages, the mind forgets names and traces, merging obscurely with the Dao, transcending the distinctions of sages, self, matters, and principles—hence it is said, "Not admiring the many sages, not valuing one’s own spirit" refers precisely to this. Thus it is said, "One who does not trust a thousand sages sees neither others nor self, neither the Dao above nor matters below." The Dao is principle. This chapter cures the ailment of failing to relinquish attachment to the ordinary and the sagely.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight**

Master Guan Yin said: The words of sages are dim and hazy, thereby making people deaf. The words of sages are dark and obscure, thereby making people blind. The words of sages are deep and heavy, thereby making people wounded. The words of sages are all wordless words, leading people to awaken to the meaning beyond words. This meaning beyond words does not belong to sound, so learners become dim and confused, their deluded feelings veiling them—unable to hear it, as if struck deaf. Hence it is said, "The words of sages are dim and hazy, thereby making people deaf."

The meaning beyond words does not pertain to form—dim and obscure, invisible when gazed upon, leaving people as if afflicted with blindness. Hence it is said, "The words of sages are dark and obscure, thereby making people blind."

The meaning beyond words does not pertain to names—profound and still, difficult to discuss in speech, leaving people as if stricken with emaciation and spasms. Hence it is said, "The words of sages are deep and heavy, thereby making people wounded."

Only the deaf do not hear sound; only the blind do not see form; only the wounded do not utter speech.

Only those afflicted with deafness cannot hear sound—thus it is said, "Only the deaf do not hear sound." Only those afflicted with blindness cannot see form—thus it is said, "Only the blind do not see form." Only those afflicted with emaciation and spasms cannot speak—thus it is said, "Only the wounded do not utter speech."

Those who do not hear sound do not hear the Dao, do not hear matters, do not hear the self. Those who do not see form do not see the Dao, do not see matters, do not see the self. Those who do not utter speech do not speak of the Dao, do not speak of matters, do not speak of the self.

The meaning beyond words, though inaudible when listened to, is not unlike the deaf who also cannot hear the sounds of the Dao, matters, or the self. Hence it is said, "Those who do not hear sound do not hear the Dao, do not hear matters, do not hear the self." The meaning beyond words, though invisible when gazed upon, is not unlike the blind who also cannot see the forms of the Dao, matters, or the self, nor do they generate such perceptions. Hence it is said, "Those who do not see form do not see the Dao, do not see matters, do not see the self." The meaning beyond words, though indescribable in speech, is not unlike those afflicted with emaciation and spasms who cannot speak—nor can they name the Dao, matters, or the self. Hence it is said, "Those who do not utter speech do not speak of the Dao, do not speak of matters, do not speak of the self."

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to relinquish attachment to form, sound, names, the Dao, matters, and the self.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine**

Master Guan Yin said: People merely recognize that within false attainment lies true loss, yet fail to see that within true attainment lies true loss. They merely recognize that within false affirmation lies true negation, yet fail to see that within true affirmation lies true negation.

Learners cling to deluded attainment, mistaking it for genuine loss in the supreme Dao, unaware that even true attainment is a genuine loss in the supreme Dao—just as gold dust, though real, becomes an obstruction when it enters the eye. Hence it is said, "People merely recognize that within false attainment lies true loss, yet fail to see that within true attainment lies true loss."

The false is illusion; the true is sincerity; "merely" denotes ignorance.

Learners discuss knowledge, clinging to deluded affirmation as if it were genuine negation, unaware that clinging to true affirmation is also genuine negation. Hence it is said, "They merely recognize that within false affirmation lies true negation, yet fail to see that within true affirmation lies true negation."

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to relinquish attachment to the real and the illusory.

**Chapter Thirty**

Master Guan Yin said: Speaking of the Dao is like speaking of dreams. Those who speak of dreams say: such gold and jade, such vessels, such birds and beasts. The speaker can describe them but cannot take and give them; the listener can hear but cannot receive and obtain them. Only the adept listener neither clings nor disputes.

Those of great awakening and thorough comprehension are like one who awakens from a dream, only then realizing that all perceived within the dream was the fabrication of deluded, inverted perceptions. Those who have not attained great awakening and thorough comprehension, though their perceptions may comprehend all phenomena, expound the wondrous Dao to others—all is but dreaming within a dream. Hence it is said, "Speaking of the Dao is like speaking of dreams."

Speaking of dreams within a dream, one says: I dreamed of such golden treasures and towering mountains, vast and immeasurable; such jade caverns, boundless and profoundly deep. These two metaphors illustrate the Dao and virtue as numinous treasures and mysterious caverns—vast, profound, immeasurable, and unfathomable. Hence it is said, "Those who speak of dreams say: such gold and jade."

Again, speaking of dreams within a dream, one says: I dreamed of such jade vessels and golden utensils, countless in their use. This metaphor illustrates the establishment of wondrous functions, universally benefiting all virtuous practices. Hence it is said, "Such vessels."

Again, speaking of dreams within a dream, one says: I dreamed of such crows and hares, tortoises and phoenixes, dragons and tigers. This metaphor illustrates the golden crow and jade hare, the black tortoise and vermilion phoenix, the white tiger and azure dragon—yin and yang in transformation. Hence it is said, "Such birds and beasts."

Speaking of dreams within a dream, one describes such gold, jade, vessels, birds, and beasts, yet the speaker can only describe them and cannot take the gold, jade, vessels, birds, or beasts to give to the listener. This metaphor illustrates that the sage cannot manifest the Dao to present to the learner. Hence it is said, "The speaker can describe them but cannot take and give them."

Those who listen to the dream within a dream only hear of such gold, jade, vessels, birds, and beasts but cannot receive or obtain them. This metaphor illustrates that those who study the Dao, through the master’s skillful guidance, awaken in an instant—not obtaining from without. Hence it is said, "The listener can hear but cannot receive and obtain them."

Only those of keen faculties, adept at hearing the profound and wondrous Dao, neither cling to words nor dispute the sage’s wordless speech. They merely, through the sage’s wordless speech, awaken to the meaning beyond words, realizing the inconceivable Dao. Hence it is said, "Only the adept listener neither clings nor disputes."

This chapter cures the ailment of failing to awaken from the affliction of discoursing on the Dao.

Lord Guan Yinzi said: Let your Dao be round, your virtue square, your conduct level, and your affairs sharp.

"You" refers to those who study the Dao. Among all who study the Dao in the world, if they can but read carefully and recite thoroughly these nine chapters, refining and delving into them, investigating and penetrating them, they will naturally awaken to the inconceivable Supreme Dao—each person possessing it in full, every individual perfected in its completeness, without need of cultivation or effort, their station equal to that of all sages. Hence it is said, "Let your Dao be round."

"Square" means upright; "virtue" is the function of the Dao. Among all who study the Dao in the world, if through the scripture they awaken to the inconceivable Supreme Dao, they must still safeguard, uphold, and preserve it. How to safeguard, uphold, and preserve it? Simply by maintaining non-action and purity, wandering carefree and at ease, neither clinging nor attaching to guard within; by humility and gentleness, not contending with things to guard without. Furthermore, rectify its wondrous function, applying it among the multitude, benefiting beings and pacifying people without seeking repayment or gratitude—all this is the upright application of it, thereby perfecting the highest virtue. Hence it is said, "Let your virtue be square."

Among all students of the Dao in the world, if through the scripture they awaken to the inconceivable Supreme Dao and safeguard, uphold, and preserve it to perfect the highest virtue, they must benefit themselves and others equally, bestowing universal kindness without distinction of noble or base, poor or rich, male or female, beauty or ugliness. With impartial benevolence and no favoritism, they perfect the wondrous conduct of the ten thousand virtues. Hence it is said, "Let your conduct be level."

"Sharp" means keen; "affairs" means function. Among all students of the Dao in the world, if through the scripture they awaken to the inconceivable Dao of True Emptiness and Wondrous Being, taking it as the essence of the true mind and the highest virtue and wondrous conduct as the function of the true mind, then in benefiting themselves and others, they will be keen in all affairs. Hence it is said, "Let your affairs be sharp."

This chapter elucidates the intent of the entire scripture as understood by Master Yin. Here ends the ninth scroll of the *Commentary on the Wenshi Zhenjing*.

#1 Based on the preceding scripture, this phrase should read: "Small insects poison the body."
#2 According to the preceding scripture, "不夫" should be "不失."
#3 "也不能" appears as "地不能" in both the *Guanyinzi* and the *Wushang Miaodao Wenshi Zhenjing*.
#4 "为医" appears as "为翳" in the *Guanyinzi*.

**Postface to the *Wenshi Zhenjing***
Composed by Ge Xianweng of the Jin

Though my form be as humble as mugwort or wormwood, I have by chance come to cherish the longevity of towering pines. Those who know the Dao, though lowly, must be embraced; though barbarian, must be esteemed.

Later, I encountered Master Zheng Siyuan. Master Zheng possessed many books of jade tablets and jasper bamboo slips. Through elixirs and herbs, he opened for me the finest medicines of the supreme Dao; through breath and absorption, he cleansed me with the purest flavors of Purple Clarity. Later, he entrusted me with the nine scrolls of the Perfected Yin’s *Wenshi Jing*. I cherished them, recited them, treasured them, and revered them.

The *Yu* is the Dao; the *Zhu* establishes heaven and earth; the *Ji* honors the Dark Man; the *Fu* is the spirit, soul, ethereal and corporeal souls; the *Jian* is the heart; the *Bi* is food; the *Fu* is transformation; the *Chou* is things; the *Yao* is compounded remedies.

With each savoring, I grew as light as treading upon a drifting leaf, roaming the undifferentiated vastness of heaven and earth; as boundless as striding a staff across the horizon, afloat upon the fathomless expanse of the great sea. Lofty as dwelling in halls of gold, jade, and precious stones; solemn as wielding the seal of ghosts, demons, and treacherous spirits. Swift as soaring phoenixes and cranes; fierce as battling tigers and rhinoceroses. Pure as bathing in azure waters; somber as dreaming of crimson hues. Seizing and releasing the great Dao, merging with the primal principle—such depths are beyond the reach of alchemists, unspoken by earlier scholars. It may be looked up to but not scaled; admired but not grasped; mirrored but not pondered; aligned with but not expressed. Is this not the discourse of those who forget things and forsake the world? The words of those who sever ties and abandon cunning? The speech of those who harmonize with yin and yang, obscuring self and other?

How exalted! How vast! And yet—how simple and effortless!

I received this teaching firsthand.

In the second year of the Xianhe era, on the first day of the fifth lunar month, Ge Hong of Danyang, styled Zhichuan, sets forth this preface.
